The author of this book, having completed for you, O sons, this book which is a complete part of the science of medicine in its entirety and having attained its peak in terms of clarity and explanation, has deemed it necessary to complete it for you with this article, which is a part of the practical application.
Because manual practice is improved in our country and at our time, it is completely absent, to such an extent that the study of its science has become forgotten and its traces have disappeared. Only a few vestiges of it remain in the works of the ancients, which have been altered by hands and distorted by errors and confusion, making their meanings obscure and their benefits distant.
I have therefore decided to revive it and compose this article, explaining, elucidating, and condensing, and including illustrations of surgical instruments and other tools used in practice, as these are essential for a comprehensive understanding.
The reason there are no skilled manual practitioners in our time is that the practice of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first familiarize themselves with the science of anatomy, as described by Galen. So that they can understand the benefits of the organs, their structures, temperaments, connections and disconnections, and know the bones, nerves, muscles, their quantities, origins, arteries, and veins, both moving and stationary, and the locations of their openings.
Therefore, Albucasis said: "There are many physicians in name only, and few in actual practice, especially in the art of surgery. We have touched on this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable about the anatomy that we have mentioned is liable to make errors that can kill people, as I have often seen many who imagined themselves experts in this science but claimed it without knowledge or understanding.
I have seen a physician, ignorant of anatomy, attempt to remove a tumor from a woman's neck, accidentally damaging some of the neck's arteries, causing the woman to bleed to death in his hands.
I have also seen another physician, skilled in removing stones, attempt to extract a large stone from a man who had been stabbed in the kidney. Due to its size, he used part of the urinary bladder to remove it. The man died approximately three days later, and I had been called to remove the stone. Upon examining it, I estimated its size and the patient's condition.
I have also seen another physician, practicing medicine for a living, serving some of our country's leaders.
I witnessed a doctor, in his ignorance, rushing to treat a young Black man with a broken leg near the ankle, who also had a wound. The physician, without understanding, quickly immobilized the fracture using ropes and casts, tightly binding them, and did not leave any space for the wound to breathe.
Then he released himself to his desires, and left him for several days, ordering him not to untie the bandage until his leg and foot had swelled and were on the verge of death.
Then I was called to him, and I quickly untied the bandage, so he found relief from his pains, but the damage had already taken hold in the member, and I could not stop its progress.
The corruption continued to spread throughout the member until he died.
I saw another physician, skilled and wise, treating a tumor that was cancerous. After several days, it became worse for the patient, as cancer, when it is pure blackness, should not be treated with iron under any circumstances.
Except when it is in a member where it can be completely removed.
For this, my son, you must know that manual work is divided into two parts: the work that brings safety, and the work that causes harm in most cases.
I have warned everywhere in this book about the work that contains danger and fear; you must be cautious and reject it.
In order that the ignorant do not find an opportunity to speak or dispute, take hold of yourself with resolve and caution, and for your patients, with kindness and careful consideration.
And employ the best path leading to safety and a favorable outcome, avoiding dangerous and difficult diseases.
Purify yourselves from what you fear may enter your thoughts and cause doubt in your faith and worldly affairs, for it is the endurance of your status and elevation in both this world and the hereafter.
As Galen said in one of his precepts: "Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become bad doctors.
This article is divided into three doors.
First Door.
In this door, we discuss the treatment of wounds with fire and sharp medicines, arranged in order from the most severe to the least, including illustrations of cauterization tools, instruments, and all other necessary items for manual surgery.
Second Door.
In the sections on incisions, punctures, phlebotomy, cupping, surgery, removal of arrows, and so forth, all are categorized and arranged with illustrations of instruments.
**Chapter Three**
In the sections on setting bones, dislocations, treatment of fractures, and similar topics, all are categorized and arranged from the newest to the oldest with illustrations of instruments.
**Chapter One**
**On Burns**
Before discussing the procedure, it is necessary to mention the benefits and drawbacks, and in what temperaments it is used.
I say that discussing the benefits and drawbacks of burn treatment is a lengthy matter, a precise science, and a hidden mystery. Many scholars have spoken on this subject.
They have differing opinions on this topic, and I have condensed their brief remarks to avoid excessive length.
I say that burn treatment is beneficial in general for various temperaments, whether with or without a material cause, except for two: the hot temperament without a material cause and the dry temperament without a material cause.
On the Dry Humours and Their Treatment: Some maintain that cauterization is beneficial in cases of dry humours, while others disagree, stating that cauterization is not indicated for conditions characterized by heat and dryness, as fire itself possesses these qualities.
It is impossible to treat a hot and dry disease with a hot and dry remedy; those who advocate the opposite state that fire cauterization may be beneficial for a hot and dry condition in the human body. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the fire's temperament, it cools the human body.
And I adhere to his opinion, for experience has shown me this many times; however, one should not imagine this unless they have mastered and trained extensively in the art of cauterization. He observed the variation in human temperaments, the nature of diseases within individuals, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for the other humours, you have no cause for concern, especially regarding cold and wet conditions; all physicians agree on this, and there is no disagreement about the benefit of cauterization in such cases.
Know, O my children, that the secret of treating with fire (cauterization) and its superiority to other hot remedies lies in fire's singular nature; its effect is localized to the member it touches, causing minimal harm to nearby connected parts.
The use of cauterizing drugs may extend beyond the intended organ, potentially causing difficulty in treatment or even death. Fire, due to its noble and refined essence, does so only when misused.
This has been proven to us through experience, with lengthy service, meticulous care in practice, and understanding of the facts.
For this reason, I have omitted lengthy discourse; however, if it were not unbecoming of this book, I would share with you a mysterious secret about fire and its effect on the body, as well as its ability to dispel illness through philosophical and rational argument that would exceed your comprehension.
Know, my sons, that there is disagreement among them regarding the optimal time for cauterization, and they consider the best time to be in the spring.
I say that cauterization can be appropriate at any time, for the harm caused by time passes into the benefit gained through cauterization. Especially if cauterization is for essential, severe, and urgent pain that cannot be delayed due to the fear of further harm, which may be greater than the damage caused by time itself.
Do not think, my sons, as the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization, which heals a certain illness, will never return and is considered definitive; they make it a necessity.
And it is not as they thought, for cauterization is not like a remedy that alters the humor and dries up the moistures that cause pain.
Cauterization, however, is superior to medication in terms of the speed of its success, the strength of its effect, and the intensity of its power.
The illness may return at times due to the patient's humor, the persistence of their disease, the accumulation of humors in their body, and neglect of self-care through diet and other causes.
However, if the illness that requires cauterization is mild and in a body part with fewer humors and moistures, then... Such as cauterizing a tooth for pain, it may not return, at least in some cases.
And while the common belief is that cauterization is the ultimate remedy, it is not correct in their view, for they believe that no treatment with medication or otherwise can be effective after cauterization, which is not the case.
The meaning of cauterization being the 'ultimate remedy' is not that we abandon all other treatments, but rather, if we have attempted various remedies for an illness without success, and then finally resort to cauterization, which proves effective, then in this context, cauterization can be considered the ultimate remedy. Not according to the common understanding nor to the errors of many ignorant physicians.
The ancients stated that cauterization with gold is better than with iron, as they believed in the moderation and noble nature of gold. They claimed that it does not cause inflammation at the site of application, which is entirely false.
For I have experimented with it and found that its effect varies from one body to another, and cauterization with gold is better and more beneficial than with iron, as they claimed. However, when you heat the iron in the fire made of gold, you will not be able to determine when it reaches the desired temperature due to the red hue of gold.
Since it is subject to rapid cooling, and if you increase its heat during fever, it melts in the fire and becomes liquid, which leads the practitioner into difficulty. That is why cauterization with iron has become faster and more appropriate for our work, God willing.
I have organized this chapter on cauterization in sections, from the head to the foot, to make it easy for the student to find what they need, God willing.
Chapter One.
One cauterization for the head.
This method is effective against the moisture and cold that overwhelm the brain, causing headaches, frequent discharge from the head to the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, toothache, and throat pains. In general, it is useful for any condition resulting from coldness, such as paralysis, seizures, aphasia, and other similar ailments.
The method involves, first, ordering the patient to induce vomiting with a purgative that purifies the head for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and habitual condition.
Then, he is commanded to shave his head with a razor and sit between your hands, with his hands on his chest.
Then, you place the palm of your hand on the bridge of his nose between his eyes, and where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then, heat the oil-soaked iron (as depicted below).
The skin should be opened to the depth of a small bone, or as large as a cumin seed. If you pull your hand away, use the same iron (or another) to apply pressure if it cools down. Until you can see the bone as I have described, take some salt and dissolve it in water, then dip a cotton swab in it and apply it to the area, leaving it for three days.
Then take a cotton swab soaked in lard and apply it, leaving it until the inflammation from the fire subsides, then treat it with the quadruple ointment until it heals, God willing.
They say that the wound, the longer it remains open, the more pus it produces, which is better and more beneficial.
Some mention that the skin should be cauterized to the bone, holding the iron until some of the bone thickness burns away, then stripping off the burned portion of the bone and treating it afterward.
Others claim that cauterization should be done excessively, so it strongly affects the bone until it falls out, like amber or a small flake, and they assert that this releases vapors from the head and keeps the wound open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals, but I never use these types of cauterization except on certain people and, in my opinion, leaving it as it is is better, if it does not interfere with the natural connection of the head, as we have observed in other parts.
Especially when the patient's head is weak by nature, the first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion, and it is this that I have used and recommend.
Chapter Two.
On the head as well.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and persists for a long time, and treatments such as cauterization, cups, applicators, poultices, ointments, and bandages are used without relief, especially if the initial cauterization described has been ineffective, then consider the following. If the patient's head is strong in structure naturally and is not weak, and they experience severe coldness, then apply another cauterization slightly above the initial one. Then apply cauterization to each temple of the head until the thickness of the skin decreases and it reveals the desired amount from the bone. Apply cauterization to the back of the head, in the area known as the occiput, and be gentle with your hand so as not to expose the bone. The patient will experience severe pain from this cauterization, unlike any other pain caused by cauterization on the head. I will mention this particular cauterization at its appropriate place.
The iron used to treat the fore and rear horns of the head should be milder than the iron used for the central part of the head. And this is its image.
Chapter III.
In the treatment of a non-chronic fracture.
If a fracture of the head causes pain with a headache that radiates to the eye, and the patient vomits after taking purifying drugs for the head, and all other treatments mentioned in the classifications of diseases do not help, then there are two ways to apply heat in this case. Either apply heat with a sharp, burning drug or use iron.
As for applying heat with a burning drug, take one garlic clove, peel it and cut off both ends, then use a wide knife to make an incision in the affected area on the side of the head (temple) to create a space beneath the skin where the garlic clove can be placed.
It should be inserted beneath the skin until it disappears, then secure it tightly with bands and leave it for approximately fifteen hours. After that, remove the bandages, take out the garlic clove, and let the wound heal for two or three days before covering it.
On it, apply a cotton ball soaked in lard to the affected area until it suppures, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you prefer, you can use some of the burning drugs mentioned in the eighteenth article on burning drugs.
As for burning with iron, it is done in this manner: heat the iron with this shape.
It is called the nail iron because its head resembles a nail, with some indentations and a small protrusion in the center.
Then place it on the affected area and hold your hand while rotating the iron slightly, so that the amount burned is no more than half the thickness of the skin. Lift your hand to avoid burning the artery below, which could cause bleeding, then soak a cotton ball in salt water and apply it to the area, leaving it for three days. Then apply the cotton ball soaked in lard, treating it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
If you prefer, seal this wound with the sharp edge of the iron, taking care not to cut the artery in non-chronic wounds, especially.
Chapter Four.
On Chronic Hemorrhage.
If the treatment for hemorrhoids, as described above and in the divisions of diseases, is ineffective, and you observe a condition that precludes the use of the initial cauterization with medication or fire, then... The surgical scalpel should then be heated until it becomes blistered, after which you draw a line along the affected area with your finger, approximately half a finger's length, and apply pressure once to cut the vessel, reaching towards the bone.
However, you must be cautious to avoid damaging the jaw joint's mobility during chewing, which could burn the muscle or nerve controlling it, leading to spasms.
Be extremely careful and watchful for any bleeding from the artery you've cut; its vessels are filled with vital (groins). Especially for those who lack knowledge of the procedure, it is better to refrain from acting and seek guidance from experienced practitioners.
Further details on managing spontaneous bleeding from arteries, as well as the cauterization techniques mentioned earlier, will be discussed in this text, if God wills. If you encounter a condition that these treatments cannot address...
I observed the sick body to be amenable to a cauterization in the middle of the head as we have described, and treated the wound so that it may heal, God willing.
If you wish, you may use the cauterization that we mentioned in the chapter on arterial bleeding using a hot iron with two blades, for it is a better and more effective treatment.
Chapter V.
On the pains of the ears.
If a pain in the ear occurs due to cold and is treated with laxatives and other remedies mentioned in the divisions, but the pain persists, apply the hot iron, which is called the "point" with this shape.
Then, after heating it, apply the iron around the entire ear, or around both ears if the pain is in them. After locating the point with ink, apply the hot iron slightly away from the origin of the ear, making each cauterization about ten drops in each ear or similar, and then treat the wounds until they heal, God willing.
Chapter VI.
In your strength.
The strength that is treated with cautery is of the type that occurs from phlegm, as I mentioned in the divisions of diseases.
And one should avoid cautery of the type that occurs from dryness and nerve spasms.
When cautery with poultices, applications, and gargles does not heal this type of strength, then the patient should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly lower on the cheek, and the third at the junction of the lips.
And make your cautery from the opposite side of the diseased area, because relaxation occurs only on the side that appears healthy.
The image of cautery is to make a single cauterization opposite the upper corner of the ear, slightly below the brow, and another in the temple, with a length equal to the thumb.
Lower your cautery hand to burn at a depth of half the thickness of the skin. This is an image of the iron, which is a type of knife as described earlier, but slightly milder. The knife should have a slight excess of thickness, then the site should be treated as mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter VII.
In the case of chronic paralysis.
If the paralysis becomes chronic and treatment with what we mentioned does not take effect, and there is no fever in the patient, apply four leech bites. Apply a leech bite on each temple and one in the middle of the forehead, as mentioned, and another at the back of the head, following the same method.
The application of heat, as mentioned above, can also be done with a leech bite on the stomach, which is even more effective, then treated using the methods previously described.
Chapter VIII.
On forgetfulness.
For one who suffers from forgetfulness due to phlegm, the patient should be given warm drinks and large quantities of diuretics and brain-cleansing grains.
Then he shaves all his head, and a poultice of mustard, as described in the article on poultices, is applied to his rear. Carrying it several times, it acts like cauterization.
Do the same as mentioned there, and if he recovers, otherwise, three cauterizations are applied to his rear of the head, aligned from the top of the head to the bottom of the neck.
Make a space between each cauterization, as thick as a finger, and then treat the cauterization with what has been mentioned before. If you wish to add more, and if the patient can tolerate it, then make another cauterization in the middle one.
If you wish to add more, make two cauterizations on the horns, and then treat it until he recovers, with a cauterizing instrument in the shape of an olive as mentioned before.
Chapter IX
For treating paralysis and relaxing all the body.
It is necessary to purify the head with aromatic herbs as mentioned, then shave the patient's head, and cauterize a cut in the middle of the head, and a cauterization on each horn of the head, and a cauterization on its rear.
Three on the vertebrae of the neck, if, in the event of a bodily weakness, you need more than that and the patient is capable of tolerating it and the illness is strong and well-established, then apply four burns to the vertebrae of his back. And continue with the burns until most of the skin is burned off, then raise your hand and treat it as mentioned earlier, so that it may heal, God willing. The iron should be olive-shaped.
Chapter X.
On seizures.
Only the person suffering from a seizure caused by phlegm should be burned, after first purifying his brain with powerful purgatives and other treatments mentioned in the previous sections, if the patient is old and capable of taking medicine.
However, if he is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then use gargles and a cleansing paste for the brain several days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then he should shave his head, and apply one burn in the middle of the head as described earlier, and another burn at the back. And on each side of his head, make a burn, if the patient is strong and tolerable, then apply the burns as mentioned for the one with paralysis, relaxing the body over the cervical and dorsal vertebrae, and the iron should be olive-shaped as described previously.
If the patient is a child, then make the iron gentle in this manner.
Chapter XI.
On the burn for melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is putrefied humours and thick phlegm, then apply the burns as mentioned for the one with paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is a dark, inclined humour and the patient's body is putrefied, then purge his brain as described in the previous division.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake from linen in the shape of a circle, and place it in the middle of his head while he sits cross-legged, holding it from all sides. Then take one pound of ancient sheep fat.
Then heat it over a moderate fire until the finger can tolerate it when inserted, then empty it onto the patient's head in the shape of a circle and leave it until it cools. Repeat this process for the patient once a week, along with all other good care, until he heals, God willing.
If you wish to treat it, apply many small drops without holding the iron with your hand, but through steaming. This type of burning moistens the brain gently.
Then apply a cotton ball soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
In the burning of water that descends into the eye.
If you notice the onset of water descending into the eye, as described in the previous sections, act swiftly to purify and protect the afflicted individual's head from all moistures. Bathe them in a hot bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave their head and apply a burn to the middle of their head, followed by two burns on each temple. If water begins to descend into both eyes or just one, proceed accordingly.
Then, using the iron, cut all veins and arteries beneath the skin, making sure to apply a burn that is both long and wide across each temple. Be cautious to prevent blood loss.
If you notice any of it, cut it off at the base with any means available to you. We will discuss later the wisdom of clamping and cutting arteries while preventing blood loss.
A burn may be made on the nape of the neck between two vertebrae with severe, powerful cauterization.
Chapter XIII.
In the treatment of chronic tear discharge.
If the tear discharge from the eyes is chronic and persistent, and it is found to be due to swollen veins and arteries on the surface of the head outside the eye, and you are certain that this is caused by a thick cold effusion in the lymphatic system, then apply the cauterization as described earlier for the initial stage of the water discharge. Cauterization in the middle of the head, two cauterizations on the temples, and two beneath the vertebrae at the nape of the neck. If further relief is required, apply an additional cauterization on each side of the eye, just above the eyebrow with a small iron.
Chapter XIV.
On cauterizing the nasal cavity.
If the treatment as described in the previous sections is unsuccessful, then immediately proceed to remove the nasal polyps over three nights, and afterward, shave the patient's head and apply a cauterization with the oil iron (mukawwa al-zaytuniya) to the central wound. Then apply the iron (coating) in two heats above the eyebrows, slightly below the hair, and be cautious of the artery, do not cut it, and this is a picture of the (iron) coating.
Chapter XV.
In the process of relaxing the eyelid.
If the eyelid relaxes due to illness or moisture, apply one heat with this horseshoe iron.
And if you wish, apply them above the eyebrows slightly, with two heats on each side, and separate them from the temples, so that each heat is as long as the eyebrow.
And do not overdo the heat with your hand, but only as much as burns one-third of the skin, and the iron should be of this form.
Chapter XVI.
In the process of applying heat to the eyelid.
If the eyelids turn inward, there are two types of heat to apply. Either by burning with fire or by burning medicine, if by fire, instruct the patient to remove their beard, if it is one that is plucked, until it grows long and even.
If you pluck it while it is young, bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so they do not move until it grows and straightens. Once it has grown and leveled out, place the patient's head in your lap, then draw on the eyelid with ink a sign in the shape of an Asian tree leaf.
The sign should begin near the lashes, then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or alfalfa saliva under the eyelid. Heat a heated iron to this shape.
Then, burn the shape you drew little by little in many sessions until the skin surface is burned, forming the shape of an Asian tree leaf, entirely visible. The mark of successful work is that the eyelid will be puffy and the hair around the eye will stand up from the eye socket.
Then, raise your hand and leave it for three days. After that, apply a cotton ball dipped in lard to remove the crust, and treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If any hair returns after some time and the eyelid relaxes, repeat the process.
If the hair is on the lower eyelid, burn it again in the same way as before, until it returns to its natural position and does not affect the eye. For burning with a hot medicine, instruct the patient to let their lashes grow long and even.
Then, make a paper from the bark of the Asia tree, and take from the usual soap and unquenched lime, each weighing a drachm or its equivalent, and crush them both thoroughly. Do this quickly to prevent drying. Then, you lay it flat on the paper made from the bark of the Asia tree, placing it over one or both eyes. Under the eye, place a cotton ball soaked in egg white and rest your head on a pillow.
Place your index finger on the medication, press slightly, and move it as the patient feels the burning sensation, as it will feel like fire. As long as they feel the burn, continue to move it. If the burning stops, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
Observe: if the eyelid raises as it does when exposed to fire or a cut, then you have successfully applied the treatment. If not, reapply the medicine to the areas that did not respond or turn black until your work is complete. Then, place a cotton ball coated in butter on the eye to soothe the burning sensation.
Then, treat it with a palm butter ointment or similar until it heals. When treating, be extremely careful not to let any of the medication fall into the eye. If after a few days the eyelid relaxes and there is concern about hair falling into the eye, repeat the treatment.
Then prepare the medicine again on that part of the eyelid which has relaxed, specifically as you did initially, and treat it until it heals, and this is a figure of the leaf of the Asian tree.
Know that people's eyes may differ in size and shape, so let your tightness be according to that, and the path to correctness is not hidden from one who has experience in this craft.
Chapter XVII
On the treatment of the abscess that appears in the corner of the eye.
If you treat the abscess as we mentioned in the classifications of diseases and it does not respond to your treatment, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head on your lap, and have a servant hold his head with both hands, firmly but without jostling it.
Then place a wet cotton ball in the white of an egg or in the saliva of a lizard on his eyes, and heat the iron in the following manner.
It should be hollow like a feather tube from one end, where the cauterization takes place, and if you prefer, it can have a perforated other end or be solid like a rod, but this hollow shape is better for your work, God willing.
Then, if the fistula is open, press it and extract the pus and dry it; or if it is not open, open it and extract the pus. Then apply a hot iron to it, which will be very soothing, and hold it with your hand until you reach the bone.
And move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose so that your hand does not mistake or disturb the patient, causing the iron to fall into the eye and damage it; if it reaches the bone in the first application, otherwise, reapply the iron if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with butter and treat it with a drying ointment until it heals; if it does not heal after forty days, apply a sharp, corrosive medicine to expose the bone and remove it as mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
There is another method of treating a fistula mentioned by some early scholars: direct your attention to the site of the fistula, make a small incision there, and insert a thin brass cap into the same opening. This is its image.
Pour into it, in a melted state, the weight of a drachma of lead, holding the cap firmly with your hand; the patient must not move at all so that the molten lead does not enter his eye. It is necessary to place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye.
If lead burns the site of the fistula, it will heal in a remarkable manner. If the fistula heals as mentioned through cauterization and treatment, otherwise, it is mandatory to use the nose hole and return the fistula to the nasal passage as will be explained in its specific section.
**Chapter XVIII**
**On Cauterizing a Lip Fistula**
**On Treating a Lip Rift (Fistula)**
The cavity should be made with the precision of a knife, then place it protectively with the wheel in the same fissure until the cauterization reaches the depth of the fissure, and then treat it with the curette until it heals, God willing.
Chapter XIX
On the cauterization of the naphthys (ulcer) occurring in the mouth.
If a tumor appears in the gum, or in the soft palate, or at the base of the teeth, and becomes ulcerated, drains, and forms a naphthys (ulcer) that exudes pus, and treatment does not take effect, then one should heat an instrument to the extent that it fits into the naphthys.
Then insert it protectively into the naphthys' hole and hold your hand until the heated metal reaches its depth, repeating this process once or twice, then treat it with the mentioned remedies until it heals, God willing.
If the material (cauterization) falls off and it heals, otherwise, the place should be examined, and the corrupt bone should be removed as mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter XX
In dealing with loose teeth and gums.
If the gums become relaxed by moisture, and the teeth move, treat them with medications, but if they do not improve, place the patient's head in your lap and then apply the iron (as depicted later), after placing a pipe on the tooth.
The iron is inserted with a wheel, and you hold it slightly so the patient feels the heat of the fire reach the root of the tooth, then remove your hand, and repeat this process several times as desired, then have the patient rinse their mouth with salt water.
It is held for an hour and then thrown away, for the moving tooth will be stabilized, the relaxed gums will tighten, and the corrupt moisture will evaporate.
Chapter 21.
For toothache.
If the toothache is due to cold or has a worm, and medications do not help, there are two methods of treatment: either apply hot butter or apply heat from fire.
As for the hot butter treatment, take fresh butter and boil it in a metal bowl or shell, then take a cotton ball, wrap it around the end of a rod, dip it in the boiling butter, and apply it to the aching tooth with a wheel, holding it until it cools.
Then repeat this process until the heat reaches the root of the tooth. If you wish, immerse a piece of wool or cotton in cold lard and place it on the painful tooth, then put a hot iron on top until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
And for burning it with fire, you should take a copper or iron tube that has some thickness to prevent the heat of the fire from reaching the patient's mouth, then heat the iron in a way that its shape is preserved and place it on the tooth, while holding your hand until the iron cools down, repeating this process several times.
The pain will either disappear that very day or on another day; after the burning, the patient should fill their mouth with good lard, hold it for a while, then spit it out, and this is the shape of the iron.
Burn with any side you choose, according to what is possible, and this is the shape of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Second.
On burning swine.
If the swine is afflicted with phlegm and cold humors, and does not yield to medications for maturation, and you wish for a rapid maturation, then heat the hollow iron in both ends, as illustrated here.
To make smoke emerge from the burned area, apply the hot iron shield-like over the tumor once or twice, depending on need, until you reach the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, adjust the size of the iron to match the tumor.
Then leave it for three days, and apply a cotton ball soaked in lard to it until the burnt parts heal, then treat it with ointment and bandages until it heals, God willing.
Chapter XXIII.
For burns affecting the throat and causing difficulty breathing.
If moisture dominates the trachea, especially if it is accompanied by cold temperament, the patient should first be induced to vomit with facilitative drugs, then apply a hot iron to the throat at the base of the neck, in the lower region.

The duration of treatment, as we mentioned for liver cancer, involves burns in this manner, and this method has risks. Either the patient dies immediately or a fistula develops at the site, which is incurable.

Some wise scholars have described a procedure for incising the hip, creating a cylinder of iron with a diameter of half a span, and an opening at the top slightly smaller than the pit of a date palm. Inside this cylinder, they insert a smaller cylinder, followed by another one.

Many times, then, treat it with a poultice until it heals. Until the stinging subsides, then raise your hand and wipe the stinging water off the hip with fresh water. Leave it for three days, applying butter to aid healing. After several more days, the pus will drain, indicating maximum benefit.
Then treat it with poultices until it heals. If the pain spreads to the thigh or leg, create a mold for it, holding water in the same manner as the hip, as described in the article on preparing medicinal poultices.
Take from saltpeter and unquenched lime a portion from each, grind them, and place them in a new pot with a small hole at the bottom, large enough to accommodate the rod, and place another pot underneath, lined with clay.
Add fresh water to the saltpeter and lime, immersing them with your finger after tightly mixing them in your hand. Leave the pot until the hot water has settled at the bottom of the clay pot, then gather all the water and pour it over new saltpeter and lime.
It becomes extremely sharp, playing a crucial role in various surgical procedures and treating various conditions, akin to the effect of fire. Among the medications that use this method are fennel, honey from mullein, and lime with soap, all applied topically.

It will heal, by the will of God. Know that no one should use heat with these remedies unless the body has been emptied, and Diascorides mentioned that goat's sweat, when applied as heat, is beneficial for sciatica.

On the cauterizing instrument for the ulcer occurring in the body.
When ugly pustules appear in the body due to cold, thick, corrupt materials, they should be cauterized at their first emergence with a gentle burn using a reed stick whose tip has been ignited, or with the root of long turmeric, or with a lens-shaped iron.
The fistula should be cauterized in this manner at its first appearance, so that it does not increase and disperse the beneficial effect of the treatment, and the patient recovers, except that this should be done after draining the patient through phlebotomy.
Chapter LXXXVI.
Cauterizing arterial bleeding upon cutting the artery.
Arterial bleeding often occurs when a cut or injury exposes a previously unseen artery, or when a tumor or other mass is incised and cauterized, making it difficult to control the flow of blood.
If this happens to anyone, quickly place your finger over the artery's opening and apply pressure firmly so that the blood is contained under your finger and does not escape, then take several small and large hot iron tools (scalpels) and heat them over a flame until they are very hot.
Then take one of the hot iron tools, either small or large, depending on the size of the wound and the location where the artery burst, and apply it to the same vein after quickly removing your finger, gripping the tool tightly until the bleeding stops.
If, when you raise your finger away from the artery's orifice and apply the iron, it extinguishes, take another iron with haste from among those prepared in the fire, and continue to do so one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve; if there is an opening, it may cause further harm to the patient. Know that if the artery bleeds, it cannot be cut, especially if it is a large artery, unless one of four methods is used: either by burning as we have mentioned, or by amputation if it has not yet detached. If it is amputated, both ends will contract and the bleeding will stop. It can be secured with tight stitches or treated with medications that prevent blood flow and tightly wrapped with bandages.
One who attempts to stop the bleeding with bindings, or by tightening rags, or applying burning substances, etc., will not succeed in cutting it off entirely, except rarely. If someone encounters such a situation without a doctor or medicine, they should immediately place their index finger over the wound's orifice as described.
They should tightly secure it until the blood thins, flows out from above the wound and onto the artery and finger, and freezes, becoming thick and stopping. During this time, they should assess what is needed, such as burning or medication.
The Second Chapter
In incisions, abscesses, phlegmasia, and other surgical procedures.
As we mentioned in the first chapter, every disease that responds to burning with fire and hot remedies, its causes, symptoms, instruments, and images of cauterization, I have organized into chapters from the ancient to the modern era.
And I follow the same method in this chapter to facilitate the student's understanding of their requirements.
Before I begin, you should know, my children, that this chapter contains even more uncertainty than the first one on burning.
For this reason, caution should be greater, as the work in this chapter often involves significant blood loss, which is vital to life when opening a vein, treating a tumor, draining an abscess, performing surgery, or removing an arrow, among other procedures that carry uncertainty and danger, with death often being the outcome.
I warn you against falling into doubt, for in this profession, you may encounter various types of people suffering from different ailments. Some may have grown weary of their illness and be indifferent to death.
To the extreme of his pain, the length of his suffering, and the persistence of his illness, there is a sign that death is imminent. Some may even give you their wealth in the hope of regaining health, but these are people whose conditions should never be assisted.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and diligence, and do not proceed with any of these things except after certain knowledge that confirms the favorable outcome, utilizing in all treatments for your patients the advancement of knowledge and warning them of the consequences for their safety.
For in this, you will find assistance in gaining praise, glory, and gratitude. May God guide you, my children, to wisdom and divert you from error, for that is in His hands, there is no god but Him.
I have organized this chapter based on what was mentioned in the previous chapter about cauterization, from the ancient to the modern, so that it may be easier for you to understand and obtain what you desire from it, God willing.
First Chapter
On the treatment of water retention in the heads of children.
This condition often affects children at birth when the midwife applies excessive force during delivery, and it can also arise from an underlying unknown disease.
I have not seen this condition except in boys, and all of them whom I have seen, death comes to them more quickly. Therefore, I have decided to abandon this procedure. I have seen a boy whose head was filled with water and the head swells every day. Even the boy could not sit because of the swelling in his head and the increasing moisture until he died.
This moisture either collects between the skin and bone or under the bone on the peritoneum. If the moisture is between the skin and bone and the tumor is small, one should make a single vertical cut through the middle of the head, about two fingers long, to allow the moisture to drain. This is an illustration of the surgical instrument.
If the moisture is excessive and the tumor greater, make two intersecting cuts as illustrated:
If the moisture is under the bone, and you observe that the seams of the head are open on all sides, and if pressing it with your hand causes the water to flow inwards, then you should make three cuts through the middle of the head as illustrated.
After the cuts, all moisture is drained, then the incisions are bound with bandages and strips. After five days, the bindings are removed, and the wound is treated with twists and ointments. The head should be held gently, and the patient fed a dry diet with minimal moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals, another method of incision is to observe where the bone of the tumor appears and the accumulation of fluid, as it may be more in the back of the head or at the front, or on the right or left side.
You intend by the incision where the tumor appears and the accumulation of fluid, so you make an incision as possible without cutting an artery to prevent bleeding that may lead to the patient's death due to this loss of blood along with the expulsion of moisture.
Chapter II.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears known as the sensitives.
When a severe attack occurs in the eyes or chest and persists despite medical treatment, the most effective cure is to cut these two arteries.
Therefore, if you wish to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to expose the artery. Next, tighten the patient's robe around their neck, look for the pulse, and locate the two depressions behind the ears, where they are most visible in most people.
Then, mark them with ink and cut them with a sharp knife all the way to the bone, making the incision across the head's width. If you prefer, insert the knife from beneath the artery and cut upwards. The length of the cut should be approximately two joined fingers.
If the pulse ceases, the blood will flow out in a pulsating manner, advancing and receding regularly. If the artery is not palpable, estimate its location from the ear, at a distance of about three fingers, then mark it with ink and make an incision towards the bone.
The blood flows in six waves or perhaps more or less, depending on the patient's condition and the fullness of their arteries. Then, examine the wound; if any part of the peritoneum remains attached to the bone, cut it away.
To prevent a hot swelling, apply a thread of cotton cloth and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter III
Treating the arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic colds or acute attacks characterized by intense moisture and heat in the temple muscles, or severe chronic headaches, and if they have undergone various medical treatments without improvement, we have found that treating the arteries in the temples (by either cutting them or cauterizing them as described) is effective for these conditions.
The procedure for treating the arteries involves shaving the hair in the temple area, focusing on the visible artery in the temple. By observing its pulse, you can determine the condition of the artery; indeed, it is rare for an individual to have a hidden or inaccessible artery.
In case of severe cold, if you do not feel the pulse, let the patient wrap himself tightly in his clothes, then rub or apply hot water to the area until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel whose image this is. Then use it to carefully peel the skin until you reach the artery, then insert a hook and pull it upwards to extract it from the skin, removing all adhesions underneath on both sides. If the artery is thin, twist it with the tip of the hook. Then cut from it a portion according to how far its ends spread and contract, without causing bleeding. If it is not amputated or severed, the blood will not flow at all. Then drain the blood from six to three openings.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong double thread, which can be either linen or string from a bow, so that mold does not quickly affect it before the wound heals, preventing bleeding.
Then cut off the remainder between the two ties. You can do this at that moment or later, and if you wish, brand it with a skinned iron tool towards the bone, so that its ends break off, serving as a replacement for this work or even better, as we have said.
Unless the patient suffers from fever or mood disturbance, because heat is helpful in expelling humors, it becomes more beneficial. After draining the veins, the wound should be filled with old cotton and covered with tight bandages. Subsequently, it should be treated with dry medicines that nourish the flesh and twisting until healing occurs, God willing.
If, while working, bleeding occurs from an artery, cut it either with heat or by filling the wound with wax and tightening your hand until the blood stops. If nothing is at hand, place your finger in the wound to clot the blood.
If the wound is washed with very cold water to relieve pain and tightened as needed, it is lighter and easier to drain the vein by burning the sweat with this iron instrument having two blades, after marking the positions with ink.
Lower it carefully until it reaches the bone and cuts off the vein at two points to separate its ends, for it will never heal with this heat. This is a diagram of the iron.
It has two blades, resembling small shears, but the edges are much less sharp. If both are very sharp, cold water will affect them faster and the flesh will not be cut as quickly. If there is some stiffness in them, they can grip hot fire.
And cut the flesh swiftly, for this action is best of all and lightest and easiest, and let it be between the two knives as thick as a finger.
Chapter IV.

Then, make three parallel incisions on the forehead, each about the length of two fingers, with one incision in the middle of the forehead parallel to the length of the nose, the second a little distance from the muscle movement in the temple, and the third on the other side.
Be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the connection of the jawbones. After each incision, use three fingers spread apart, and have sponge pieces or dry cloths ready to absorb the blood.

From the fissure next to the temple to the middle fissure, and peel off all the skin between the two fissures with the peritoneum that is on the bone, and then do the same from the middle fissure to the third fissure, and then remove this scalpel. After allowing enough time for the blood to flow, compress the areas from which the clotted blood is removed, and then in each fissure place a cotton thread and cover it with a sterile dressing soaked in a mixture of wine and oil or vinegar and oil.
So that a hot swelling does not occur, and on the third day loosen the bandage and use plenty of warm compresses, then treat it with a paste of basil and rose oil and all other treatments for wounds until healing occurs, God willing.

Chapter VI.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear belongs to one of four types: either a metallic stone or a semi-precious stone like iron and glass, or a plant seed such as lentil and nut, or something fluid like water, vinegar, and the like.
And if it is an animal, when a pebble or a type of pebble that does not grow large in the ear falls into the ear, and you receive the sun with your ear, then squeeze some violet fat or serum into it and try to remove it by moving your head or blowing through a cotton bud. And plug the nostrils when a sneeze comes, after placing a bandage around the ear with cloth or wool and stretching the ear upwards. Often, this treatment will expel the foreign object. If it does not come out, try to remove it gently using a soft tool like that shown here.
If the foreign body has come out by itself, try to remove it with a delicate, flexible hook. If it does not come out this way, make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole. It is good.
Seal around the tube with grease-soaked pitch to prevent air from entering other than through the tube, then pull it strongly and frequently; it will often come out this way. And try to cut that type of grain that has fallen into the ear, but only if you are certain that this grain has become softened by the steam of the ear, making it into small pieces, then remove them with a blind hook or a delicate dry cloth or by sucking.
As we have mentioned, it is easier to remove them. As for the water in the ear, the patient should first use a blow nose device or something similar while lying down on the affected ear with some old cotton, if it comes out this way.

He should continue this process with stone after stone until all water is expelled. The water can also be removed by taking a quill or feather, inserting one end into the ear, and burning the other end to create more steam, then repeating with another feather.
Do this several times until all the water is removed or draw it out with a tube, as mentioned in the case of stones. As for removing the animal that has entered it, if it is small-bodied like a tick and similar, treat it as mentioned in the classification.
If the body is large and visible, try to remove it with tweezers and hooks; this is easier than all other things that get stuck in the ear. Chapter VII
On the treatment of an ear blockage.
Some children may be born with their eardrums unperforated.
Some people may also experience a blockage in the ear canal due to injury or flesh growth inside, and this blockage can be deep within the ear hole, making it invisible to the naked eye.
If the foreign body protrudes slightly from the wound and is visible, it will be difficult to remove. If it is hidden deep within the ear and cannot be seen, it will be even more challenging to extract. In all cases, the patient's affected ear should be exposed to sunlight. If, upon examination, the blockage is visible, open it gently with a delicate scalpel. This is its appearance: .
The blade should have a slightly curved tip and both sides should be smooth to avoid damaging the ear. If the blockage is caused by flesh that has grown within the ear canal, use a fine hook and cut it carefully, removing all the flesh.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, take a gentle, smooth bend, warm it slightly in fire, and then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the blockage along with any sensation of hearing weight from the patient, carefully break off the tip.
Be careful not to injure the ear drum. Then, make a wick inside the ear that fits the size of the opening. Dip it in Egyptian ointment and apply it for several days until you are sure the wound will not stick or close. Alternatively, take a wick, soak it in water, and sprinkle powdered glass on it.
If you see that the ear has swelled and is hot, remove the wick and replace it with a new one soaked in rose fat to soothe the burning inflammation. Then treat it until it heals.
If there is bleeding, immerse a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use all other treatments to stop the mentioned bleeding as described in the article on catheters.
Chapter VIII.
In the treatment of warts appearing on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry, so they should be grabbed with a tweezers or a hook and cut off at the root with a scalpel, then the affected area should be treated with powdered glass.
If blood bleeds, remove them with a gentle lentil-shaped iron, and applying heat afterward is better after cutting; however, they often return if any roots remain. If the roots are burned with fire, they will not grow back.
Chapter IX.
In the treatment of cold in the eyelids.
It often exposes something that resembles cold in its intensity and hardness on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called "al-برد" (the cold), which is the accumulation of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The procedure involves observation: if the cold is prominent on the outer surface of the eyelid, it moves in all directions, then it is easy to make a horizontal cut through it.
Then, peel it gently from all sides until you are free, then hang it on a hook and cut it. If cutting it is not possible without completely severing the eyelid, this will not harm the patient. If the cut is large, sew it together and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no need to worry, as the ointment will heal and seal it. If the cold tends towards the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the lid upside down and hang the cold on a hook without the need for cutting, then cut it from all sides.
If cutting through the eyelid is necessary, this will not harm the patient. Then, wash the eye after removing the cold with salty water and treat the area to promote healing until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of a fissure that appears in the eyelid.
The conjunctiva is a membrane that lies in the upper eyelid, most commonly affecting children. It weighs on their eyes and causes them to have discharge at night, preventing them from seeing sunlight. This causes tears to flow more quickly towards them.
Therefore, they are often seen resting their faces and sleeping with their eyelids under the eyebrows moistened by discharge, and the conjunctiva appears clearly visible. When you press on the area with your fingers, you can feel the conjunctiva between them. The treatment involves placing the patient's head in your lap.
Then, take a cotton cloth, prepare a thread from it, and make a circle with that thread around the conjunctiva from all sides. Place it on top of the conjunctiva and press gently with your fingers from all sides to gather the conjunctiva in the center of the circle.
Then, using a sharp scalpel, make a vertical cut in the center of this discharge, not larger than the cut made during phlebotomy. In depth, you should cut through all the skin layers until you reach the conjunctiva.
In most cases, the conjunctiva protrudes from the cut as a white piece of fat. Using a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers, gently pull it out and rotate your hand to the right and left until it comes free. Be careful not to increase the depth of the cut or the scalpel may enter the eye, causing damage.
If the snare does not appear at the beginning of the incision, it is necessary to slightly increase the depth of the incision gently until the snare becomes visible, then pull it as we have said. Then dip a piece of cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the wound, securing it with a bandage, and some people crush salt onto it.
He places it inside the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture, then treats it until it heals. If a hot lump forms at the site, treat it with soothing remedies until it heals.
Chapter 11.
On various forms of eye compression.
If growths develop on the eyelid, forming additional lashes that grow in a different direction from the natural lashes and become persistent, they can harm the eye and cause various conditions such as constant tearing, drooping eyelids, white spots, and thickness, potentially leading to vision impairment.
Eye compression can be performed in four ways: either by burning with fire, or using sharp medications as mentioned in the chapter on burning, or by cutting and stitching, or by using reed (or similar material) as I have previously stated.
You should position the patient's head in your lap and, with your left hand, flip the eyelid. If it flips, otherwise, insert an needle with a thread underneath the eyelid, passing the needle through the thread from above, close to the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards towards the eyelid.
Turn it with the retractor, then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid, avoiding the surplus hair, using a scalpel from the outer corner to the inner corner of the eye. Then pass the thread and place a small pad of cotton or cloth under the eyelid. Finally, mark on the eyelid with ink.
It should resemble the shape of an oak leaf, but the shape should be adjusted to achieve the desired lifting of the eyelid, as people may differ in this regard; some may require cutting a suitable amount from the eyelid depending on how loose it is.
Some may require less cutting, adjusted to the looseness of the eyelid; then, using the scalpel, make incisions along the marked lines, starting from the outer corner to the inner corner of the eye, with each cut close to the natural hairline, approximately the width of the retractor.
Then, insert the forceps into one corner of the skin and peel it entirely; then, sew the edges together with a needle and thin woolen thread, clean the blood, and attach any remaining threads to the eyebrows using sticky substances.
You may choose to do so or not, but either way, the stitches and threads will remain for about three or four days, then you can treat it; alternatively, you can leave the wound unstitcher and treat it with drying and constricting agents, as closing the wound and stitching it together is preferable.
This aspect of tightness is mentioned by the ancients, but it provides relief to the patient and is one of the good practices with no risk involved. There is another aspect in the tightening as well, which is to shape the eyelid like the form of a date palm leaf, as we have described. Lift the entire marked skin as we have described, then sew together the edges of the wound and treat it until it heals. If a hot lump appears when you cut or incise, soothe it with cauterization and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also experience a condition where its lashes turn inward. In this case, the procedures mentioned, including cutting, stitching, and tightening with fire or sharp drugs, are more effective and less harmful to the patient than complete removal and stitching.
The incision with the reed should be made as follows: turn the eyelid inside out and make a cut as described, then create two thin reeds or wooden splints whose length corresponds to that of the eyelid and whose width is less than that of the scalpel.
