Chapter Twelve.
Raising the dislodged eyelash with a needle.
If it is a single hair or two, the procedure is as follows: take a fine needle and insert a thin, smooth silk thread into it. Then gather both ends and tie them together with a very small knot. The length of the thread should be approximately one span (about 10 cm).
Then, attach a finer thread to the bandage, slightly shorter than the previous one, and tie both ends. Place the patient's head on your lap, close to the sun for better visibility, as this is a delicate procedure. Then, insert the needle with the thread into the base of the stray hair.
Perform this procedure on the eyelid with haste to extricate it above the natural hair, then pull the thread over the bandage and insert it into that bandage, whether one, two, or three hairs at most.
Then, with your hand, you pull on it until the hair comes out with the bandage in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pulled the bandage but the hair did not come out with it, pull the bandage down using the thread that was inserted into it, so it comes out from the upper hole.
Then, you put the hair back in and pull it until it rises in the upper hole, making it clearly visible. At that point, release the bandage and thread, remove them, and apply pressure to the eye for two or three days until the hair takes its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a longer natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after some time, then raise it.
Chapter XIII.
In the treatment of the aperture (or wound) that occurs on the upper eyelid of the eye.
The condition described, where the aperture (or wound) forms on the upper eyelid, is called "arnabiya" (or rabbit-like). This aperture can be either natural or acquired through conditions such as healing of a scar, laceration, incision, or any other similar cause.
In this procedure, the task is to incise the hematoma, divide its edges, and insert a thread of linen between them, securing it until healing occurs. It is not advisable to use drying or constricting agents in its treatment.
If, however, you do this, the hematoma will return worse than before, and you should use agents that relax it, such as fennel or anusmith (a preparation) in water with crushed chamomile and flaxseed, mixed with a quantity of fat, and apply it to the thread.
The essence of its treatment is to strive by every means to prevent it from returning to its original state, and the procedure we mentioned is merely a form of repairing the hematoma, not reversing the site's condition as it was before.
Chapter XIV
In the treatment of hematomas on the lower eyelid.
This hematoma, which occurs on the lower part (of the body), is what is truly called a "stra" (hematoma). It is natural and occurs incidentally; the incidental one results from a wound, cut, burn, or similar event.
The procedure involves taking an needle with a doubled thread and sewing it into the flesh, passing it from the left corner of the eye to the right, so that the thread passes through both sides of the flesh. Then, using the needle, you lift the flesh above and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is appropriate, otherwise take a knife and place it on the site of the incision and flip the eyelid with it, making two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, and the ends of the incisions will be from the angles of the cut that was made until they meet, forming an angle. Until they meet, it will have the shape as shown here.
It is the Greek letter lambda, then you remove that flesh as much as the sharp side of it is below what lies against the eyelid, then gather the scattered parts with two stitches, stitching it with a woolen thread, and treat it with the soothing drugs and twists as we mentioned until it heals.
If the injury was from a cut, stitching, or burning, then one should make a simple incision without affecting the hairs of the lid, and then separate the lips with twists as mentioned before.
In conclusion, when treating an injury from above or below, the treatment should be tailored to the specific condition of the wound, as it may vary greatly in appearance. The surgeon must use their discretion and devise a solution using any means available.
Until the shape returns to its natural state or approximates it, the same principles apply for determining suitable treatment and tools in most cases.
Chapter XV.
In the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may prevent many people from moving their eye lid freely and impede its natural functions, so a thin strip should be inserted under the eyelid.
Then raise it above or extend it with a hook, and then cut the adhesion with a delicate scalpel that is not as sharp as ordinary scalpels, but rather blunt, so as not to disturb the patient during the procedure, preventing the scalpel from slipping and causing harm to the eye with its sharpness. The cut should be made as if you were peeling off the adhesion.
And when the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed, then pour salt water into the eye to clean it or apply a drop of ginger-based solution, followed by separating the lid from the eye with a cotton string.
Place a wet woolen pad over the eye and, after three days, use compresses soaked in honey until it heals.
Chapter XVI.
In cutting the tendon and stitching the flesh of the wound.
The tendon is of two kinds: either nerve-bound, resembling a thin, rigid membrane; or non-nerve-bound, resembling a hard, white consistency that breaks when touched with iron or caught with a hook.
And if a hook does not adhere to it, both types begin from the larger orifice and gradually increase in depth until they obscure vision and prevent light and eye movement. The method of cutting them involves the patient lying on their back on your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, you raise the eyelid with your hand, grab the tendon with a slightly flexible hook, and pull it up. Then take a needle and insert a horse or cattle hair and a strong thread into it, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and pierce it through the middle of the tendon.
You push the needle through and tie the tendon with the thread, pulling it up, and then peel away the tendon from the iris with the hair as if spreading it with the hair until the end. Then cut the remaining part at the base of the orifice using a delicate scalpel or a small scissors, leaving the natural eye meat.
To prevent the constant flow of tears, the tendon can be pulled with a hook alone or with thread as mentioned, then peeled using a smooth-ended scalpel, as illustrated below.
Be cautious that the surgical knife does not touch the corneal membrane, causing a rupture and widening of the pupil. After making the cut, apply some powdered salt or zingari (a type of ointment) to the eye and leave it until the following day, then treat it as necessary until healing occurs.
If the eyelid is non-nervous and neither an needle nor a hook can be inserted into it, this type of eyelid often heals on its own with medication as described in the preceding sections. If you decide to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a delicate, smooth scalpel as shown in the figure. Carefully remove the eyelid from above with a gentle, smooth motion. If it appears loose, brittle, and separated only due to this motion, apply some zingari (a type of ointment) or red salve or powdered salt to the eye and leave it until the following day.
Then, continue the process until all of it is removed, unless a hot, swollen tumor appears in the eye, in which case leave it and treat the hot tumor until it heals, then resume the process of carefully removing the eyelid until healing occurs.
If the fold of skin (نتو) is causing severe harm to the eye, attach it with a hook and cut off some of it, but do not excessively cut to prevent excessive tearing. Then, apply red salve or zingari to the eyes until healing occurs.
Chapter XVII.
In the removal of the eye's redundant flesh and growths.
In some people, a red flesh may grow in the eyes, overlapping or almost covering the pupil, and sometimes overflowing onto the eyelids, which can turn inside out and resemble a mandrake flower.
The procedure involves the patient lying on their headrest while you open their eyes and use appropriate pincers to remove all of the red flesh.
You should hold it with a scalpel or forceps, cutting away the flesh in layers from the outside in until it is all removed, taking care to preserve the eye during the process. You can use either a scalpel described for cutting the nail or small scissors. as illustrated in the following figure.
After removing all the flesh, fill the eye with crushed salt or drop in some red soot (or other astringent medications) and then apply a clean cotton ball soaked in egg white from the outside to prevent excessive swelling.
If there remains something from the bleeding and the blood overcomes you, and you fear a hot tumor, then leave the eye and treat it with what calms the tumor, then continue with the operation until it heals, and do the same with any excess tissue that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating every weak eye with natural remedies or surgical intervention with iron, and do not put your hand into any of these treatments until you see that the organ is suitable for that treatment.
Chapter XVIII
On the treatment of eye problems.
The condition is red veins that are woven over the eye, obstructing vision and weakening it with the passage of time. You should first examine it: if the eye with the veins is strong and has no other illness besides the veins, then...
The treatment is as follows: instruct the patient to lay their head on your lap, then use a hook (or two, depending on your skill) to gently attach these veins. The hooks should be flexible and bent in this manner:...
or two hooks in a single body, as shown here:...
Then, using a delicate scissors, carefully snip the vessels and wipe away the blood from time to time until the eye perceives that the vessels have disappeared and the blood has drained. Be cautious with the eye, do not hurt it with the scissors' tips, and let your work be done in the afternoon facing the sun.
And be very careful in your work so as not to cut any vessels other than the ones you intend to, and if you finish, use a green or red eye dropper to consume what remains of the substance with its sharpness; if you cannot remove all of it at that time, dress the eye with something to alleviate the pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the swelling disappears, then continue with the treatment in the same manner until the eye heals completely.
Leave it [with the sick head resting on the student's lap, while another dries the tear ducts and checks the vision in the front and back of the eye, and you] are the main focus of the work to open the eye with fixed or triangular openings held by a student.
Cut carefully with the instrument, keeping the sick head resting on the student's lap, while another dries the tear ducts and checks the vision in the front and back of the eye. You have the option to begin cutting from either above or below the eye; it is better to dry two channels on the upper side, one at the end of the eyelid and the other at its beginning.
Grip it between the thumb and index finger, then attach pincers underneath. Hold it again between the ring and little fingers, and gently lift your hand to see if the tendon has risen. Cut from the smaller end with scissors or a delicate scalpel.
The surgeon inserts the needle through the tendon, piercing it completely so that it rises above the flesh. Do not cut the tendon, as the blood and moisture will spread upon you.
Then, cut with scissors what exceeds the tendon both above and below, so that nothing remains attached except for the bone. If you can lift it from the bone using the surgeon's tool, do so, as this is the best method. In some cases, it may come off easily.
If the cutting causes any pain or swelling, it should be left alone for a few days until the discomfort subsides, and then the procedure can be resumed.
Chapter XIX
In repairing the nose.
The physicians call this condition 'nasorrhoea'. If it does not heal with cauterization or sharp, burning medicines as described previously, the only remedy is to puncture the abscess when it has ripened and drain out all the moisture or pus within.
Until the bone is exposed, if it is revealed and you see corruption or blackness in it, remove it with this instrument, which is shaped like this:
The instrument called "al-khashnah al-ras" is made of Indian iron, and its head is rounded like a seed. It has a fine engraving, similar to that of a knife or a scalpel, which you place on the affected area of the bone. Then, you rotate it between your fingers while slightly tightening your hand until the corruption or blackness is completely removed.
You repeat this process several times, then apply drying and contracting medications to the wound. If it heals, new flesh grows, the flow of blood stops, and no further issues arise for forty days, the treatment is considered successful.
Know that it has healed. If not, there is no other remedy but to push the fistula back into the nostril, revealing the bone again with iron or a sharp medication. Once the bone is exposed, take a forceps shaped like this:
The end of the iron should be triangular, and its handle should be a cone-shaped protrusion, as you can see. Then place it on the bone itself, close to the nose and slightly away from the eye, and rotate your hand with the forceps until the bone is extracted and the patient feels it.
By having the patient hold their hand over their nose and feeling the air escaping, you can determine if the fistula is open. Then, apply drying and contracting medications as previously instructed. If the flesh grows back and hardens in place, the discharge that was previously coming out will return to the nostril, which will be lighter on the patient, God willing.
Chapter XX.
In the repair of the eye's net (or mesh).
If the eye's net (or mesh) is entirely detached, without causing any harm or loss of sight, it should be carefully loosened, then sutured in place, and finally a small needle should be inserted on the back of the eye without any specific condition.
Then, place on the eye a poultice made of poplar, fenugreek, frankincense, and myrrh, and secure it with many bandages. The method for preparing the bandages is to take soft lint, fold each piece into four parts, and thus create many of them.
Then, place them one upon the other, as much as the eye requires, and secure it with a strong cloth, reinserting the needle for suction without any condition, then remove the needle and leave the eye bound for a day and night.
If the bandage relaxes during this time, it should be tightened, and then, after a day and night as we said, if you find that the eye has returned to its place, otherwise, repeat the dressing, bandages, tightening, and suction until it returns.
Chapter XXI.
In Amputating the Grape (or Strawberry) Fistula.
If a fistula appears in the grape (or strawberry) layer, and then becomes gangrenous and protrudes outside the skin like a strawberry, distorting the human form, and you wish to amputate it, proceed as I describe.
Insert a needle from the base of the strawberry upwards, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the opening and pull it through, leaving the first needle in place.
Then, cut the bend of the doubled thread and tie a portion of the strawberry around the needle, pulling it tightly. Remove the needle and place a wet plaster of egg white over the wound, leaving it until the threads and strawberry detach. Then treat the eye with remedies to strengthen it.
Until the wound heals, tighten the strawberry from all sides with a circle of cloth to promote eye growth. Then, use a thin scalpel to access the depth of the eye, allowing egg white fluid to drain and the eye to descend and wilt slightly, before securing it until healed.
Chapter Twenty-Second.
On Treating Hemorrhoids.
This condition, known as the "coma," is merely a period in which the eye accumulates fluid resembling descending water but without it. The method of treatment involves having the patient sit on an upright chair and then holding their head with both hands from the sides.
You should move the head until you observe the period descending in your eye, then it stabilizes and does not disappear, and light is released, allowing the patient to see objects as they normally would. If the descent of the period does not occur as described, we know that it is not water; if it does not prepare for the descent as mentioned.
Otherwise, have the patient sit between your hands and then take a thin scalpel and make a slight cut above the cornea membrane where it connects to the sclera at the limbus, allowing the material to exit.
If it exits, pour warm water in the eye, mixed with honey or water boiled with fennel and honey, and then treat with all other remedies until healing occurs.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
As we mentioned the types of water in division and which one is suitable for the cup with detailed, explanatory speech, take it from there for health. Then, you should have the patient sit between your hands, squatting facing the light near the sun.
Secure and fasten the healthy eye, then raise the eyelid with your left hand if it is the water eye (left eye), or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the cautery near the edge of the cornea, with a thick rod resting on the white part of the eye, from the side of the smaller pupil, and push the cautery firmly while rotating it in your hand until it penetrates the white part of the eye, and you feel the cautery reach something hollow.
The depth to which the cautery goes should be proportional to the distance from the pupil to the edge of the cornea (the limbus), for copper will appear in the observer's field of vision due to the clarity of the cornea, and then the cautery should move upwards to the position where the water is.
Then press it down repeatedly; if the water begins to drain, the patient will see what their vision has revealed, and the cautery should remain in their eye. Then pause slightly. If the water rises again, lower it a second time without removing the cautery.
If it stabilizes and does not rise again, carefully remove the cautery while twisting your hand slightly, then dissolve a small amount of fine salt in water and wash the inside of the eye with it. Finally, apply a moist bandage or cloth over the entire eye.

Show them objects and then replace the bandage until the seventh day. You should not do this during treatment or after using your instrument, but rather avoid it until before the water rises quickly with intense looking.

Chapter XXIV.
In the treatment of flesh growths in the nose.
Various types of flesh growths can develop in the nose, some resembling legged scorpions, others being cancerous and hardened like a black mass, while some are soft and not discolored.
For the soft flesh that is neither malignant nor cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun with their nose open, and you should insert a hook into these growths, then pull it out and cut what has been reached using a delicate, sharp scalpel on one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone, if anything remains, you will not be able to cut it, so remove it with one of the delicate instruments gently until nothing is left. If the blood overpowers or a hot tumor appears.
If it is confronted with what is appropriate or it was a malignant tumor, then hurry to cut it off until the blood stops and all the flesh is gone, then pour into the nose afterward a vacuum or a drink, for if the nose opens and moisture flows from it to the throat.
Know that it has healed, if moisture does not flow as it should, know that there is flesh inside the bones that the cutting tool did not reach, in that case, you should take a thread of linen slightly thick.
And tie many knots in it, making each knot slightly thicker than a finger or less, and the patient inserts one end of the thread into his nose with a rod or what he can make, similar to a plug, and pulls it until it reaches the nostrils.
It comes out in a loop, and often young boys in schools do this, which is easy for anyone who wants to, then you gather the two ends of the thread, one that came out of the mouth and the other left in the nose.
Then you use the instrument to spread the flesh tied with the knots in the thread, doing this until you know that the flesh is cut by the knots in the thread, then remove the thread and clean the nose after wiping away the blood with a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment, do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh, then it becomes the last thing in the nose a lead pipe for some days until it heals, if it needs treatment, dryness is used for it.

Chapter Twenty-Fifth.
On the warts growing at the tip of the nose.
A wart often grows at the tip of the nose, increasing in size and becoming more disfiguring over time.
Therefore, it should be cut off as soon as it appears and all of it removed. Then, apply either cauterization or a burning medicine that acts like cauterization to the site. If it is impossible to cut it off before it grows large, examine it; if it has become hard and colorless with little sensation, then proceed accordingly.
Do not attempt to treat it with iron, for it is a cancerous tumor, and I have often seen great harm result from cutting into such a tumor. Even if the tumor feels soft and is not discolored, I believe that incision is still possible throughout its entirety. Then induce the patient to vomit and cut without caution or delay, and treat the wound with astringents and tonics until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In stitching the nose, lip, and ear. If the connection breaks away from a wound or similar injury, know that once any of these cartilages' connections break, little to no effective treatment can be done for it in most cases, except perhaps for certain individuals.
If one of these conditions arises, you should first see if the wound is still fresh and filled with blood. Then sew the edges together and treat it until it heals. If the connection has broken apart, leaving two distinct parts, you should carefully scrape each part to make it bleed, ensuring a stronger healing process.
Then sew the edges together, tighten them, and apply a mixture of myrrh and frankincense powder on top. Place a seal made from a paste of nettle ointment or another sealing balm over it. Leave it for two to three days before undoing the stitches.
Changing the dressing and leaving it until the stitches dissolve on their own, then treating it with a salve until healing occurs. The method of suturing involves bringing together what is separated either with needles as we described in the suture of the abdomen, or with thread as we mentioned there.
Chapter XXVII.
In removing knots that appear on the lips.
Many people may present you with small, hard tumors inside their lips that resemble sesame seeds or are smaller or larger, in which case you should turn the lip inside out, make a cut on each knot, and remove it with a hook.
Cut them off from all sides, then fill the incision site with powdered ash until the bleeding stops, and rinse with vinegar and salt. Treat the areas with constrictive agents until the wounds heal.
Chapter XXVIII.
On cutting excess flesh from the gums.
Excess flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients call "abulus." You should remove it with a hook or scrape it off at the base and allow the blood to flow, then apply powdered ash to the site.
If a dry and contractile cartilage returns to its original state after treatment, it often re-forms. In this case, cut it off again and cauterize it; for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In the inventory of teeth with iron.
Coarse, unpleasant scales may gather on the inner and outer surfaces of the teeth and between the gums. They may become discolored, turning black, yellow, or green, leading to gum inflammation and tooth deformity.
You should have the patient sit between your hands and head on your lap, then extract the tooth with scales or anything resembling sand, ensuring nothing remains. You should also do this for any blackness, greenness, yellowness, or other discoloration until it is purified.
If the scales do not disappear from the first inventory, repeat the process on a different day, and then again and again until you achieve your goal. Keep in mind that each tooth may require different techniques and shapes according to what is suitable for your work.
The instrument used to clean a tooth from the inside is different from that used for external cleaning and between the teeth. There are several types of instruments, each with its specific shape, that you should have ready.
Chapter Thirty
In the extraction of teeth.
One should treat the toothache with every means and not hesitate to extract it, for it is a noble substance, even if there is no other option but to remove it.
If the patient is determined to extract it, then it should be secured until the aching tooth heals. Often, the patient in pain is deceived and thinks that it is a healthy tooth, so they extract it, but the pain does not disappear until the diseased tooth is removed; we have witnessed this many times by the actions of barbers.
If the aching tooth is correctly identified, one should then use a scalpel with some force to loosen the gum from all sides, and subsequently move it slightly with their fingers or gentle pliers until it becomes loose.
Then, firmly grasp the tooth with both pincers, placing the patient's head between your knees for stability, and pull it straight without breaking it. If it does not come out, take one of the instruments, insert it under the tooth on all sides, and move it as you initially did.
If a tooth is hollow or decayed, it should be filled with a cloth and properly sealed with a thin rod to prevent its rupture during the application of forceps and clamps. The condition around the gums should be thoroughly examined on all sides.
Beware of your efforts, lest you break it and some remains, causing greater harm to the patient than their initial pain. Do not do what the ignorant surgeons do by cutting off parts without using the described methods.
Often, they inflict great harm on people; the least of which is that a tooth may break and its roots remain intact or partially so. The Thirty-Second Chapter.
In the removal of teeth that have grown crookedly among others.
If teeth grow crookedly deviating from their natural alignment, it distorts the beauty, especially in women and slaves. One should carefully examine if a tooth has grown behind another.
If it cannot be straightened or moved, and it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, whose shape is as follows:
It resembles a small beak, and it should be made of sharp, high-quality iron. Use it over several days to cut the tooth firmly, avoiding damaging nearby teeth. If the tooth is easily movable, cool it with a cold tool from India, whose shape is as follows:
It should be entirely made of India, with a very finely engraved handle, similar to the tool used for making needles. Cool the tooth gently over several days using this tool; do not rush, lest you damage surrounding teeth. Then smooth it and file it away with other tools.
If a tooth has broken, causing pain to the tongue when speaking, it should also be cooled until the roughness of the break goes away, becoming smooth and not hurting the tongue or impairing speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
In the interweaving of mobile teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the anterior teeth become unsteady and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient is unable to chew anything edible without risking further loss, having tried without success various astringent remedies.
The solution is to tighten a gold or silver thread, with gold being preferable as silver tarnishes and corrodes over time, while gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium softness and firmness, suitable for the space between the teeth.
The technique involves taking the thread and inserting its bend between the healthy teeth, then weaving both ends of the thread between the mobile teeth, one or more, until the weave reaches the healthy tooth on the other side.
Then, reweaving from the starting point, gently but firmly tighten your grip on the thread to ensure it does not move, tightening at the roots of the teeth to prevent slippage. Cut off any excess thread with scissors, gather and twist the ends together using a twine, and hide them between the healthy and mobile teeth.
To prevent damage to the tongue, it should then be left in this position, taut, for as long as it remains intact. If it becomes loose or detached, tighten it with another thread so that it may function properly for all time. This is an illustration of teeth and their intermeshing in two healthy teeth and two mobile teeth, as you can see:
A tooth or both teeth may sometimes return to their original position after falling out and intermesh as described, remaining in place. This is only possible with the skill of a skillful dentist, who may also carve a bone from a cow to create a tooth-like structure and insert it into the gap left by the missing tooth. It is tightened as we have said, remaining stable and functioning properly.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
In cutting the ligament exposed under the tongue, it prevents speech.
This ligament exposed under the tongue may either be a natural feature present since birth or a result of a wound that has healed.
The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth and placing their head in your lap, then raising their tongue and cutting the nerve ligament running across it horizontally until the tongue is released from its attachment. If there is some stiffness or complexity due to scar tissue from a previous wound, you may use a hook to pry it open and slice through the ligament vertically to completely release it.
Be cautious that the incision does not go too deep into the flesh, as it may cut a vessel and cause bleeding. After making the cut, rinse with rosewater or vinegar mixed with cold water. Then, place under the tongue a piece of cotton which the patient holds each night.
If it bleeds again, apply powdered glass to the wound. If blood persists, use a lens-shaped iron to suck out the blood. Then treat it with all other remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the removal of the toad formed under the tongue.
A tumor may develop under the tongue, resembling a small toad, preventing the tongue from its natural movements and possibly even swelling to fill the mouth. The treatment involves opening the patient's mouth towards the sun and examining the tumor.
If you see it as a black, hard lump that the patient cannot feel, do not interfere, for it is cancer. However, if it leans towards white and has moisture, insert a hook in it and cut it with a delicate knife from all sides to remove it.
If blood prevails while you are working, apply powdered glass until the blood stops, then resume your work to remove it completely. Afterwards, rinse your mouth with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with all appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter XXXVI.
On the treatment of tonsillar swelling.
And any growth in the throat, other than tonsil stones, that resembles tonsils within the throat, if it does not heal after being treated as mentioned in the sections, is called enlarged tonsils.
Examine it: if the tumor is solid, dark in color and not sensitive, do not treat it with iron. If it is red in color and its origin is thick, do not also treat it with iron to avoid bleeding. Leave it until it ripens; either it will subside on its own or it will burst from within.
If it is white in color, round, and its origin is thin, this is what should be cut. The action consists of examining it before treatment to see if the hot tumor has completely subsided or has decreased in size.
When ready, seat the patient facing the sun with their head in your lap. Open their mouth and place a servant between your hands, who will press the tongue downward using this instrument: [image or description follows]
The instrument should be made of silver or copper, thin as a knife. When pressing the tongue and observing the swelling, use a hook and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outward as much as possible without disturbing the surrounding tissue.
Then, cut it with this instrument, which resembles scissors but has both ends bent towards each other, extremely sharp. It is made of Indian iron or Damascus steel: [image or description follows]
If this instrument is not available, cut it with a scalpel: [image or description follows]
Sharp on one side and dull on the other, after cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, rinse the patient's mouth with cold water or a mixture of vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, rinse with boiling water mixed with pomegranate peel or assafoetida (or similar astringents) until the bleeding stops. Then treat as mentioned for tonsil issues. Similarly, any other growths in the throat, such as additional tumors, can be cut out using the same method described for tonsils.
A woman was treated for a tumour that had grown inside her throat, extending to the tonsil, which was only slightly sensitive and almost blocked the throat. The woman's breathing was restricted, and she was unable to eat or drink.
And if she had remained for one or two days, she would have died. The tumour had sent out two branches that emerged from her nostrils, so she acted quickly and inserted a hook into one of them, then pulled it out, and a good piece came off.
She cut it off at the point where she reached it from the nostril, then did the same for the other nostril, opened her mouth, pressed her tongue down, and inserted the hook into the tumour again, cutting off some of it, leaving only a little blood.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and eat food. For a long time, they continued to cut off the tumour, which kept regenerating until it affected both of them.
I tried and cauterized the tumour inside the throat, which prevented its growth. Then I left the region, not knowing what God had done with her after me.
Chapter Seven and Thirty.
In the removal of the tonsil tumour.
If a tumour, called an "abouta" (grape), descends to the tonsil and swells, becoming white, elongated, and rectangular, it is called a "column." If it is thicker at the bottom and round, it is called a "grapes," if treated as described in the classification, but without effect.
You should cut off the hot tumour if it has subsided and is thin, but avoid cutting off any that are rounded, compact, of a purplish or black color, or without sensation.
If you observe these characteristics—whiteness and elongation—you should have the patient sit facing the sun, press his tongue with the instrument described earlier, then insert the hook into the "grapes.
Pull it down and cut it with one of the instruments mentioned for removing the tonsil, but only remove what exceeds the natural amount; otherwise, you will harm the voice and speech.
After cutting, use the same method described for removing the tonsil and treat it until healing occurs. If the patient refuses to have it cut, apply heat using a sharp remedy by placing his head in your lap.
Then compress his tongue with the instrument mentioned, and take some sharp water as mentioned in the chapter on burning, and mix it with non-extinguish clay until it is neither thick nor thin, and fill the hollows of this instrument with it. This is its shape: .
The end of the instrument that holds the medicine has a hollow shape like a spoon, and you place the medicated instrument on the uvula while the patient lies on their side, allowing saliva to flow from their mouth into their throat without any foreign objects entering and causing harm.
Then grip the medicine with your hand and squeeze it onto the uvula for half an hour until it turns black and the sting of the medicine subsides. If you wish, take a cotton swab, wrap it around the end of the rod, dip it in the medicine, and apply it.
Insert the rod with the cotton swab into the tube from above, sticking the cotton onto the uvula. Repeat this process as many times as needed to achieve the desired burning effect on the grape. Leave it, and after three or four days, it will wilt and fall off. If required, reapply the medicine.
After the burning, wipe around the grape with a cotton swab soaked in butter and dry the surrounding area of medicine. Then rinse with cold water and apply external treatments with compresses and internal gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further away from fear.
Treating the throat may also be done more gently than with burning or cutting, using herbs such as fennel, za'far (saffron), thyme, dolichos, chamomile, and mugwort, either individually or in combinations, boiled in vinegar.
The pot is sealed with clay and placed over a fire, with a hole in the lid through which the hollow instrument is inserted. It is made of silver or brass.
The end of the instrument, containing the apple, is inserted into the patient's mouth, allowing the vapor to reach the throat. This process should be repeated several times until the throat is soothed and relaxed. Avoid using this treatment in the early stages of a tumor.
If the swelling increases, it is a sign of the heat in the tumor, and you should only proceed when the tumor has subsided. If you do not have the hollow instrument, take a tube and attach a slice of egg shell to its end to avoid burning the patient's mouth.
Because the egg shell prevents the hot vapor from burning the mouth, this is a good treatment for its safety and effectiveness.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
In the removal of thorns and anything lodged in the throat other than that.
Often, a bone, fishbone, or other foreign object becomes lodged in the throat. One should remove whatever is visible after pressing the tongue with an instrument at the sun's angle.
To determine what is in the throat, and what is not visible but hidden within it, one should induce vomiting in the patient before their food has been digested in the stomach, for perhaps the lodged object will emerge through vomiting or the patient might swallow a piece of string or a fragment of food.
or they might swallow a dry piece of bread or take a piece of soft seaweed, tie it to a thread, and swallow it. When it reaches the location of the bone, quickly pull on the thread, doing this several times.
Often, the bone or fishbone becomes stuck and can be extracted by these means. If it does not come out as mentioned above, use an instrument made of lead in the following shape: .
It should be slightly thicker than a rod and have a curved end that the patient inserts into their throat gently while lifting their head, taking care not to touch their throat to avoid coughing. The doctor can also use their hand to insert it.
The insertion of the sick person is better for his knowledge of the location of the embedded object, and it pushes or pulls his hand with the tool to the desired position until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
In the removal of a lodged phlegm from the throat.
If the treatment of the phlegm with the mentioned remedies does not succeed, look then at the patient's throat in the sunlight after pressing his tongue with the tool described to you.
If you spot the phlegm, draw it with a small hook or a delicate, firm forceps. If that is not possible, take an evacuated tube, insert it into the patient's throat near the phlegm, and then insert a heated, protected iron rod into the tube.
Do this several times, and have the patient refrain from drinking water for the entire day. Then, he should take a glass filled with cold water, open his mouth, swish it around, not swallow any of it, and occasionally move the water with his hand.
If the phlegm falls onto the throat when it feels the water, and if it does not come out as described, then dry the throat with smoke from a burnt piece of gum arabic using the tool mentioned for drying the tonsils. Do this several times, and it will fall.
The method of working with incense: Take a vessel in which there is hot coals from a fire, and the vessel has a cover with a hole in the middle. Assemble the end of the instrument into that hole, then throw the incense and have the patient place his mouth at the end of the tube and close his mouth so that the incense does not escape until he knows that the incense has reached his ring.
If the phlegm does not come down, repeat the incense and make the patient endure thirst, eat salt and garlic, and do not drink water; in this way, it must certainly come out through this treatment. This is a picture of an instrument that attracts the phlegm from the throat if one looks at it.
It resembles a hook as you can see, except it has this bend that enters the throat, and both ends resemble a bird's beak with its rough sawtooth; if one presses on something, it does not let go.
Another instrument compresses the tongue, which is a wide ring of iron that fits across the mouth above the tongue; it is inserted into the mouth, leaning on its side, keeping the mouth open against the patient's will and he cannot remove it at all until it is moved away.
The instrument that opens the mouth, designed precisely for this purpose: a metal ring of specific width that fits comfortably over the tongue and within the mouth, leaning on one side to keep the mouth open without the patient's conscious effort.
Chapter Forty.
On the discussion of the speech regarding the formation, treatment, and incision of tumors.
The types of tumors are numerous and varied, as mentioned one by one in this book. They differ in their shell (بطّ) and incision (شقّ) according to two factors: the type of tumor itself and what it contains in terms of moisture.
The second type of distinction is based on the locations where they occur in the body, as a tumor in the head differs from one in the abdomen, and a tumor in a hot spot (لحمى) varies from one in a joint, each with its own rules regarding treatment.
Some tumors should not be incised until the pus within has ripened and reached its fullness, while others require incision even if the purulent matter is not yet fully formed, such as tumors close to joints.
Because if a tumor forms near a joint and its condition persists until it putrifies the surrounding areas, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, leading to a prolonged disability of that body part, or the tumor might be close to a vital organ.
If you delay the incision until the pus ripens, it may cause harm to a vital organ or affect an area near the abdomen, making it difficult to incise (بطّ) even though the tumor is not yet fully formed. Therefore, it is necessary to incise tumors that are close to the abdomen while they are still in an unripened state, lest they putrify and spread internally, becoming a fistula.
When a tumor does not heal, one must know the time when it has matured completely. This is when the pain of the tumor ceases, fever subsides, redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor hardens, and other signs appear.
If possible, the bandage should be placed at the base of the tumor to facilitate the flow of the discharge downwards, or in the thinnest part of the tumor with the most distinct contour. If the tumors are located on the hands or feet, the bandage should extend along the length of the body.
And one should place the bandage around muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, in general, in all flat positions with no curves. However, for those with bends, the bandage should follow accordingly. If the tumor is in fleshy areas, it is better to leave it uncovered.
Until it has completely matured as we have said, one should not bandage it too early, for then the discharge of pus will be abundant and harmful, and its stench may become stuck, and sometimes the tumor's edges may harden and its depth may narrow. Some tumors may be bandaged across the body if necessary or according to what is required by the organ.
For small tumors, a single bandage should be used, while for large tumors, a wide bandage or several cuts should be made according to the tumor's size. Some tumors may require the skin to be scraped and cut if they have become like a cloth.
And it becomes, in a manner, as if it were dead, as is often the case in many ganglions and abscesses, some of which are incised in three angles, and others have part of them cut off like the shape of a palm leaf.
And some use a round incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and other types of incisions; and if it has no head, like a smooth, flat tumor, it should only be bathed simply. If the tumor is large and has collected pus over a long period, do not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Instead, remove some of it and then compress the tumor until another day, then remove some more pus, repeating this process gradually until all of it is out.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a very young child, or an old person, the animal spirit (vital force) often dissolves with each discharge of pus; so much so that the patient might die without your realizing it. Be extremely careful in this matter.
After compressing these tumors, clean the wound and examine it; if the tumor's wound is small or the incision simple, use old linen or cotton string. If the tumor is large and the compressions numerous, consider other methods.
Then, insert a string in each incision until they connect; if the tumor has detached some skin or formed an abscess, stuff it with old cotton or linen beard without moisture and compress it for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a piece of cotton fabric in them and apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should employ the measures, treatments, and applications described in many places within this book and its sections. If it is winter and the area with the swelling has many nerves, you should soak bandages in a hot drink and oil.
Place them on the area. If it is summer and the conditions are hot, you should soak the bandages in cold water and oil or a drink and oil, as mentioned. This should be done for three days, after which the swelling should be drained and cleaned.
Treat it with similar things until it heals. This is all the knowledge needed to treat tumors in general, and each type of tumor has been described previously in detail, summarized in a clear manner.
Chapter Forty-One.
On the incision of tumors that occur on the scalp.
There are small tumors on the scalp, which belong to a category of conditions called "surlins," enclosed in bags that have specific properties, similar to a chicken's ovary. There are many types, some are fatty and others contain moisture like mud.
Some have a moisture similar to wool or porridge, and others are solid and rocky. All of them pose no danger in terms of incising and extracting them, unless a vessel (artery) stands in the way during incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument depicted below, known as a drill, to determine what they contain. If they hold moisture, make an incision along their length with a simple cut, as illustrated.
Begin the incision from line ~B~ to line ~C~. Once the moisture has drained, peel and completely remove the sac containing that moisture, leaving no part of it untouched, as it often returns if any remains.
Then, dip a cotton swab in pure honey (if available), or alternatively in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the following day, as it will consume any remaining sac tissue. Repeat the process of applying the cotton swab with honey a second and third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that no part of the sac remains, then treat the wound with remedies until it heals. If the mass contains fatty tissue, incise it as illustrated.
Make two perpendicular incisions and use forceps to pull the edges of the wound apart. Peel it from all sides, and strive to remove the peritoneum that contains it. If a vessel (artery) stands in the way, follow the instructions provided for you after this, and do the same for a hard mass.
The treatment, as mentioned, is specific, and the incision on the solidified tumor is easier because it contains little blood or moisture. Some of these tumors in the head in certain people have no moisture at all, so much so that when I incise a tumor, there is no fluid to drain.
On the head of an elderly woman, I found a tumor as hard as a solid stone, rough and white, so firm that it could not be broken, even if someone threw a blow at it. Such tumors are similar to those that occur on the heads of children.
At the bases of the ears, make a simple incision through all of them and ensure the flap is from below, allowing the discharge to flow downward. Then treat it with the appropriate method.
Chapter Forty-Two
In the incision on pigs that occur in the neck, these tumors often appear in the neck, under the armpits, and in the groins, and they can also appear elsewhere on the body. Some may be solitary, while others are numerous, and some may develop from one another.
Each of these tumors has a separate membrane, similar to the membranes surrounding organs in the body, as is the case with tumors inside the skull, as we described earlier. There are many types of these pigs; some are solid, others contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
What I observed of its condition upon examination was good; it appeared similar to skin in color and moved in all directions, not attached to the neck's nerve, tendon, artery, or depressed. It is necessary to make a simple incision from above to below the body, following this line (~J~) to (~B~):
And peel it from all sides, expanding the skin's lips with a hook or hooks or many hooks if necessary, as we mentioned regarding tumors of the head. Gradually extract it, leaving no vein or nerve cut on the neck, and let the blade be not too sharp.
To prevent your hand from making additional cuts or disturbing the patient, do not cut what you do not need to cut. If you cut a vein or artery and are hindered from continuing, apply powdered glass or some fine grit to stop the bleeding.
Bandage the wound and leave it until the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes, and it is about to putrefy. At that time, the blood will stop flowing, then return to your work until you are finished. Then, check with your index finger if any remain, small last pieces, cut them off and clean them.
If there is a large vein in the root of the pig, do not cut off that small pig from its root, but rather tie it with a double thread tightly and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm. Then fill the wound with old cotton that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment and treat it.
If the entire pig is cut, one should gather the edges of the wound and sew it up immediately after realizing that no flesh remains, except for the bone of the pig.
One should aim to cut through the flesh and bone, using stitching as mentioned. For pig parts that retain moisture, apply a simple bandage where it appears healed, and ensure the bandage is placed below as advised.
Then, use a twist of Egyptian ointment or similar to cover what remains of the corruption. Once you see the wound has healed, treat it with flesh-regenerating ointments until it is fully cured.
Chapter 34.
In the incision of the throat, a tumor occurring within the pharynx.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen it practiced in our land, and this is their manner of speaking. As for those suffering from strangulation, they should avoid the incision in the throat, as it is not beneficial to them.
In order that all the veins and lung are not damaged, and for those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no issue with the trachea, the incision in the throat should be used to escape the damage that may occur from strangulation.
If we wish to do so, we should make three incisions in the larynx below the rings of the trachea, or four small transverse incisions between two rings, as deep as the incision in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This location corresponds to the incision.
Since it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant, if the physician is timid, he should stretch the throat skin with a hook and cut the skin until it reaches the trachea, avoiding blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should make an incision in the peritoneum that we described, and he can determine the location of the tracheal incision from the mucus that comes out with it, and from the loss of voice and leaving the wound open for a while. Once the time when suffocation was feared passes, he should close the wound by bringing together the edges of the skin.
He should then operate only on the cartilage-free part, and use drugs to promote tissue regeneration until healing occurs. According to the author of this book, when they observe that the patient's throat is blocked by one of the mentioned tumors, this is what the preceding explanation refers to.
When the patient is on the brink of death, and he himself feels that he cannot breathe, they should make an incision in the larynx so that the patient can breathe through the wound, thus saving him from death. They are instructed to keep the wound open until the illness subsides, which may take around three days or so.
When they saw that, they ordered the wound to be stitched and treated until it healed. I have personally witnessed a servant taking a knife and cutting through part of her lung with it; she was then called for treatment, and upon examination, I found her bleeding heavily as if she were being slaughtered. I exposed the wound.
I found that the blood coming from the wound was thin, which convinced me that she had not cut any arteries or veins, and air was exiting through the wound. I then cleaned the wound and treated it until it healed, and nothing else happened to the servant except a slight sound when breathing.
After several days, she recovered completely, which is why we say that cutting the throat poses no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four
On the incision of the tumor that is exposed in the pharynx from the outside and is called the "pharyngeal fistula.
This tumor, called the pharyngeal fistula, is a large growth that resembles the patient's skin tone and is more common in women. It has two types.
It can either be natural or accidental. The natural one is beyond any control, while the accidental one has two types: one resembling fatty tissue and the other a tumor that forms from arterial complications, both of which pose a risk upon incision. It is not appropriate to expose them with iron at all, except for what is small if you explore and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles fatty tissue and is not attached to any vessels, then cut it as you would cut merchandise. Remove the content from the sac if it is in one, or thoroughly investigate and then treat the area as appropriate.
Chapter Forty-Five
On cutting according to the types of merchandise.
The types of merchandise are numerous, and all have been mentioned in the classification. Here, we must distinguish between merchandise and extractions, which is complex. I say that extractions are characterized by heat, fever, and severe pain until the excess fluid subsides. And then, the abscess matures, and the fever and severity subside. Unlike extractions, merchandise is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain. It is enclosed in a sac of serous membrane, which serves as its special casing, resembling the color of the body. Its onset is like a pea, and it expands to the size of a duck egg or larger.
They are of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture vary, as mentioned in the classification. When treating merchandise, it is essential to probe and examine it first with a tool called a probe (as depicted in the following chapter).
Estimate the size of the tumor, then remove the probe and observe what comes out as a result. If it is an exudate, regardless of color, make a simple incision as mentioned for other tumors. If no exudate comes out after the probe, know that it is fatty.
Make a cross-incision on it as you were taught, using this figure, and hang it with pincers. Gently peel the skin from all sides, taking care to preserve the sac if possible so that it can be removed intact along with the tumor.
If the sac ruptures during the procedure and you are unable to remove it intact, this often happens, so cut it into pieces until nothing remains. For if any part of it is left behind, whether small or large, the wound will tend to become infected.
If you are unable to remove it entirely and only a small part remains, thoroughly clean the wound with some sharp, consumable abrasives, close the incision, and apply hot compresses as well as other remedies until healing occurs. If the tumor is large, draw lines around its edges to seal them and treat it with adhesive remedies.
If you encounter a pulsating or non-pulsating vein and bleeding occurs, quickly apply powdered ash to the area, keeping it tight for two or three days until the wound becomes gangrenous and the blood flow stops. Then return to cutting away what remains of the tumor.

Chapter LXXIV.
In the treatment of men's breasts.
Which, resembling the breast of women, may become swollen in some people at the age of puberty, resembling the breast of women, and forming an unsightly bulge, those who dislike it should make a half-moon shaped incision on the breast.
From line ~B~ to line ~C~: Then, remove all the fat, and fill the wound with a mixture of salt and medicine, then close the edges of the wound with stitching, and treat it until it heals; if the breast tilts downwards and relaxes due to its bone structure, as is the case for women.
Then, make two incisions on its upper sides in the shape of a half-moon, connecting at their ends, so that the larger line encircles the smaller one as shown. From line ~B~ to line ~C~, then peel the skin between the incisions, remove the fat, and use the stitching and bandages as mentioned, along with any necessary treatment, until it heals.
If you fail to discern what needs to be cut in order to alleviate the patient's distress or prevent excessive blood loss, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in a consumable ointment and leave it until the remaining fat is consumed, after which you may treat it until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Eight
In the case of tumors under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the type known as "swine" (hard, containing a sac), and some contain moisture. In such cases, you should make a semi-circular incision in the shape shown here.
From line "B" to line "C," once you have drained all the contents from the tumor using cotton soaked in a suitable agent, leave it for a day or two before removing the cotton.
Treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the type known as "swine" (hard, containing a sac), make an incision as we described for swine tumors; if the wound continues to discharge pus, apply heat treatment as mentioned earlier.
Chapter Forty-Nine
In the incision of the tumor that is exposed by the artery or vein and is called aneurysm.
If the artery is wounded and the skin above it heals, a tumor often develops from it; likewise, a vein may exhibit swelling and a tumor.
And the signs by which it is known whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or a vein: if the tumor is from an artery, it is rectangular and located deeply in the body; if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel a creaking sensation.
And if it is from a vein, the tumor is round on the surface of the body; the incision on these tumors is dangerous, especially those located in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many places on the body, and they are very great in size, so one should avoid treating them with iron.
And what concerns those in the limbs or head, one should avoid them; for if it is a swelling of an artery, make an incision along its length in the skin, then open the slit with forceps, remove the artery from the adhesions.
Until it is exposed, then insert an needle under it and penetrate it to the other side, tighten the artery with a doubled thread at two points as I have taught you for the arteries in the neck; then use a scalpel to knock on the spot between the bands until all the blood comes out of it.
When the tumour dissolves, the treatment that produces pus is then used until the bands (or ligaments) fall off, after which they are treated with appropriate ointments until healing occurs.
If the tumour is located next to a vein, hold as much of the tumour and skin in your hand as possible, then insert an needle below that point and push it through, with a doubled thread attached, until you exit from the opposite side. Then, tie the tumour securely as previously described when tying a grape, using both ends of the thread. If you are concerned that the threads will slip, insert another needle with a new thread beneath the entire tumour at the point where the first needle crossed.
Tighten your threads at four points, then make a cut in the middle of the tumour. Once the contents have been extracted, cut away any excess skin and apply a dressing soaked in a mixture of wine and oil. Then use the treatment involving twisting and ointments until healing occurs.
Chapter Fifty
On tumours that expose nerve twisting.
As the tumor appears in the artery and vein, so it appears in the nerve if a blow occurs or results from excessive fatigue, and so on, and this is most common in the wrists and ankles.
In every location where joints move, there is a firm tumor that resembles the color of the rest of the body. In most cases, it is painless. If pressed strongly, the patient feels a numbness similar to paralysis, and the tumor is not concentrated in deep tissues.
On the contrary, it is located under the skin and moves in all directions, neither advancing nor retreating. As for what is found in joints, it should not be treated with iron, as it may cause permanent damage. And what is found in the head or forehead should not be subjected to such treatment.
Make a cut in the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, use a tweezers to grasp and cut it from the root. If it is large, hang it on a hook and peel it off, then remove it and close the wound with stitches and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty
On cutting moles that appear on the stomach.
They may often appear on some people's stomachs and other parts of their bodies as moles called fungous because they resemble fungi in shape. Their base is thin, but their head is thick. Sometimes their edges turn inward, and they can vary in size, from very small to extremely large.
And I have seen a man with two growths in his abdomen, similar to mushrooms, with no distinction between them; their bases were thin and their edges had turned inward and fissured, and moisture was constantly seeping from them. So I cut them off, and weighed one at eighteen ounces and the other at six or seven ounces.
In performing the cut, observe: if the patient is moist and the tumor white, damp, and thin in origin, then cut it with a broad blade; be prepared with hot irons, as blood often flows profusely upon cutting.
If blood flows excessively, apply a hot iron. If the patient is timid and frightened of having the tumor cut with iron, take a fine metal thread, tighten it around the tumor, and leave it for two days; then increase the tension on the thread until the tumor detaches.
Whenever the tumor is cut and detaches on its own without difficulty, this is a good sign; however, if it is thick in origin, cutting it may be risky, especially when located in the abdomen. It may be possible to cut off half or some part of it.
Then apply a hot iron to prevent it from returning; then treat the wound until it heals. Be cautious when cutting a tumor that is pale in color, lacking sensation, and unappealing in appearance, as it may be a cancerous growth, which we will discuss later.
Chapter LXXV.
In the treatment of navel hernia.
A navel hernia may result from various causes, such as a tear in the peritoneum (the membrane lining the abdominal cavity) allowing the omentum (a fold of abdominal fat and tissue) or intestine to protrude, similar to other types of hernias. It can also be caused by blood from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned, or due to excessive gas accumulation within the hernia.
If the hernia is due to a tear in the peritoneum allowing the omentum or intestine to protrude, the swelling will resemble the body's natural tissues, be soft without pain, and change its position. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, its position and characteristics will differ as described. If you press it with your fingers, it will disappear then return, and it may be accompanied by borbing sounds and become significantly larger during defecation, indicating a possible fecal impaction. If caused by moisture (or fluid), it will be soft.
If pressing it with your hand does not make it disappear, its size does not change, and it doesn't become more pronounced or smaller, then it is likely caused by blood. In this case, the hernia will appear black, indicating bleeding. If due to growing tissue (or scar tissue), the swelling will be firm, consistent in size, and not easily reducible.
If it is caused by the wind, its touch will be gentle. The procedure is to observe: if the hernia is due to arterial or venous blood or wind, one should refrain from treating it, as there is fear of complications and uncertainty, as I have explained in the chapter on tumors caused by arteries and veins.
If the hernia is due to the intestines or fat, one should order the patient to hold himself firmly and stand upright, then trace around the navel with a pen. Then, you should order the patient to lie down on his back between your hands, and cut around the navel with a broad blade where you have marked with ink. Then, pull the middle of the hernia upward with a large forceps, and tie the cut edge tightly with a strong thread or catgut.
The tie should be a simple knot. Then, open the hernia sac and insert your index finger to explore. If the intestine is caught in the knot, loosen the knot and push the intestine back into the abdominal cavity. If it is fat (throb), use forceps to extend it.

However, we must mention the procedure as described by the ancients: The patient should sit on a high chair, then press the anthema (gland) downward while holding the skin of the testicle with your fingers, along with the vessels close to the penis.

Secure it at the beginning and end of the affected area, then make a straight incision down the middle of the body and remove the accumulated foul moistures.
Then treat the fistula as other wounds that require a period of time for the vessels supplying one of the ovaries to fall, which is not harmful. If the fistula affects all vessels, one ovary should be removed along with the vessels, otherwise the egg will lack nourishment. By cutting the vessels, it wilts and becomes useless.
Chapter 65.
In the treatment of intestinal adenoma.
This adenoma occurs due to an incision that exposes the peritoneum extended across the abdomen towards the groins, causing the intestine to protrude from this hernia into one of the ovaries.
This hernia can either be due to a tear in the peritoneum or its extension, and these types are caused by numerous reasons such as a blow, a jump, a shout, or lifting a heavy object, etc. Its sign is that if it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, it occurs gradually over a long period of time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the tumor is level towards the depth before the peritoneum compresses the intestine. Its indication is that if it arises from a tear in the peritoneum, it begins with a severe, sudden pain, and the tumor appears distinct beneath the skin.
If the intestine protrudes and exits the peritoneum, it may carry with it adhesions, and this is called an adherent or a putrid intestine. It can also contain feces that accumulate, leading to the patient's demise.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially if pressed, treating this condition with iron is dangerous, and one should be cautious of such practices. The treatment involves instructing the patient to push the intestine back into its cavity if possible.
Then, the patient lies on their back between your hands and raises their legs. Next, you stretch the skin below the testicles upwards and cut the scrotal sac lengthwise. You then insert hooks into the edges of the incision as needed to keep it open.
The incision should be deep enough to allow the egg to exit. Then, you strip the layers beneath the scrotal skin until the white, tough peritoneum is exposed from all sides. At this point, insert your index finger into the space behind the testis.
Between the white peritoneum beneath the tunica of the testis and the second peritoneal layer, there is a adhesion behind the testis. It is then bent with the right hand towards the interior of the tunica, yet the white peritoneum extends upwards with the left hand, lifting the testis along with the peritoneum to the side of the incision.
The servant is commanded to extend the testis upwards, and you release the adhesion behind it completely. Inspect with your finger to ensure there is no twisted bowel in the rigid white peritoneum. If you find any, push it back into the abdomen below.
Then, take a needle with a thick thread twisted from ten strands and insert it at the end of the peritoneum beneath the tunica of the testis, next to the incision. Then, cut the ends of the looped thread so that there are four strands. Then, arrange them one on top of the other in a crossed manner and tie the peritoneum beneath the tunica of the testis with a strong ligature from both sides. Also, twist the thread ends and tie them tightly so that nothing can reach the vessels supplying them. To prevent any hot swelling and also to create a second ligature outside the first, away from it by less than two fingers, after these ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the tunica of the testis equal in size to a finger.
To prevent any hot swelling and also to create a second ligature outside the first, away from it by less than two fingers, after these ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the tunica of the testis equal in size to a finger. Cut off the remaining tissue in a rounded manner, along with the egg, and then make an incision below the scrotal sac, through which blood and pus will drain, as we have described previously. Afterwards, apply the wool soaked in oil to the wound and use the bandage that we have mentioned.
And the white peritoneum, after it is cut with caution to prevent blood loss, often poses a challenge. Leave the bandage until it falls off on its own. If it does not fall quickly, use hot water to soak it and help it slip off.
Then treat the wound with all the treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter 66.
In this intestinal hernia, I have never seen anyone dare to treat it with iron. The pioneers mentioned that one should proceed as we described for the hernia accompanied by a goiter, tying the vessels after making an incision on them gently below.
