Chapter 76.
In the hernia that occurs in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may expose itself as we have mentioned, and nothing from the intestine descends into the female part; if it does descend, it is slight and always returns.
However, if time passes, the hernia in the peritoneum increases, allowing the intestine and fat to descend into the scrotum, which is a result of the extended peritoneum in the groin as previously mentioned. This is because the peritoneum extends, relaxes, and then shrinks, causing the hernia to protrude and twist, which can be treated with heat as previously described.
It can also be treated with iron, following this method: the patient lies on their back between your hands, you make a vertical cut in the protruding hernia area, about three fingers wide, then separate the peritoneum layers beneath the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath it is exposed, take a strip of cloth, place it on the hernia opening, press it deep into the abdomen, then sew the two edges together, binding them with stitches.
Then take the edge of the cloth, without cutting the peritoneum or touching the testicle or any other structure, as I have taught you for treating intestinal hernias, and treat it with surgical methods.
If the stitches break, clean them and repair the wound until it heals; then the peritoneum will contract and the hernia will not increase in size.
And this method is better at this point because it is closer to safety.
Chapter LXXVI.
In the relaxation of the scrotal skin.
The scrotal skin often relaxes in some people without the muscles inside relaxing, which makes it look ugly. Therefore, when one desires to treat this condition, the patient should be put to sleep on his back.
He should cut all the relaxed skin over the thick skin, then gather the folds, and if he wishes to sew first, he can sew three or four stitches through the relaxed skin and secure them. Then he cuts between the stitches.
Then treat it like any other wound until it heals, and the stitches will fall out.
Chapter LXXXVI.
On castration.
In our religion, castration is forbidden.
Therefore, I have refrained from mentioning it in this book, but I have included it for two reasons: one, so that physicians are aware of it if asked and can offer a remedy to those who encounter it; the second is that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
As with lambs, goats, cats, and similar animals, I say that castration can be performed in two ways: by pressing or by cutting and severing. In the case of pressing, we make the animal sit in hot water until its female organs relax, soften, and hang down.
Then, you should massage it with your hand until it softens and becomes unnoticeable upon touch. For castration by cutting, you must hold the animal and, using your left hand, grip the scrotum, then tie the testicles off and make a single cut on each testicle.
Once the testicles are exposed, cut them after having removed the outer covering, leaving only the thin membrane surrounding the vessels intact. This method of castration is superior to that by pressing.
Because pressing might leave some of the female organs intact, causing the animal to desire copulation, then you should treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of hermaphroditism.
In men, hermaphroditism appears in two types: one where it manifests as a space or an opening between the testicles, resembling the vulva of a woman, and sometimes there is hair growth and urine may flow from this opening in the scrotum.
In women, there is one type, where it appears above the vulva in the groin, resembling a small penis extending outside, like the male genitalia, and both are female in nature. Treatment for all three types.
For both types of men and the single type of woman, the excess flesh should be cut off to remove its effects, then treated like any other wound until healing occurs. However, for the second type of man from whom urine flows through the scrotum, there is no operation possible, and it does not heal on its own.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the amputation of the clitoris and the flesh protruding from the vagina of women.
The clitoris may grow beyond the normal size, becoming distorted and ugly, and in some women it can become so enlarged that it resembles the male organ, leading to sexual activity.
It is necessary to hold the clitoris with your hand or a forceps and cut it, without exceeding in cutting, especially at the depth of its base, lest there be bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical treatment until it heals. As for the flesh that grows, it is flesh that grows inside the womb, filling it.
And it may protrude outward, similar to a toe. Therefore, some early scholars call it the "clitoris disease," and you should cut it as you cut the clitoris, treating it with surgical care until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On the treatment of epiploic hernia.
Epiploic hernia is when a woman's vagina is not perforated or the opening is very small, either naturally born that way or from an accidental cause. The accidental cause may be due to a surplus of flesh or other conditions.
It can be a thin or thick peritoneum, located either deep within the uterus, or along its sides, or at the top or bottom, preventing intercourse, conception, and childbirth. It may also halt menstruation. This is determined by an experienced midwife's examination.
If the obstruction is visible, examine it with your fingers or a speculum. If it is an obstructing perineum membrane, close to the labia, quickly make incisions and place your thumbs on both hands.
For a woman lying on her back, spread her legs and stretch the labia to puncture the thin perineum membrane, allowing the obstruction to open. Then take a woolen cloth, soak it in oil, and apply it to the area. Continue sexual intercourse with the woman daily to prevent the opening from sealing again.
If the perineum membrane is thick and tough, use a broad scalpel similar to a palm leaf to cut it. If the obstruction is from grown tissue, use pincers to grasp and cut it. Have at hand medications to stop bleeding without causing pain, such as fennel, hyssop, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube to prevent the wound from closing quickly. Hold it for several days and apply a dry linen string as a dressing, treating the area with all appropriate remedies to promote healing. If another meat-like growth appears in the womb, cut it following the same method.
Unless it is a benign tumor, a cancerous growth in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
In the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red sores that occur in the female genital area.
As for hemorrhoids, they are an inflammation of the veins' openings such that they bleed constantly, and if they present over time, they become warts. We have mentioned in detail their types, symptoms, and here we mention them.
What is curable and what is not: If hemorrhoids and warts are located deeply within the uterus and do not cause sensation, they should not be treated with iron. However, those that are in the uterine opening and cause a sensation are the ones to be treated.
A woman should enter a cold house, then use a scalpel or rough cloth to remove the warts, cutting them back to their roots. Then, she should apply something that causes bleeding from one of the cut ends without causing pain.
Like turpentine, turmeric, gum, and similar substances, then raise her legs against a wall for a while, dip them in cold water. If the bleeding persists, sit her in a mixture of sumac, pomegranate peel, and galgal, then take barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar.
If the blood stops, bind it on her back. Otherwise, fasten the ribs and breasts without a specific condition. Then take a piece of wool and immerse it in a mixture of pomegranate seeds, or date palm juice, or sumac branches with a drink made from gall. Apply it to the wound.
If the tumor subsides, treat the area with a ointment until it heals. As for the red pustule, it resembles the heads of hair, rough to the sight, so you should cut what is visible from it, just as you would with warts, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter LXXIV.
On the fallopian tube that appears in the uterus.
It may appear in the uterus with various types of tumors, such as cancer, fibrosis, ulcers, abscesses, erysipelas, fistulas, hemorrhoids, cracks, warts, and hot tumors. All these conditions and their types have been described in detail in the previous sections.
In this article, we should mention the hot tumor that appears in the uterus if it is one of the tumors that persist for a long time, how its fallopian tube is lined with iron. You should examine it; if the pain of the tumor is acute at the beginning with palpitations, inflammation, and fever.
If it is red and the sensation persists, do not hurry to bind it. Treat it with what aids maturation. Once these symptoms subside, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean back.
Bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, spread her thighs, and place her arms under her knees, binding them with a suitable bandage.
The midwife should then sit on the right side and use the instrument to dilate the cervix, whose image is depicted later. When using this instrument, one must assess the depth of the woman's uterus to avoid inserting more than necessary.
If the instrument extends beyond the depth, it may harm the patient. In this case, padding should be placed on the cervix to prevent further intrusion. The screw within the instrument should be turned from the upper side.
As you hold the instrument, the server turns the screw to dilate the cervix. If an effusion appears and feels soft when touched, it should be incised at its base with a broad blade once the discharge has ceased.
A soft plug, immersed in rose fat or green oil with some constriction, should be inserted into the wound. The plug protrudes from the cervical crack and is covered with pure wool soaked in boiling water mixed with bread dough.
Then it is treated after two days with the ointment that is suitable for it until it heals.
The womb and the wound may be washed by infusing water and honey, then water in which the root of fennel or long anise has been boiled, and finally return to the ointment, if the abscess is hidden internally within the womb.
One should abstain from treating it with iron, but rather treat it as we have mentioned in the categorization.
Chapter LXXV.
In instructing midwives on how to treat live fetuses that have emerged in an abnormal form.
The midwife should first know the signs of normal childbirth, which include when the woman feels a need to push downwards and desires fresh air, and finds the labor process easier despite postnatal conditions.
The speedy delivery of the fetus indicates a normal birth, where it emerges with its head first, and the placenta either accompanies it or is attached to the umbilical cord. If these signs are observed, the midwife should gently press on the mother's abdomen to facilitate a swift delivery.
If the foetus descends head first, the placenta follows, and this is a good sign. Any other presentation is abnormal and to be condemned, as the fetus may emerge with its legs first, followed by its hands, before its head and legs.
If it extends one hand or leg, or if its head and hand emerge first, or if it curls up or turns over on its back, or assumes any other such abnormal position, the midwife must be skilled and delicate in dealing with all these situations and avoid any mistakes.
I will describe each shape and the method to recognize and manage it, if the fetus emerges head first in a natural position but the woman experiences difficulty and her strength weakens, I recommend seating her on a chair while other women support her.
Moisturize the mother's womb with cooked fennel and wet oils, then the midwife inserts a small knife between her fingers, cuts the placenta, or uses her fingernail to make a slit, allowing the moisture within to flow out, and press on the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If the fetus does not descend, the midwife should apply a mixture of fennel saliva and oil of syrig (or sherbet) to the mother, then instruct her to sneeze gently while holding her nose and mouth closed. This is said to prompt the fetus's descent. If the hands emerge first, they should be carefully guided back inside the womb, little by little.
If the baby does not come out, place the woman on a bed and raise her legs above, then shake the bed on the ground while she holds on to prevent falling. If the hands do not deliver the baby and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the remaining fetus.
If the baby is positioned with its legs first, tie the mother's hands with a cloth and gently pull them, and the baby should come out. If the baby comes out on its feet, you should raise it slightly and then turn it slowly to align it, before taking one of its legs and extending it gradually.
If they come out to the thighs, order her to squirm and blow on it with a quill, and it will come out. If it does not come out as described, turn the fetus slightly until it takes its natural form, and then it will come out smoothly. If all else fails.
Take some of the mucus from the nose, mucus from fennel, shark fat, and a diluted resin, grind them all together in a mortar, then apply it to the woman's vagina and lower abdomen, and sit her in warm water until the vulva is hydrated.
If you see that her waters have broken, make a pocket from a raw date and have her carry it. If she holds the pocket for an hour, sit her on a chair, then blow on her and cover her mouth and nose, gently press her lower abdomen, and the baby should come out immediately, with its knees and hands first.
If the patient is in this position, the surgeon should slowly insert his hands and then have the woman lie on her stomach with her legs hanging down from a bed while she holds her hands. Then, press slightly above her abdomen. Then, remove the pillows under her legs so they are pointing downwards. If the baby does not come out, take both her legs and move them vigorously, then press gently above the perineum to help the baby ascend. Then, the midwife should insert her hand and carefully position the baby.
Instruct the woman to move and strain until the baby is born, with the baby's hand extended in opposition. If she cannot withdraw her hand, hold the woman in a supportive position as she stands or sits, or have her lie down if necessary.
Have her lie on her bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then, use the birthing pad (made from the described materials) if labor has begun. If she feels a strong urge to push, seat her on a chair. Treat her hand and position the baby correctly. Encourage the woman to strain until the baby is born, ensuring it comes out naturally.
When the baby is born on its stomach with its hands extended, the midwife should take hold of the baby's hand and gently turn and position it correctly. Once positioned, apply the recommended oil and birthing paste as described.
Then make her move little by little. If she does so, sit her down on the chair and order her to lean to the right side. Then instruct her to turn over and cough, expelling the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends into the womb in this manner with the placenta, cut the placenta with your nail until it is free from moisture. If it attaches to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the umbilical cord to prevent suffocation and death. Then push the fetus back inside until it is in the proper position.
Instruct the woman to cough several times, and it will come out easily, the birth of twins or many fetuses. Know that twins often give birth to three or four, although this is rare. Five is something beyond nature; they do not live at all.
They are surrounded by a single placenta, and they separate with a membrane barrier between them attached to the umbilical cord of each. They can come out naturally and easily, like one fetus, or they can come out difficultly, as described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and turn to the forms we described, treat them as we described. The midwife should be gentle and do what she does with kindness, patience, and cunningness, using all means available to ensure safety.
The number of fetuses imagined in the womb and then aborted may be one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or more than ten. It is established in my experience that a woman aborted seven, and another aborted fifteen, all of them having conceived multiple fetuses.
According to the most wise and knowing, all of this should be within the knowledge of a midwife. She may one day encounter such a situation and handle it as she should.
Chapter LXXVI
In the removal of a dead fetus.
If, after applying the treatments mentioned in the previous sections, the dead fetus does not exit and manual intervention is required, you should assess the patient. If the woman is physically strong and free from any concerns that could lead to damage, she should lie on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted down, her legs raised, and she should be supported from both sides or tied to the bed to prevent her body from moving when the fetus is being extracted.
Then, moisten the mouth of the womb with wet fats, saliva from fennel and dill, and flaxseed oil. The midwife should then rub her hand with these oils and saliva, insert her hand into the womb gently, and seek out a suitable place to insert the forceps used to extract the fetus.
If the fetus is positioned such that it is about to descend by its head, then hooks should be inserted into its eyes, or back, or mouth, or throat, or under its beard, or in the areas close to the ribs, or beneath the sternum.
If, however, it descends by its legs, then the area around the genitals and middle ribs is targeted. The surgeon should hold the hook with their right hand and wrap its fold between the fingers of their left hand, then insert the hand gently and embed a hook in one of these mentioned positions.
Until it reaches something hollow, then insert another hook in front of it, or a third if necessary, to ensure an equal pull without bias towards any side. Then extend it evenly, not just straight but also moving it to each side.
To facilitate its exit, as is done with a tooth when it is extracted, the surgeon should loosen the extension. If any part becomes stuck, the midwife should smear some fingers with grease and insert them to rotate what is stuck. If part of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other positions.
She should raise it slightly to do this until the fetus is completely delivered. If a hand emerges before others and cannot be pushed back due to compression, wrap it in cloth and then pull it until it comes out entirely, then cut the cloth.
Either at the wrist or the forearm, and so you should do on the other hand and feet, if the fetus' head is large and encounters pressure during delivery, or there is water collected in its head, then you should insert a sharp instrument between the fingers.
And you should make an incision with it to extract the water, or use the instrument called a forceps, which is illustrated along with other instruments in the following chapter.
Similarly, if the fetus has a large head, you should naturally make an incision in the skull or use forceps as we mentioned earlier, and then extract the bones with hooks. If the head emerges but is compressed at the collarbone, it should be incised until the moisture in the chest drains.
If the chest does not expand, and it is not possible for the fetus to emerge, then cut as much as you can. If it is located below the abdomen or has a hernia, you should press on it until the moisture drains.
If the fetus is in a position with its legs, it is easily pulled and aligned towards the cervix. If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, you should use a cloth wrapped around your hand to pull it, and then make an incision at the abdomen or chest until the moisture inside drains.
If all other members have been removed and the head is retained, the midwife should insert her left hand. If the cervix is open, she should insert her hand into the cervix and seek out the head, then draw it towards the mouth of the cervix with her fingers, after which she should insert a hook or two hooks and pull with them.
If the mouth of the womb is attached to a hot tumor, it should not be forced, but instead, wet, fatty substances should be applied, as well as medications, and sitting in relaxing water to soften and moisten it.
If the fetus is on its side and can be repositioned, use what we mentioned for a living fetus; if this is not possible, it should be cut and removed, ensuring no part of the placenta remains inside. Once you have completed your treatment, apply the mentioned remedies for hot tumors that appear in wombs.
If there is bleeding, she should immerse herself in a contracting water bath and use all the remedies. I have seen a woman who had become pregnant but the fetus died in her womb; then she became pregnant again with that same embryo.
Then, the other fetus also died, and after a long time, a tumor appeared in her womb, expanding until it opened and discharged pus. I was called to treat her, and after a long time of treatment, the wound did not heal, so I applied some strong absorptive ointments.
A bone exited from the site, and after some days, another bone emerged. I was amazed by this since the abdomen is a place without bones; I presumed it to be from the bones of a dead fetus, so I examined the wound and removed many bones from the head of the fetus.
I continued following this process until I removed many bones, and the woman was in good health and lived for a considerable time, during which a small amount of pus drained from the site. And I have only shared this rare case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for the skilled physician in their handiwork of treatment.

Chapter Seventy-Eight
In removing the placenta.
If the placenta becomes stuck after childbirth, instruct the patient to press against her abdomen and then sneeze while you cover her mouth and nose with your hand. If it dislodges in this manner, otherwise take a sharp object and carefully extract it.

Chapter LXXIX.
In the treatment of hemorrhoids without perforation.
It may happen that many boys are born with hemorrhoids not perforated, but blocked by a thin membrane. Therefore, the midwife should puncture this membrane with her finger, otherwise, she should tie it off with a sharp scalpel, taking care not to touch the muscle.
Then, she should place a woolen pad soaked in a drink and oil on it, and treat it with ointments until it heals, or if she fears that it will get blocked, insert a small lead pipe into the opening for several days, removing it when the child needs to defecate.
The obstruction of the anus may also result from a healing scar or tumour, which should be incised and then treated with the remedies and plasters we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty.
In the treatment of hemorrhoids occurring in the lower regions.
Hemorrhoids occurring in the lower regions is a complication and thickening that takes place near the anus from the outside or within one of the spaces, and the hemorrhoid may be single or multiple.
If this complication persists, it will open up and release a watery white discharge or thin pus. Some hemorrhoids may extend to the rectum or colon, and those that are not extended may be recognized by what is expelled from them during defecation, both in terms of feces and gas.
Sometimes worms may exit from them, and they can be hemorrhoids if they are in the space and extend to the bladder or penis, or even to the hip joint and the coccyx—in other words, any location where a hemorrhoid is known to have extended but is not open.
Insert your index finger into the anus and, using a thin needle of copper or iron, enter the hemorrhoid if it is not curved. If it is curved, insert a thin lead needle or a horsehair into it.
Until you feel the probe or a hair in your finger, if you do not feel it at all and nothing comes out of the hole, neither feces nor air nor worms, as we have said, know that it is non-perforated and hurry to treat it.
If the fistula is perforating to the bladder or urethra, its indication is the passage of urine through it and its inability to heal with medications. On the other hand, if it is perforating to the thigh joint or gluteal region, its sign is the arrival of the probe there.
If it does not have any curvature towards the bone, there is pain in a specific direction, and pus always comes out of it, and no treatment works and it does not heal with ointments, then there is no cure for these types of fistulas at all.
Treating them is a futile effort for those who attempt it without knowledge, and while the non-perforated and non-chronic fistulas can heal with iron, as I will describe and as I have experienced, place the patient on their back with your hands beneath their chest.
Raise the patient's legs and tilt their hips towards their stomach. Then, insert a copper or lead probe into the fistula if it has any curvature, until you feel where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel that the probe has penetrated all the way through your finger without you sensing any gap or separation between your finger and it, then be certain that it has successfully gone through. Do not exert further effort, as there is no cure for such a condition.

Chapter LXXXIV.
In the treatment of surgical wounds.
As I mentioned in the division concerning the treatment of burns, here I recall the treatment of surgical wounds with the hand and with drugs, to the fullest extent. I say that surgical procedures vary according to the nature of the wound and the location of the cut.
The things that are the cause of wounds are numerous, such as a blow from a stone or a cut from a sword or knife or a stab from a spear or arrow or similar objects.
And as for wounds according to their locations on the body, they are like a wound that occurs on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and similar organs.
And I will describe the treatment of certain wounds to make them a measure and a rule for all other wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds especially, as the complex ones will be mentioned in the first section of the third chapter of this book.
I say that if there is a simple wound on the head, and it is not a broken bone, and it was caused by a blow from a stone or similar objects, and only the skin is pierced, but the wound is large and I fear that the patient may develop a feverish edema, then immediately incise it with a scalpel at the appropriate place. Do not delay this, and drain the blood from him to his strength, if there is no obstacle to incising, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is small, and let the incision be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinking, and heavy foods; if there is an obstacle to incising, let him reduce his food intake and avoid meat and drinks, and apply a cotton ball soaked in rose fat alone or with a drink that has constriction properties.
If the hot tumor is secure, then apply pressure to the wound if it is still fresh with its blood and has not been altered by the air, which I will describe in detail shortly. However, if the air has changed it, apply some ointment to it.
Until the pus is drained, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and was caused by a sword or similar sharp object, and its edges do not meet due to lack of sutures, then sew it up as I will describe in the section on abdominal wound surgery.
If the skin has exposed the bone, and it is hanging but can only be secured with a slight clamp, then cut it away and treat the wound with remedies that promote the growth of strong flesh in place of the skin, and instruct the patient to consume foods that are firm, such as heads, skulls, etc.
If a fungus develops in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, cut off the infected part and throw it away, then treat the wound. If there is an artery or vein that is bleeding and the blood flow cannot be stopped with medications, examine the artery.
If it becomes gangrenous and does not fall off, cut it off with a scalpel and bind it. If, in the case of small, simple wounds, the above remedies do not help and it is necessary to stop the bleeding, cauterize them, and the healing process will be easy and straightforward.
Before the air alters it, the recipe for the dust calls for one part resin, two parts frankincense, and three parts slaked or unslaked lime to be crushed, sieved, and filled into the wound, then tightly bound to ensure a good seal.
It hardens with blood, then secured from above with bandages, left intact as long as it does not swell. The treatment is effective until healing occurs; the lime alone can achieve this if resin and frankincense are unavailable.
It can also achieve this effect with just two components in many major surgeries if the application and binding are done skillfully, as long as the wounds remain fresh with blood. However, if the air has altered them, especially during the summer season, additional measures may be required.
Therefore, using this dust effectively does not imply merely applying some of the prepared ointments or a porridge made from barley flour with water and honey to promote pus formation; rather, it should be treated with all other remedies until healing occurs.
If a fracture occurs in the bone, even if it is minor, draw it out with splints, as mentioned in the treatment of skull fractures. In neck surgery, if the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment from a simple head wound.
If a nerve in the neck has been cut, or an artery, as for a nerve, there is no remedy but to avoid treating it with strong constrictors such as iron, glass, and similar substances, as they will harm and spasm the nerve.
No cold applications should ever be placed on the wound, for the nerve's essence is cold, and it connects to the brain, which is the most noble of organs. Therefore, the treatments used should be gentle, such as a compress soaked in pure water, or oil infused with rose and nutmeg.
If the wound is also washed with pure water and other metallic stones in this manner, and moist plasters are applied, while for a large wound, stitching or binding it tightly with bandages should be used until healing occurs.
If the wound has a depth and a collection of pus has formed at its bottom, it should be drained in the lowest part; if an artery has been severed within the wound and blood is still flowing, it should be cut, tied off, or cauterized if necessary.
If the wound has severed some or all of the pharyngeal muscles and the jugulars are intact, sew together the edges of the skin over the pharynx, taking care not to touch the pharynx itself, then shape it back to its natural form and secure it tightly, leaving it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that cause contraction and drying until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, open it, if there are pieces of bone in it, extract them entirely or as much as possible, and if you cannot do so, leave it until the wound putrefies and heals, making its removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the described powder; if it becomes gangrenous and forms a bone abscess (nasal), treat it as mentioned in its corresponding section, under chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see that it has penetrated deeply, check if air emerges when the patient breathes; if so, know that it is a fatal wound. If there is no deep penetration and the wound is fresh with blood, do not immediately apply powder or tighten it, lest the blood become trapped in its depths.
It will then affect the heart, causing the patient's death. Apply a drawing ointment and dissolve it in water twice a day; if you do not have an ointment, use a worn-out cotton ball to absorb any discharge and place the patient's sleep on the wound.
To drain any accumulated fluid. Chapter LXXV
On abdominal wounds, the emergence of the intestine and its stitching.
The abdominal wound may be large or small or moderate; a large one might expose the intestine or several intestines, making their reinsertion and stitching more difficult and complex.
A small wound can also be difficult to manage from another perspective, as prompt reinsertion of the intestine into its proper position is crucial. If delayed, the intestine may become swollen and thickened, making its reinsertion even more challenging. Therefore, a moderate-sized wound is preferable because it does not present the same difficulties in returning the intestine to its original place as these two types do.
Know that sewing the abdomen on four sides: The two general stitches are suitable for sewing the abdomen and for sewing all other body wounds, while the other two stitches are specific and apply to all wounds except the abdomen.
And I will describe them one by one with an explanation, so I say that if the wound is small and something from the intestine has emerged and it is difficult to return it, this is for one of two reasons: either because the hole is too small as we mentioned, or because the intestine was previously inflated by the cold air.
If this is the case, then you should warm it by immersing a sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or one that has been boiled with cinnamon, calamus, and fennel, until the bloating dissolves.
It may also be achieved with a drink that contains a constrictor, which is better than water alone in dissolving the bloating. If you observe that the bloating has dissipated, moisten the intestine with water boiled with fenugreek or fennel, as it will facilitate its entry with ease.
If, after this treatment, it still cannot be returned, make a small cut in the hole using the tool used for cutting pipes (nassur), and the initial treatment is preferable if it can be done. However, he may be forced to make a cut only as a last resort and when all other methods have failed. This is a picture of the tool:
One side of the wound is curved and limited, while the other is unlimited. The thin edge is not as sharp as the scalpel but slightly concave. It is a tool similar to a small spear. If the wound expands and the intestine is exposed, it should be returned to its natural shape.
Its specific position is preferable if possible, but if the wound is wide and located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, the head and chest should be raised above the lower part.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to have the surgical area higher than the other side. This applies to both major and medium surgeries.
In minor surgeries, place the patient as you are able to, then have a helpful assistant hold the wound with both hands, gathering the edges together, and reveal them to the person doing the stitching one at a time.
This is the general method of stitching: take a needle or several needles suitable for the size of the wound, leave a gap at the edge of the wound equal to the thickness of a finger, and sew with one needle without threading it with a thread on both sides of the skin. With the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin, from within to penetrate them on that side, and having gathered together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, they become four incisions, then it is tightened with a doubled thread around the needle from both sides many times until the edges of the wound meet firmly.
Then it is left for the thickness of a finger, and another needle is inserted, then it is threaded as it was done with the first needle, continuing to do so with as many needles as needed until the whole wound is drained, and the needles should be placed between the thick and thin parts.
Because very thin needles quickly cut through meat, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin, they should therefore be placed halfway between the thin and thick parts, and the needle should not be inserted at the edge of the skin close to the wound.
So that the meat is not cut off quickly and the wound does not reopen before healing, nor should it be too tight with stitching, which might prevent the wound from healing. Then cut the ends of the needles to avoid harming the patient during sleep and make them secure with pieces of cotton fabric from each side to hold the needle ends.
And leave it until you know that the wound has healed. This method of stitching with needles is most effective for small wounds, as it may only require one, two, or a few more needles to sew them up.
The technique of stitching the second layer of the common approach consists of gathering all four margins, that is, both margins of the skin and both margins of the peritoneum, in a single pass with an needle containing a moderate length of spun thread suitable for its tenderness and thickness. Once these four margins have been passed through the needle, the needle returns from the same side it started.
The thread should be interlaced across the top of the wound, forming a stitch similar to that used for sealing containers, which tightens the load and spaces each stitch apart by the thickness of the small finger. This stitch is known to everyone.
By this stitch, a surgeon treated a man who had been cut with a knife, and the abdominal wound was more than an inch in size, with about two inches of the middle intestine protruding. The tear was located in the middle of the abdomen.
After 24 hours, the intestine was returned to its place inside the wound. The wound healed in about 15 days, and he was treated until he recovered fully and lived for many years, performing all his activities. The doctors had judged that he would never recover.
It is remarkable that I did not treat it with a salve, as I was in a place where there were no medicinal supplies. Instead, I placed old cotton on the wound twice a day and cared for its washing with honey water until it healed.
And as for one of the two types of suturing, I mention it based on Galen's words: to begin suturing with the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside, then, once the needle has penetrated through the skin and muscle in a straight line along the entire length of the abdomen.
I leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where the needle was inserted, and insert the needle from inside to outside on its other edge, passing through the margin of the rectus muscle, then, once inserted, pass it again through the same edge from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and insert the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, then, as you pass it through, also pass it through the rectus muscle's edge, until it completely penetrates into it.
Then, begin from the same side, following the line with the edge closest to the peritoneum on the other side, and remove the needle from the skin next to it, then return it through that same skin, along with the peritoneum's edge on the opposite side, in line with the rectus muscle.
Remove the needle from the skin on this side, and repeat this process several times, until you have sutured the entire surgical site, following the example provided. The characteristic of this suture is that it resembles the stitching of furriers on leather.
By sewing the peritoneum once on one side with the edge of the skin and leaving it open on the other side, allowing it to empty. The second type of stitching, specifically mentioned by Galen, is as follows.

Chapter LXXVIII.
In the treatment of the common cold and nosebleed.
Know that every wound or lump that persists, grows old, and becomes a ulcer that does not heal and continuously produces pus, is called, in general, a fistula, and we call it a cold (or catarrh) when it occurs in any part of the body.

Some have therefore named it a feather, and it can sometimes be moist, continuously producing pus, and at times the moisture may cease. It can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.

Either the ulcer is deeply infected and filthy, or itself putrid with a foul-smelling substance, or due to poor quality material it produces, or because the treatment is unsuitable, or due to a plague in the town, or some other local factor.

Therefore, know that you have not removed all corruption, so expose all of it once again and explore its removal and purification with all your effort, then force it (the tissue). If it heals, otherwise, be aware that this corruption is deep and remote from the initial corruption, so expose all of it once more.
Explore its removal and purification with all your effort. If the corrupt bone is a small one and you can pull it out, then use delicate hooks suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, explore their removal all at once and do not leave any behind. If something prevents you, such as a ligament, and cutting it poses no danger, then cut the ligaments and flesh to free the bones.
If you are unable to remove the bones at once, then apply something that prevents putrefaction to the wound and leave it for several days until the flesh around the bones becomes rotten along with the ligaments. Preserve the wound so as not to allow the cut you made during your work to heal.
Instead, if you are afraid of this, place a cotton ball soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or a cotton ball soaked in Egyptian paste or one of the green pastes. If these ligaments rot and the bones become loose, it will be easy to remove them and pull them out.
If it is a single large bone, such as the leg or thigh bone, and the corruption affects only its surface, then remove it thoroughly to eliminate that darkness and corruption. Afterward, close the wound. However, if the corrupted part is substantial and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, it must be cut away.
Cut it all away to where the corruption ends, and then treat it until it heals. I will tell you of a case that occurred to a man in his leg, which can serve as an example and aid to your treatment; this man was young.
Around thirty years of age, he experienced pain in his leg due to a movement inside the body, which caused materials to reach the leg and form a large lump that was not caused by something external. Over time, with the persistence of the pain and the mistakes of the doctors, the lump opened up.
Much pus and fluid drained from it, and it was poorly treated, causing the leg to become gangrenous with numerous openings exuding pus and body fluids. It was treated by a group of doctors for about two years, but there was no skilled surgeon among them.
Then I examined his leg, and the pus was draining from these openings in great quantities. The man's body had wasted away, and his skin had turned yellow. I inserted a probe into one of these openings, which reached the bone, so I examined all the openings.
I found them opening into each other on all sides of the leg, so I incised and cut open one of those openings, exposing some bone which was decayed, having been eaten away, blackened, putrefied, and perforated to such an extent that the perforation reached the brain. I then spread apart what had become exposed.
He removed the rotten bone, and I believe that there was no other damage to the bone than what I had cut out and spread apart. He then used compresses with healing drugs for a longer time, but it did not heal. Then he examined the bone again for a second time.
I found the decay attached to the bone, so I spread apart what had become exposed from that decay as well. I then attempted to force it together, but it did not heal or bind. I examined it again, and I kept cutting off pieces of the bone one by one, trying to make it heal, but even after cutting off about a palm-length of bone, it still would not bind.
He removed it with its brain, then used compresses with healing drugs, and it healed quickly. However, this repetition was necessary due to the patient's weakness, lack of endurance, and my fear of death, as excessive vomiting had previously caused a severe condition that only fully recovered.
Flesh grew in the place of the bone, and its condition improved in his body. His strength returned, and he regained control over his movements. No harm affected him while walking, and if a bone broke in any part of the body, it healed properly.
It is necessary to proceed as follows: take a bandage, tighten it at the end of the protruding bone, and order someone to pull it upwards, and make another bandage from thicker wool than the first.
Then bind it to the flesh beneath the bone, and extend its end downwards to draw the flesh down, while exposing the flesh in the area you wish to cut, so as not to harm the saw, and place a wooden block or board under the bone firmly.
For if you do not cut the rotten bone, the saw will not be able to cut it, and you should make the cut slightly above the area of rot, so as not to leave a cavity in the bone that would not be visible to the eye, and you might have to cut it again.
If the bone is rotten and not protruding, but connected to each other, with rot in the middle or in some of them, then expose the flesh from all sides, place a wooden block below, and cut it from the first side where the rot is.
Once you have finished cutting, cut from the other side, and make the cut slightly away from the rot as we mentioned. If the rot is in a joint, then cut the rotten joint itself and cut the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the rot affects the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but to remove it. If the rot is in a finger or toe joint, its treatment is very difficult, but you should peel away the rot as best you can, clean and remove it, however possible.
By what means can you achieve it, if a vessel or nerve does not impede you? Know that the saws and chisels for cutting these bones are many, according to the position, arrangement, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and softness of the bones. Therefore, for each type of work, you should prepare a tool suitable for that work.
And know that the work itself may indicate the type of tool you need, if you have long experience and knowledge of these arts, for he who is skilled in the craft and has witnessed various forms of illness. He may himself deduce what is suitable for each illness, and I will show you in the last part of this door several tools.

Chapter LXXVIII.
On the amputation of limbs and the separation of bones.
The limbs may decay either from a cause external or internal, and if you treat the corruption with drugs but the treatment is ineffective and you see the decay spreading in the member, nothing will stop it.
You should then cut off the limb at the point where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death or a great calamity greater than the loss of the member; and a sign that this is the case is when the member turns black as if it had been burned by fire.
Or it may turn black and the decay will spread to what is adjacent, taking over the body; so be swift in cutting it off. Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the bite of some creatures such as a sea scorpion, or a snake, or a spider, or the like.
If the corruption or bite is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger, and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand; similarly, if it has taken hold of the hand, cut it off at the wrist, and do not allow the corruption to spread further until it reaches your forearm.
If, however, the injury is in the arm, it should be amputated at the elbow joint itself; if the damage allows for it, and you consider it necessary, take it towards the shoulder, but do not ever cut off the shoulder, as this would cause the patient's death. Rather, use other treatments to the best of your ability.
Similarly, if the damage in the foot affects a finger, it should be amputated at one of the phalanges; if it involves the sole of the foot, cut off the entire foot, but if it reaches the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and let the patient succumb to death. The method of amputation or dismemberment consists in tying a ligature below the point where you intend to cut, and another ligature above that point.
The assistant should tie one ligature below and another above, then, using a broad scalpel, remove the flesh between the two ligatures, exposing it completely. Finally, cut or sever the tissue, ensuring to apply a piece of linen cloth from all sides.
To prevent the saw from striking the wrong place, causing additional pain and a hot swelling to the patient, if bleeding occurs during the operation, quickly stop or press on the site with some sharp flakes to stem the flow, then resume your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured member with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals, and I will tell you of an example that occurred to a man in his foot, this very occurrence that I describe to you, for there appeared on his foot a darkness with burning like fire.
This corruption was the first to occur in his finger, until it spread throughout his foot, and the man, upon seeing the corruption spread in the member with the intensity of pain and burning, amputated it at the joint, and he healed.
After a long time, the same corruption appeared in his index finger, so he came to me, and I treated it with the remedies I had on hand, after cleaning his body, but the corruption did not stop and spread to the other fingers.
Until the corruption spread to his hand, he called on me to amputate it, but I refused, hoping to stop that corruption and fearing for his life when cutting off his hand, as his strength was waning, and when he despaired of my help, he left for his hometown.
I was informed that he amputated his entire hand and healed, and I have related this story to serve as a help in the case of this disease and as a guide to be followed.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
In the treatment of abscesses and the method of injecting them with medicines.
If a tumor occurs in some fleshy organs and its duration is prolonged until it ruptures or opens, expelling everything within it, leaving the site empty as if it were a vessel.
The skin covering it is like a cloth that has become thin but has not yet decayed to the point of affecting a bone, nerve, or ligament; from here it deserves to be called an abscess rather than a fistula, except if its duration prolongs until the decay affects any of these members.
If it is then called a fistula or an ulcer, and its treatment involves cutting off all that skin completely, as it unravels, especially if it has become thin like a cloth and is confirmed to not adhere to the site due to its decay.
If the skin still adheres and has not yet reached that level of decay, but there is enough flesh, then treat it with injection; that is, examine the abscess. If it is large and the pus within it has a bad odor, inject Egyptian medicine into it.
Which is a mixture of vinegar, oil, honey, and cinnamon, equal parts, combined in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens like honey; then take as much as needed, dilute it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess, sealing its opening and leaving the medicine inside for two hours.
Then, remove it with the era (potter's clay); do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor disappears. If this remedy is not available, you can use ashes of date palm wood or oak wood, mixing them with water.
Then, strain it and inject it until you are certain that the abscess has been washed out. If there is no chance of obtaining Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject a mixture of water and honey with a touch of powdered zingiber (ginger).
Or, you can inject honey and a drink mixed together, as honey purifies and washes, while the drink adheres to the abscess, especially if it contains residual astringent and drying properties. If you do this several times and are certain that the abscess is free from corruption, then inject what promotes tissue regeneration.
For example, take some palm ointment and mix it with rose oil and astringent drink, or inject it with other medications and ointments whose properties have been proven in the article on ointments. If the opening of the abscess is narrow and cannot accommodate the syringe, then...
Gradually widen it with iron or insert a clean string soaked in Egyptian ointment or syrupon ointment to make it larger. If the opening is also wide, sew its edges together and leave enough space for the syringe to enter without excess.
If the mouth through which pus flows is elevated, make an incision in the lowest part to allow the pus to flow downward, for if pus accumulates in the depths of a wound, it prevents tissue regeneration.
If it is not possible to make an incision in the depth of the wound towards the bottom as desired, then mold the tissue so that the pus can flow easily from it, according to what you find suitable, and ensure that no pus accumulates there at all.
As for the protective medications that are applied to a wound from the outside, and how they should be applied, take one of the protective ointments suitable for the condition you wish to treat, and spread it over a cotton cloth, enough to cover the entire wound. Then, using scissors, make a hole in front of the wound's opening, slightly wider than the wound itself. Next, apply a soft ointment to another cotton cloth, cut to the size of the hole, and place it over the wound, ensuring it is not too close to the wound's opening. Leave the large cloth untouched until the pus has drained, then remove it.
For several days, the ointment on the wound's opening should be removed at regular intervals to allow any accumulated pus to drain and to assess the nature of the discharge: whether it is excessive or minimal, mature or immature.
And moreover, examine the site of the abscess itself to see if its owner feels pain there or not, and if there is a swelling or not. If your treatment continues in this way and you observe that the material that comes out is meager at the mouth of the abscess.
If you know that the abscess has adhered or is about to adhere, then dissolve it on the morrow or on the third day. And when you have opened the wound, replace the bandage with a new one and renew the ointment. If after a long time thin pus comes out from the abscess, do not despair of its healing.
Healing is near, because among people there are those who slow the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others do the opposite. If after many days pus that is not yet mature comes out from the abscess, know that the abscess has not adhered. If it takes time for the abscess to adhere.
If the process takes a long time, increase the drying medicines, and let them be in a state of being moist in composition and dry in power, such as the palm butter if it is increased with more of the collicular (a traditional medicinal ingredient). Among what is most effective for treatment is to use the palm butter.
Apply a mixture of rose oil and an ancient, moderate-strength drink, then knead it and use it. If rose butter is not available, you can use honey after boiling it until it thickens, or mix powdered asafoetida, galangal, and cinnamon and sprinkle the powder over the honey while it's on the fire. Then, apply it to a cloth and secure it over the wound, or coat it with honey that has been cooked until it thickens, then sprinkle medications on top and place the cloth over it and secure it.
In such cases, long ziraund, the root of the blue lily, and powdered calum (cress) or centaury (centaurea) can be used alone or mixed, ground and sifted, then sprinkled over the honey described earlier and applied; however, if after a prolonged period the wound does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within.
It has affected bones, or other hard nerves or ligaments, and its treatment is the same as for fistulas, as mentioned earlier.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
In the treatment of ganglions, crushed nails, amputation of the surplus toe, and incising the healing of the fingers.
A ganglion is a mass of flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb or any other finger, and sometimes in other parts of the fingers. If its condition persists and treatment is neglected, it becomes swollen and hot.
Or it decays and putrefies until it consumes the nail root, and perhaps all of it, and the decay may reach the bone, emitting a foul odor, becoming broad at the tip, and its color blackened; if you treat it as mentioned in the previous sections and your treatment is ineffective.
All the debris that grows from under the nail should be cut with iron, and then the wound should be cauterized. Indeed, cauterization is very beneficial in this case, especially if the bone is intact and so is the nail.
And the angle of the nail, which pierces the flesh, should be filed and raised slightly, and a thin rod should be placed under this angle to gently lift and cut off the piercing flesh.
You should apply burning, corrosive medicines to the remaining flesh until it all disappears, and then treat it with ointments for healing. However, if corruption has affected the bone, you must cut out and remove that bone, for it will not heal as long as there is a corrupt bone left.
If you observe decay and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the phalanges as previously mentioned, and then treat it until it heals. If the nail is injured or bruised and causes severe pain, you should first drain the blood or pus.
Then, using a sharp scalpel, make an oblique cut in the nail from top to bottom, taking care not to reach the flesh beneath. This will cause severe pain for the patient and may lead to excess tissue growth at the site. Treat the area with remedies to relieve pain.
And as for the surplus finger that is generated in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy or perhaps have bones in it, and sometimes there is a nail, and some of them grow from the base of one of the fingers' joints.
Or if it grows from some of the phalanges of the finger, and those that grow in some phalanges do not move, and those that grow at the joint of the finger may move, then what is entirely fleshy should be cut off easily by cutting it at its base with a wide knife.
As for the one whose growth is at the joint's base, its treatment is difficult, so it should not be cut off; but for the one that grows in the finger at a phalange, the flesh around it should first be cut away circularly, and then the bone can be split using one of those saws suitable for this purpose.
Then treat it until it heals; and the adhesion that occurs between fingers, often occurring naturally or due to a wound or fire burn, etc., should be incised in order to break this adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural state, then place between them a thread or a small hole soaked in rose fat so that it does not adhere quickly and separate, or use a thin lead plate between them until the wound heals as it should.
And if the fusion of some fingers with the hand is exposed, then that fusion should be cut according to what is suitable and proper for the shape of the member.
Chapter Ninety
In the cutting of veins.
Veins are twisted, thick vessels filled with a dark, viscous fluid, most commonly found in the legs, particularly in the forks of the legs, the ankles, and the feet. Therefore, it is first necessary to use vigorous rubbing of the body to dislodge the black mud.
Then, the vein (basilisk) should be cut, and its treatment with iron consists of two methods: one is to open it and drain the black blood, and the other is to scrape it off completely. In the first method, the leg should be soaked in hot water first.
Until the thick, cloudy blood is dissolved, then the patient's leg should be tightly bandaged from above the thigh to below the knee, and the vein should be cut in one, two, or three wide incisions.
Then, the black blood should be drained by hand from below the leg upwards and from above downwards, until you have removed what you consider sufficient or what the patient's strength allows. Then it should be tied off, and he should be instructed to avoid foods that promote the formation of black bile. This process should be repeated with vomiting and fainting.
When the veins are filled and this causes harm to the patient, if his leg has a lot of hair, it should be shaved, then immersed in hot water, and the leg should be washed with hot water until it turns red and sweats or undergoes intense physical exercise.
If a bath is not available to heat the affected area, then the skin should be split lengthwise over the sweat gland, either at the knee or below the ankle, using a sharp instrument, and the sweat gland should be removed from each side until it becomes visible to the touch.
When it appears, you will see it as a dark red color, and if you remove the skin, it resembles a tendon. Then, insert a smooth, blunt hook underneath until it rises and comes out of the skin. Hang it on this hook, and make another cut nearby, about the length of three fingers.
Then, remove the skin from the sweat gland until it is exposed, and proceed to raise it with the hook as before, hanging it on another hook. Then, make another cut or multiple cuts if needed.
Then, pull and cut it at the end of the first cut near the ankle, then pull and remove it from the second cut until it emerges from the third cut. Finally, cut it off at the third cut, which is above all the others.
If the traction and the slip do not work, introduce a needle with strong doubled thread, tie it, pull it, and insert under it a trocar, then twist your hand in all directions until it comes out. Be careful; if it breaks, it will be very difficult to extract.
Once the foreign body is removed, apply a poultice of soaked linen in rosewater or oil to the wound and treat it until healing occurs. If the object is twisted and has curves, it must be turned in all directions.
If it is not straight, as we mentioned, it should be cut at each curve and twist, then hung on pegs until completely removed. Be careful when cutting it; do not cut any vessels or cause a wound, as it will make extraction difficult.

Chapter Ninety-One.
On the Sweat Gland of the Body.
This sweat is generated in the legs in hot countries such as Hijaz and Arab lands, and in arid, impoverished regions with little vegetation, and possibly in other parts of the body besides the legs. It is also produced due to fermentation occurring beneath the skin.
Similarly, it occurs within the bodies of living creatures and worms, requiring scraping and the removal of worms that develop between the skin and flesh. The onset of this sweat is indicated by a severe itching in the leg, followed by a tingling sensation, after which perspiration begins to exit from the site of the itching.
It is as if it were a root or an animal part, so when a portion appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead, weighing between one and two dirhams, and tied off. The lead is then left hanging from the leg.
Therefore, whenever something emerges from it towards the outside, wrap it in lead and tie it off. If it persists for a long time, cut some of it but do not remove it from its origin before all of it has emerged, as cutting it too early may cause it to retract and enter the flesh, leading to swelling, putrefaction, and a bad ulcer at the site.
Therefore, it should be managed and cut little by little until it exits completely from the body, without leaving any trace. In some people, this vein may be as long as five or ten fingers.
I have heard that in one case, it reached twenty fingers' length for a man. If it disconnects during treatment, insert a rod into its opening and wrap it tightly with the body until everything inside is exhausted, and try to heal the site with drugs for several days.
Then treat it with tumor treatment, especially if it appears in a joint or in the foot, where it has numerous branches, and each branch should be treated as we mentioned in the division and as previously stated.
Chapter 92
In the incision on the worm that develops under the skin, known as cow disease.
This disease is called "cow disease" in some of our countries, as it often affects cattle, a small worm that develops between the skin and meat, and moves throughout the body, rising and falling.
It is perceptive to the touch when it moves from one part of the body to another until it breaches through the skin, forming a sore from the putrefaction of certain humors, as occurs with worms and snakes, and rashes on the stomach.
And it is expected that its harm comes from the fact that if it spreads in the body and rises to the head, reaching the eye, it may open and come out, thereby destroying the eye. This occurs frequently. If one wishes to treat and remove it, it can only be done when it has spread and become apparent.
One should tighten the skin above and below it with strong bands, then make an incision and extract it. If it has become embedded in the flesh and cannot be found, one should apply heat from a fire to burn it. The greatest harm it can cause is to the eye, as we have mentioned.
If it is seen to have moved to the head, near the eye, tighten the skin under the forehead firmly, then make an incision and extract it. The patient should purify their body with purgative medicines that eliminate bad, stagnant humors, and be cautious of foods that cause fermentation.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On the incision for the condition known as 'nafar'.
The condition known as 'nafar' in our land is a pain that appears in certain members and then transfers from one member to another, which I have observed as I describe.
I was called to a sick woman in a certain desert. She exposed her arm, and I saw a slight swelling in the vein of the forearm. After an hour, I observed that the swelling was moving towards the elbow with great speed, similar to mercury.
If the pain moves from one place to another, it disappears from the original location and settles in the elbow, then after an hour, it spreads throughout the body, reaching the other arm. She told me that her whole body was turning as she observed.
I was amazed by the speed at which it moved from one part of the body to another, and I had never previously seen this disease in such a way with such rapidity, except for having observed a group of people experiencing pain that shifted from one part of their bodies to another.
I initially thought that the reason for this was that the woman was from a desert region, with dry bodies and visible veins, which made the moving sensation more perceptible. This explanation would not apply to those in comfort, whose bodies are moist and have hidden veins.
If one wishes to treat it when the patient feels pain, and the swelling appears as we described, apply pressure above and below it with haste, make an incision, and drain the accumulated fluid, then bandage the area.
If you cannot see the condition, treat it by shaking the body and expelling the winds, such as with powder, or drops, or similar remedies.
Chapter Ninety-Four
In removing arrows.
Arrows may vary according to their types and the positions they occupy in the body. As for their differences by type, there are large and small arrows, hollow and solid glass arrows, and those with three or four angles.
Some have tongues, others have fragments, and according to the organs they enter into, they fall into two categories: either they are in the main hollow organs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidney, intestine, bladder, and similar ones. If an arrow enters one of these organs and the signs of death that I am describing to you appear, it is necessary to avoid removing that arrow; for in most cases, death will ensue. And if the arrow does not show any of these evil signs and has not become embedded in the organ, remove it and treat the wound. As for the signs of the brain if an arrow penetrates it and breaks the bone and injures the peritoneum covering the brain, it will cause severe headache, confusion, and dizziness.
And if the arrow does not show any of these evil signs and has not become embedded in the organ, remove it and treat the wound. As for the signs of the brain if an arrow penetrates it and breaks the bone and injures the peritoneum covering the brain, it will cause severe headache, confusion, and dizziness. Redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, spasms, mental confusion, sudden vomiting, and possibly blood from the nostrils or ears. Speech may be cut off, voice lost, and white, mucus-like discharge emanates from the wound site.
If these signs appear, refrain from further treatment and arrow extraction if it has not already left the body. As for the signs of an arrow that has struck the heart, close to the left breast, and is felt as though it has become embedded in a solid object rather than something hollow.
The arrow's movement may resemble a pulse, and black blood issues from the wound, followed by cold limbs, cold sweat, and fainting. Know that death is inevitable. If the arrow wounds the lung, frothy blood will exit from the wound.
The vessels around the neck swell, the patient's skin changes color, they breathe heavily, and seek cold air. If the arrow has pierced the pleura (the membrane surrounding the lungs), it is close to the small ribs, causing immense breathlessness and pain.
The patient sighs, and all the muscles around their shoulders tighten. If the arrow has struck the liver, intense pain follows, and bright red blood issues from the wound. If it has hit the stomach, undigested food may exit from the wound.
And his command is clear. If the arrow enters the abdomen and becomes embedded there, or if something from the feces emerges from the wound, or if the intestines are perforated, then there is no hope for treatment or for extracting the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and causes urine to emerge, then it is a matter that requires immediate attention.
A visible protrusion from the wound, accompanied by severe pain, indicates a fatal outcome. However, most other organs, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, back, ribcage, thigh, and leg, among others, can often recover.
When the arrow does not strike a blood vessel or nerve and is not poisoned, I will share with you some observations on these arrows to guide your treatment. For example, consider a case where an arrow struck a man in the eye socket at the base of the nose.
He removed the arrow from the opposite side beneath the earlobe, and the patient recovered without any harm to his eye. I also extracted another arrow that had struck a Jew in the eyelid below the lower lashes; the arrow was partially withdrawn and only its small tip remained embedded in the wood.
It was a large arrow from a composite bow, made of smooth iron square-tipped without barbs. The Jew recovered without any eye complications. I also removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian; it was an Arab-made arrow.
He who has two ears and between them a wound had penetrated, which had sunk into his ring, I treated it gently until I extracted it, and the Christian was saved. I also removed another arrow from a man who had been wounded in the stomach; we thought he would die from it.
After he had been thus for about thirty days, without any change in his condition, I made an incision over the arrow and treated it, and he was cured and did not suffer any further complications. I also saw a man who had been wounded by an arrow in his back; the wound healed over it.
After seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his thigh. I also saw a woman who had been wounded in the stomach by an arrow; the wound healed over it, and the arrow remained without causing any change in her condition or hindering her from performing her natural actions.
I also saw another man who had been wounded in the face by an arrow; the wound healed, and he did not experience much pain. Many similar cases existed. I removed an arrow from a commander of the sultan that had penetrated his nose in the middle, slightly leaning to the right.
The arrow completely disappeared, and I was called to treat it three days after the injury. I found the wound caused by the arrow very narrow; using a thin probe, I explored it, feeling nothing, but he experienced a pinching and aching sensation under his right ear from the opening.
I have failed to expect that the point of the arrow would cause such a wound, so I dressed the area with a bandage containing an absorbent force to promote healing, hoping that the area would swell and show the mark of the arrow. I incised it, but there was no sign that the arrow had reached the area.
I persisted with the bandage for many days, but there was no improvement; the wound healed over, and the patient remained bedridden, hoping to expel the arrow. One day, he felt the arrow inside his nose and informed me of it. I then applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened and I probed it, I felt the fine point of the arrow attached to the wood. Then I continued to open the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared, staying with me for a period of time lasting about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to insert hooks into it, I pulled and moved them, but they would not come out. So I continued to manipulate them and devise ways to extract them using various tools until one day I managed to grasp them firmly with secure hooks, as illustrated in the final chapter of this book.
The doctors had judged that his nose cartilage would not heal, but I managed to fix it, and the wound healed completely, without causing him any further harm. I will tell you how I extracted some arrows, which serves as a comparison and proof for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts and details of this craft cannot be fully conveyed through description or contained within a single book. Rather, the skilled practitioner measures with a few words against many, applies what is present to what is absent, and infers a new work and tool at unforeseen crises.
If we delve into this craft, I say that arrows depart from the parts where they became lodged in two ways: either by being drawn from the place they entered, or from the opposite direction. They exit as they entered.
Either the arrow protrudes from a feverish part and can be drawn out, or if it does not come out on its own, you should leave it for days until the flesh around it putrefies, making it easier to draw and remove, especially if it becomes lodged in a bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for additional days and repeatedly attempt to draw and move it daily; it will eventually exit. If it still refuses to come out after several days, you should drill around the arrow in the same bone with a delicate drill on all sides until you create an opening, then draw and remove it.
If the arrow is lodged in the skull bone and deeply penetrates one of the brain's cavities, causing some of the symptoms you mentioned, refrain from drawing it out and allow the patient time to recover after several days.
In cases where the arrow must be drawn and removed, if it is deeply embedded and does not come out with pulling, use a drill around the arrow as described to you, then treat the site until healing occurs. However, if the arrow has withdrawn into a body part, treat accordingly. If sensation is absent, use a probe to locate it. If you feel it, draw it out with suitable instruments. If you cannot do so because the wound is too narrow or the arrow is deeply embedded and there are no bones, nerves, or blood vessels nearby.
Make an incision to widen the wound and insert your hand to grasp the arrow. If it has two ends embedded in the flesh, use every means possible to free the flesh from both sides. If you cannot free the flesh without breaking the bones or cutting them off, do so.
If you attempt to extract the arrow from any angle, use your hand with pliers in all directions until you free it. Be gentle to avoid breaking the arrow, which would make extraction more difficult. If you cannot do this within a reasonable time frame.
Leave it for several days until the flesh around it decays, then treat it again. If bleeding occurs, use the treatment methods mentioned in their respective sections and be careful not to cut any vessels, nerves or tendons, but employ other techniques instead.
In every way, you can extract the arrowhead with gentleness and caution, ensuring it is secured as described. When withdrawing the arrow, you should position the wound as it was when the arrow entered, for this is most beneficial.
If this cannot be done, use whatever shapes you can manage. As for the arrow that emerges from the opposite side, either a portion may have protruded outward or you might feel the end of the arrow close to the skin's surface.
Make a cut around it, as wide as the arrowhead, then pull. If it is stuck to a bone, twist your hand in a circular motion until the arrow impacts the bone and widens the opening, then draw it out.
If it does not come out after several days, treat it again. If the arrowhead remains inside, push it with a suitable instrument; if the head has fallen off but you still want to use force, insert a hollow tool into the arrow's shaft and push it through the tail of the arrowhead.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that fits inside the hollow, for this will facilitate its exit. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize the flesh in which the poison has taken hold, if possible, and then treat it as appropriate.
If the arrow pierces the chest, abdomen, bladder, or side and is close to what can be felt with a probe, make an incision and be careful not to cut any blood vessels or nerves, then remove it.
Then, sew the wound if necessary, and treat it until it heals. Figure showing the hooks used to extract arrows:

Chapter Ninety-Five.
On the puncturing of veins.
The veins which are usually punctured in the body number thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the sensitives; and the two arteries in the temples, visible, along with the two veins in the eye sockets, known as the lookers.

As for the veins punctured in the arm and hand, there are five: one is the cephalic vein, on the lateral side, commonly called the 'vein of the head'; the akhol (or axillary) vein, the middle vein, composed of a branch from the basilic and another from the cephalic, commonly known as the 'body vein'.
The basilic, situated on the medial side, also called the 'underarm' vein; it is generally known as the 'stomach vein', or the 'arm tendon', which attaches to the ulna and appears clearly above the thumb, along with the annular vein.
It is the vein located between the ring finger and the middle finger, which has two branches. On the leg and foot, there are three veins: one beneath the kneecap on the outer side, another at the ankle on the inner side, and a third, the sciatic vein, at the back of the heel on the outer side.
