Chapter LXXXIX (69)
In cupping and its method of use.
The cupping instruments may be made of bone, wood, copper, or glass, and there are two types of cupping: one for draining blood (conditional cupping) and the other without drainage (unconditional cupping).
This unconditional cupping has two faces.
And the cups for the temples (on both sides of the neck), and those for the chin (underneath the lower jaw, near the mouth), and those for the shoulders, and those for the hip bone (on the lower back), and those for the elbows (in the middle of the arms), and those for the legs, and those for the ankles.
Cupping draws blood from the delicate veins spread throughout the flesh; for this reason, it does not cause the body to void itself as leeching would, and cupping should not be used for any condition marked by swelling, so that the whole body may be relieved.
If cupping is indicated, whether for illness or by habit, it should be used at any time of the month—first, last, or in between—and at any hour of the day.
That is because some people, when blood accumulates to the extent that it requires removal through cupping, experience a sensation of heaviness and headaches. Others may feel swelling, redness, and discomfort in their faces, heads, and necks. Some have itching on their faces and sides, and darkness and pain in their eyes, while others scratch the area where they had cupping done.
Some people laugh excessively, while others taste blood in their mouths, with swollen gums and bleeding. Some sleep a lot, and in their dreams they see blood, redness, wounds, and other similar things. We should be wary of these signs.
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we prescribe cupping after two or three hours have passed in the day. The benefit of a cupping on the head is that it relieves heaviness and reduces fluid buildup in the eyes.
However, this should be done after a complete evacuation of the body's humors. This cupping can be an alternative to phlebotomy, but it is dangerous for those with cold-blooded temperament or suffering from a cold, as it may cause significant harm.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold-related illnesses should avoid it, as long-term use can lead to memory loss.
Therefore, the cupper should lower their hand slightly during the procedure to prevent memory loss. Cupping on the back is an alternative to phlebotomy for the eyes and tongue.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, cracking in the respiratory system, coughing, and fullness. It is necessary to raise the bleed from the back slightly, because if it is directed downward, it causes weakness in the heart and stomach.
And as for the bleed from the temples, it is beneficial for pain in the head, eye strain, migraine, sore throat, and pain at the roots of teeth, serving as an alternative to bleeding the basilic vein. The surgeon should instruct the bleeder not to deepen their hand excessively to avoid cutting a artery, leading to bleeding.
And as for the bleed beneath the chin, it is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, gum disease, and other oral conditions, serving as an alternative to bleeding the gums located between the lips. The bleed from the shoulders is effective for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
As for the bleed from both sides of the abdomen (the zenzi), it is beneficial for what bleeding into three veins—the basilic, ocular, and temporal—achieves, as it draws blood from all those delicate veins in the flesh and pulls those delicate veins from other, thinner vessels into which they connect, until the attraction reaches the three thick veins.

One must be cautious about cupping after bathing or immediately after bathing.
One should use it (cupping) after leaving the bath, an hour or two later, and no one should sleep after cupping. What one should arrange for the person who is undergoing cupping, both before and after.
One should first examine if the person undergoing cupping or bleeding has a jaundiced appearance, with sharp and inflammatory symptoms in their blood, then they should take cooling substances such as pomegranates, dandelion, vinegar, endive, lettuce, henbane, and similar plants, and make their diet consist of fried foods, lamb meat, bitter fruits, and similar substances.
For those with a cold temperament, one should give honey drink, or a drink made from henbane seeds, and moderate perfumed wine, which is between the old and new. They should be instructed to eat little, and their diet should consist of endive, artichokes, and sparrows' eggs. The drink on the day of cupping and bleeding should be more abundant than food.
In certain cases, it may be necessary to give specific tonics or medications such as the distilling elixir, musk, or chiletha to individuals before cupping or bleeding, or afterwards, in order to strengthen the vital organs and purify the blood. However, it should not be given to those who are feverish.
And as for incisions that are unconditional, they are those made on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney position, and hip joint, because these organs do not tolerate conditionings, but rather aim to draw blood from one member to another.
As for placing the incision on the breasts in the case of nosebleed or using it to release a cold, stiffened air from the organ, it loosens the organ, warms it, and alleviates the pain by releasing that air. Similarly, when we place the incision on the abdomen and navel, it relaxes the organ, heats it, and relieves the pain caused by the trapped air.
It may also be placed on the kidneys if there is a blockage or stone, as the powerful suction might open the blockage or dislodge the stone. Similarly, it acts when placed on the liver and spleen during periods of trapped gas, causing confusion. These incisions can also be used solely through suction.
It can also be used with fire or filled with warm water for conditions of constipation. To do this, the incision should be filled with hot water alone or water in which certain herbs suitable for this purpose have been cooked.
Then, it is placed over the affected area, secured, and removed multiple times as needed. This is an illustration of the incision used with fire:
The mouth's capacity is the width of two fingers, open as we have illustrated, and in depth half a cubit. On its side, there is a small hole that is about half its size, suitable for an needle, made from brass or yellow copper with a thick edge.
Smooth, even, and polished to avoid damaging the organ when placed upon it, there is a crossbar made of copper or iron in its center where the candle with fire is placed. This scale can be made larger than described or smaller according to the diseases.
The shape of the instrument should be considered, for the scales used by boys or thin people are not the same as those used by men. The proper way to use this scale is to light a thread with fire from fine cotton or a small wax candle and place it on the center of the upright bar inside the scale.
The flame should be directed upwards towards the bottom of the scale to prevent the patient's body from burning, and then placed on the organ with a finger on the hole we mentioned, so that when we grasp the scale, the finger can be easily removed.
The steam emerges from the hole, causing the scale to relax on the member. The thread is then placed on the groove and repeated as needed. For the scale used in the case of water retention, it does not have a upright bar or hole.
It is used by filling it with water and placing it only on the affected area, and this is its form:
And the more this vessel is large enough to hold much water, the better it is.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids.
Hemorrhoids are used most often in areas where the application of hot compresses is not possible, either because they are too small, such as the lips and gums, or because the area is exposed and lacks flesh, like the finger and nose, among others.
The method of its use involves taking the fresh, uninfected hemorrhoids and leaving them in clean freshwater for a day and night until they are hungry and empty within. Then the body is purged either by lancing or cupping, and then the affected area is wiped.
Until it turns red, then apply it; if it fills up and falls off, it is effective. If not, wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water, massage and squeeze; if the bleeding persists after the hemorrhoid falls off, soak a cotton cloth in cold water.
Place a bandage from above to stop the bleeding, and if the blood is abundant, sprinkle it with a powder of glass or gum or similar constrictors until the bleeding ceases, or apply to the wound halves of the dried and peeled plant (basil).
Leave it until the basil sticks to the wound, then the bleeding will cease. If, however, it is necessary to repeat the leeching, do not use the same leech if possible; if the leech refuses to attach, the area should be wiped with fresh blood.
Alternatively, pierce the wound with a needle to draw out some blood, then apply it. If it feels adherent to the place, you can make it come off by sprinkling it with frankincense or salt or ash.
Third Chapter.
On fractures.
This chapter is also about the treatment of broken bones, including fractures and dislocations.
Know, O children, that this chapter is claimed by some as the sole authority for physicians and laypeople, yet there are those who have not studied a single word from the ancient texts on this subject.
For this reason, this art of science has become non-existent in our country, and I have never encountered a skilled practitioner in it. But I have only gained knowledge of it through lengthy study of the works of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them, until I extracted the science from them, then through experience and practice throughout my life.
I have therefore described for you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses on this subject. My experience with it, after I have made it clear for you and simplified it, removing the complexity and condensing it to its essence, and explaining it in detail, has included numerous illustrations of the instruments used in it.
Indeed, it is through increased explanation, as I have done in the previous chapters, that I have provided these details. There is no power but that of the Most High, the Great.
Chapter One.
In this chapter, which deals with the treatment of bone fractures and dislocations, it is appropriate to begin by presenting general principles before discussing specific broken or dislocated members. Initially, we should outline key statements and sections. You must first understand and grasp the truth of these principles if you are to learn this noble art, or teach it to others.
So I say, when a bone is broken, or a joint dislocated, or there is a wound or fall, you should first hasten to sever or relax it, or both. If there is no impediment, such as weakness of strength, or if the event occurred in a young child or an old and frail person, or at a time of extreme heat or extreme cold.
Then, his diet should be limited to cold legumes, poultry, and fowl, while avoiding drinks, hearty meats, overindulgence in food, and any meals that fill the veins with blood, until the swelling is alleviated and there is no expectation of effusion into the affected area.
Then, the patient should return to his initial diet according to his usual habits. If the broken bone is being set, the patient should consume foods that are rich, dense, and strong, such as porridge, rice, heads of grain, and beef bellies. Eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, among others, will aid in the speedy and effective healing of the fracture. Be aware that broken bones in strong men or old people never fully heal to their original state.
The dryness and hardness of their bones, and sometimes the softest bone can join and adhere to the hardest, as in the bones of young children, but nature generates on the broken bone from all sides a substance similar to glue, which thickens and adheres, thus uniting them.
They adhere to each other so that they become as strong and secure as they were originally, without anything hindering their functions.
For this reason, the patient's diet should include foods that are firm, sticky, and thick, as we have mentioned. Know that fractures vary in type depending on the member affected, for a fracture of the leg bone is not like a fracture of the skull bone, or a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone.
Similarly, all other members differ from one another, and we will mention each type of fracture, explaining it in detail in its respective section, without repetition. The type of bone fracture can also vary within itself.
Because it may be a fracture without chips, and it may be a fracture along the length of the bone with fragments that are either healing or unhealed, and the fracture may occur with a wound and tear in the skin, and it may be a slight crack.
Each type has a specific method of treatment, which will be detailed in its respective section, as follows: The recognition of a bone fracture is through its deformity, loss of function, sensitivity, and crepitus when touched with the hand.
When there is no visible deformity, nor any creaking or sensation of disturbance upon palpation of the bone, and the patient experiences little pain, it is not a fracture but may be a sprain, a slight break, or a thin crack. In such cases, it should not be moved by stretching or twisting under any circumstances.
Instead, apply to it the medications mentioned later, suitable for the condition, and then gently tighten it. Know that if a bone breaks and splits into two without causing fragments, its parts may still separate from each other.
You should promptly correct and set it straight before a severe swelling occurs, for if a severe swelling does develop, leave it for several days until the swelling subsides, then straighten it as best you can with kindness and ingenuity.
Know that setting and straightening it is easier than dealing with a bone that has fragments, then proceed as follows: if there are fragments, extend the broken member from both sides, whether it is a hand or a leg.
Either use your hands for a small member, or two ropes, or a rope and a hand, and place the member on a level surface in its natural shape. Once the broken parts of the bone have extended, then cut off the excess with whatever means you can, using all possible care and ingenuity.
Take care not to cause the patient pain or discomfort during your treatment, and strive to align one bone with its companion in the best manner possible. At this time, you should touch and feel them with your hand. If you find anything amiss, correct it and make it even as best as you can.
Beware of excessive stretching or forceful manipulation, as many ignorant people do, causing hot swelling or damage to the member, as you have often seen from their actions.
Then, after adjusting and perfecting the position, ensure that member remains still and at rest. Warn the patient to avoid moving it during wakefulness, sleep, transition, disturbance, or defecation, and strive for a posture that relieves pain.
If, while in a certain position, the patient feels pain or discomfort, they should change to a different posture where they are free from pain, and ensure that this new position is level and straight.
To prevent any deformation of the member if it is straightened, and regarding the method of binding a broken member, as I have described to you. Know that broken members vary in size and shape, such as the arm, finger, ulna, and others.
The bandages should be soft and flexible, and if they are thick, such as those for the thigh, back, or chest, they should be wide and rigid, since a broad strap holds a large member firmly from all sides without any gap.
Then, after you have finished adjusting, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. In a gentle tension over the fracture site, begin wrapping the bandage around the same area three or four times, depending on the size of the member, slightly tightening the bandage with each wrap, and then move it up to the upper side of the fracture.
With less tension than you applied to the fractured site, then slightly space out the wraps from the fracture and relax the tension little by little until you achieve a suitable position, taking a second bandage to wrap it around the broken place as well.
Continue wrapping, moving the bandage down to the lower side of the fracture. Follow the same procedure for tension and relaxation as with the first wrap around the upper side, and place a layer of soft padding or cloth between the wraps to help align and stabilize the fracture.
If there is an angle, do not add any padding; otherwise, wrap another bandage over the existing ones and secure with firm splints if the member is not swollen or hot to the touch.
If there is swelling or a hot tumor, apply what soothes that tumor and dispels the swelling, then leave it for some days before applying compression bandages. The bandages can be made from halves of wide bamboo strips carved and shaped wisely, or they can be made from grapevine wood.
which are made from cedar or palm fronds or juniper or heather, and other similar materials readily available, and each bandage should be of the same size and shape as shown.
Except that the bandage applied directly to the broken bone should be thicker and wider than the others, and the length of the bandage is made according to what suits the size of the broken member. The shape of the bandage is as follows:
Then, apply another strip of bandage according to the first tension, and bind it with strong threads as mentioned, ensuring more tension on the broken place. And the tension should be lessened as it gets farther from the broken bone.
The threads should be of medium thickness and softness, and it is best to use wet cotton threads specifically. The threads should not be too thick like those made from twisted jute strips, as this is a great mistake often committed by the ignorant.
Because pulling on it exceeds the moderate amount, and delicate threads are also unsuitable because you cannot achieve the desired tension with them, and there should not be less than a finger between the splints.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after proper tensioning, place beneath them soft padding or fluffy wool so that nothing harms him from it, and if the fracture is with a wound and there is a tear in the skin, we will mention it separately.
Know that not every broken member should be immobilized with splints from the first day, for if the member is large, splints should not be applied to it until five or seven days have passed, or more, according to your security from the hot swelling.
This is the description of the bandages that were used by the ancients to immobilize fractures, dislocations, and cracks: a general, concise dressing that sets the bone, suitable for most conditions, especially for children and women, as it is neither too hot nor too cold.
It is to take flour dust, the inner part of the wheat that adheres to the walls of the millstone when the grinder rotates, and knead it as it is, without sifting it with egg whites, making sure it is neither too thick nor too thin, then roll it out.
A last dressing for fractures, dislocations, and contusions: taken from sheep, lamb, acacia, raisin, alder, and clay, ten dirhams each. From the poppy and patience, five dirhams each. From olive or Armenian/Roman clay, twenty dirhams. All should be crushed and mixed with white of egg or mint water, if the patient's condition is hot. This dressing, when used, is good for composition, quickly mends broken bones, and is suitable for most people due to its moderation.
Another dressing that is also beneficial for fractures and contusions: take alder, poplar, and acacia, ten dirhams each. From the poppy and patience, five dirhams each. Armenian clay, twenty dirhams. Crush everything finely, sift it, and mix with water or egg white to use.
A dressing for joints and bones that have come out of their place, alleviating the pain and settling the cracking of bones and fractures: take woolen padding, immerse it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply to the affected area. This dressing is not powerful for mending.
However, it is excellent for alleviating edema and relieving pain, especially heat-related swelling. A final dressing that mends a broken bone: take papery olive leaves and wild poppy leaves, crush them together, and use as a wet dressing. Another concise dressing used when a bone has snapped: apply crushed leaves directly to the affected area.
And if you wish to analyze the remaining tumor, take a part from each of the original materials - chamomile, malva, violet, and fennel seed - making them all fine and mix with paint if the tissue is not fresh; if it is fresh, mix it with parsley water or water. Use it.
The stronger poultice, in the second instance, is used when a solid tumor occurs due to a broken bone. Take a part of each: chamomile, flaxseed, fennel, rosemary, lavender, and malva.
All should be ground and mixed with either parsley water, fresh water, or paint, depending on the temperature of the tissue and its state. Hippocrates did not mention in his book that it should be placed on a broken member when setting it.
However, Galen suggested that substances with astringent properties and some heat, such as pepper, alum, and resin, should be placed on the broken member during its setting.
A poultice that is beneficial for weakness and pain: take equal parts of licorice, chickpeas, human hair (cut), bird feathers, chamomile, and salt. Grind, sieve, mix, and apply as a compress. The amount of binding required depends on the size of the wound.
Then, if it heals, you should observe. If no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone occurs in the patient, do not treat it for many days. If such symptoms appear at the site, then treat it.
So, act swiftly and treat it at that moment, without delay. Remove the dressing, then take a soft cloth or sponge dipped in warm water and wash the affected area until the itching and pain subside. Leave the member to rest for an hour.
Then, apply a cloth soaked in vinegar and oil (or rose fat) and secure it with bindings for the night, to prevent swelling, relieve pain, and ease the member's discomfort. After that, reapply gentle bindings and light dressings, not as tightly as before.
Treat it gently until it heals, if the swelling, redness, pain, and other symptoms disappear completely, and you still need to use bindings and tight dressing, apply them as initially done. If none of the aforementioned symptoms occur, proceed as before.
Do not treat it until three, four, five, or seven days have passed, and sometimes even up to 20 days, depending on the patient's condition as described. Once the bone is healed and the flesh has closed around it.
Increase the tension at that time even more than your initial tension, and also increase the thickness of the food for the patient as mentioned earlier. If you find that the fracture site has dried out and become weaker than it should be, know that the food is prevented from reaching it.
Then, each time you apply it, immerse it in warm water and do this every three days, reducing the tension slightly. This action allows food to reach the member and promotes quick healing. On the other hand, what the ignorant do by breaking the member again is harmful.
For if it is not set right as it should be and is instead set in a crooked position, it is an error on their part and a great risk. If it were correct, they would have mentioned it in the first books and practiced it; but I have seen no trace of this among them. The correct course of action is not to follow it.
Chapter Two
On the Occurrence of Fractures in the Head.
There are many types of fractures occurring in the head, with various shapes and differing causes. Some fractures result from sword strikes and may completely sever the bone, extending to the abdominal cavity beneath the bone. As it does in wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called "coming-in" and it may be that the sword cuts through some of the bone, leaving its point only in the surface, and the cut does not penetrate to the end. This type of fracture is called a complete dislocation, and these fractures can be either large or small.
Some fractures are fragmented or crushed, caused by a blow with a stone or falling onto a stone or similar. These fractures can either penetrate closely to the membrane beneath the bone or affect the surface of the bone.
The size of these fractures can also be either large or small, and some fractures may be subtle, hidden within the bone, just visible as a thin split, known as a fine fracture.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called "hairy" (or "coarse"), and it may occur from a fall or a blow with a stone or similar, where the bone surface is penetrated into the interior, leaving a concave shape, much like a dent in copper when struck.
These conditions are most common in soft, growing bones, such as the heads of children's bones. All these types of fractures can have detached or adherent fragments, and we will discuss their treatment in detail in a later section, identifying each type through examination.
Inspection and removal of rotten flesh with probes, and for the hairy type, it is known by exposing the bone and wiping and marking it with ink; then the fracture appears black. As for the treatment of a fracture, first consider the symptoms of the patient.
If you observe symptoms that clearly indicate fear, such as vomiting bitter substances, restlessness, loss of consciousness, hoarseness, fainting, fever, redness and swelling of the eyes, and other similar symptoms, do not approach or treat the patient. In most cases, death is inevitable with these symptoms.
If you observe symptoms that do not cause severe concern and you hope for his safety, then proceed with treatment. If the wounded person comes to you soon after being injured, especially during the winter months, you should:
You should make every effort to remove the bone within 14 days, or even earlier in summer, before the tissue under the bone decays, as this may lead to the symptoms we described.
If the fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and there is crushing or bruising, you should cut off the damaged part as I have described, which involves shaving the head of the wounded patient.
Expose the bone in any way that is possible for you, depending on the shape of the wound and what eases the patient's discomfort. If, upon examining the bone, you see bleeding or a hot swelling, treat it as necessary.
It is to fill the wound with bandages soaked in a mixture of rose water and fat, then clean the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding stops, after which one proceeds to trim the bone and extract it, all of which can be done through one of two methods.
The single method is to cut the bone with a delicate, narrow blade, and this is its image: .
Then one uses another blade, slightly wider than the first, and its image is: .
Then one also uses another blade, wider than the previous one, and it is necessary to have several blades of varying width and sharpness, some longer and some shorter, preferably made of Indian steel or good quality steel.
And use gentle force when striking the blade, so as not to dislodge the head and cause harm, for if the bone is strong and hard, one should drill around it before using the blades with non-penetrating drills, which they call "non-grouting drills.
Because they do not extend beyond the skull bone, as the drill bit has a rounded tip similar to a ring or small circle below its sharp head, which prevents it from penetrating and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
You should choose from these drills a large number, each of which is suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have for every skull with a hole a drill whose sharp end in length and shortness corresponds to the thickness of that skull, and this is a picture of three types. From the drills, large, medium, and small.
As for the method of drilling around a fractured bone, you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you feel that the bone has been penetrated, then move the drill to another location and make holes at intervals corresponding to the thickness of the rod or similar.
Then, you cut with the saws between each hole as much as possible with kindness, as we have said, until you remove the bone either by hand or with other tools prepared for that purpose.
Like pincers and gentle tweezers, you must be extremely careful not to let the drill or saw touch the peritoneum. If you successfully strengthen the bone and separate it from the peritoneum, and extract it, then you should clean it.
You smooth the roughness of what remains in the bone with another tool similar to a saw, but it should be thinner and gentler than other saws. If there are any remaining small bones or fragments, you carefully remove them with the tools at your disposal.
Then, the wound is treated subsequently with tourniquets and ointments as mentioned, while the other aspect of the work concerns what is easy to perform and free from risk, as described by Galen, who praised it greatly, saying: You should first begin by exposing the portion of the bone in the location where the fracture is most severe and prominent, and once you have exposed that part, place the tip of this lens-shaped fragment underneath it. And here is its image:
The lens-shaped fragment should be smooth, not cutting anything, and the sharp part on its sides, when extended in length, as you can see, is to rest against the peritoneum, and the face of the sharp fragment against the bone; then strike on the fragment from one side.
With a small hammer, cut all the bone gently as it rotates, and be secure from the peritoneum; it will not cause any frightening incident at all. Even if the healer is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he is asleep.
If anything remains necessary for the bone from the peritoneum in some locations of the bone, scrape it off with the same lens-shaped fragment, and remove it gently; it will come off without harm or fear.
And if the bone fracture did not penetrate the peritoneum, and what broke off from the bone has a rough surface with fine fragments remaining in it, you should smooth that roughness and remove those fragments using delicate forceps; prepare a set of different sizes from these forceps, so that you can use the most suitable and appropriate one for each location as guided by the work.
On the Same Bone and Its Shape: You should first examine the bone using the widest of knives, then use a narrower one after that, continuing in this manner until you reach the most precise and finest among them all.
As for other minute cracks and delicate breaks, they should be treated according to what leads to their healing, which is something evident to anyone with even a slight experience in this field, and by referring to what we have written and explained regarding major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, take a piece of cotton cloth suitable for the wound and dip it in rose fat, then place it on the wound. Then take another cloth, folded or cut into a triangle shape. Dip it in a drink and rose fat, then place it on the first cloth, doing so as gently as possible to avoid weighing down the peritoneum. Then use a wide bandage and only tighten it enough to secure the wound.
Leave it with this treatment for one or two days until the hot swelling subsides, then remove the bandage and use medications with a drying effect, such as roots of coltsfoot, flour of horseradish, ground myrrh, turmerick, and similar herbs, by making powders from these medications.
Treat the wound while it is still dry, and use a medicine that clarifies without causing irritation. During treatment, strive for cleanliness, ensuring no fat, dirt, or pus accumulates.
Since pus, if it accumulates on the brain membrane, corrupts and spoils it, causing great harm to the patient. It may appear in the skull's dura mater when the bone becomes exposed, especially if treatment is neglected.
If you see blackness on its surface, and the patient exhibits the symptoms we mentioned, know that death is inevitable. Even if the blackness occurred due to a medication applied, that medicine was strong enough to cause this blackness.
One should take a portion of honey and three parts of rose fat, mix them well, apply the mixture to the dura mater, and then treat it with all the necessary remedies until healing occurs.
Chapter III
Treating a broken nose.
Know that the nose is broken when both upper cracks (or one) are affected, as they are bones. The lower part is cartilage and does not break, but it may bend, become deformed, or suffer from pressure.
If one of the sides breaks, the little finger should be inserted into the nasal hole and that fracture should be set from inside with the index finger and thumb from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape, and this should be done gently, taking care not to cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger did not reach it, it should be set with a slight bend at its end, and if the fracture is on both sides, do the same, and seek to correct it on the very first day of the fracture.
If possible, and otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then a thread of linen should be inserted into the nasal hole if the fracture is on one side, or two threads if the fracture is on both sides.
The threads should be of a thickness that fills the nasal hole, and some early surgeons recommended greasing them with lard and changing them every day, although I do not agree with this. Instead, I suggest soaking the threads in a paste made from egg whites and flour ground into a powder.
Then, the threads should be left until the bone heals and the cartilage hardens. After this, pipes made from duck feathers can be inserted into the nose where the threads were, wrapped in soft cloth, to keep them in place, making it easier for the patient to breathe.
And if you wish, you can make them (bandages), even if in the course of your work a hot swelling appears on the nose, then bandage the nose with pitch or a cotton swab soaked in vinegar and rose oil or any other dye from the diakonion ointment.
If no hot swelling appears, then it should be bandaged from the outside with fine semolina and ground myrrh, mixed with egg white, then place a soft pad on top, but do not tie the nose at all, for if the nasal bones break, they should not be bound.
Or if they break, you should make an incision and remove them with the appropriate tool, then sew up the cut and treat it with suitable healing ointments, for if there is a wound inside the nose, it should be treated with bandages and lead pipes. until it heals.
Chapter IV
In the case of broken lower jawbones.
If the lower jawbone breaks and there is no wound, and it is broken from outside without splitting into two pieces and does not collapse inward, then its condition is clear.
If the fracture is in the right side, the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth, and likewise, if the fracture is on the left side, the index finger of the right hand should be used to lift the deformity gently from inside out.
With your other hand, from outside the bone, you control its alignment. If the jaw fracture is split in two, it should be straightened by applying pressure from both sides until it is corrected. If it affects the teeth, they should be extracted.
If the wound does not heal with a thread of gold, silver, or silk, then apply pitch to the broken jaw, place a folded cloth over it, and use a large, tight splint or a piece of leather from a horse's hoof that is the same length as the jaw. Then, secure it from above as best you can, ensuring the binding is tight to prevent disruption. Order the patient to rest and maintain a calm demeanor. Provide soft porridge as food. If, after three days, you notice any change in the shape, immediately untie it.
Then, secure it from above as best you can, ensuring the binding is tight to prevent disruption. Order the patient to rest and maintain a calm demeanor. Provide soft porridge as food. If, after three days, you notice any change in the shape, immediately untie it. Then, prepare a new dressing with flour from a mortar, mixed with egg white or fine semolina, after removing the pitch. Place a soft pad over the wound and secure it with gentle bindings. As long as the dressing adheres well and there is no change in the bone's condition, leave it intact.
Do not reduce it until the fracture heals and the bone strengthens. For this fracture often becomes more severe within three weeks, if a hot swelling appears during that time, use what we have mentioned repeatedly to soothe it until that swelling disappears.
If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a fragment or fragments of bone have separated, be gentle in removing them, using whatever means are appropriate for their extraction from the body. If the opening of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as much as necessary.
Then, if you have removed the fragments and none remain, outline the wound with a line if it is wide, or apply one of the appropriate ointments to it. And heal the wound until it heals.
Chapter V.
On the treatment of a broken collarbone.
The collarbone (clavicle) most often breaks in the front, near its end at the shoulder joint, and it can break in one of three ways: either it cracks without splitting into pieces, or it breaks into two pieces, or it shatters into multiple fragments. The first case is the easiest to treat.
If the fracture involves fragments, which is more difficult to set, or if it occurs with a wound, when dealing with a fracture without a wound, one should have two assistants: one to hold the humerus next to the broken scapula, and the other to stretch the neck towards the opposite side.
Then, align the fracture with your fingers until it assumes the desired shape and there is no displacement or deformity; if more extension is required, place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit.
The bone should be manipulated as needed, stretching and lifting the scapula while pressing the ball with your hand to align the fracture correctly. If it is impossible to draw the edge of the scapula outward due to its depth, the patient should lie on their back.
Place a pillow under the patient's arm, and have an assistant press the scapula downward to lift the deeply situated bone above; at this point, you can align and correct the fracture using your fingers.
If you sense that a fragment of the scapula has broken off and is moving, you should carefully drill into it to remove the fragment; if the fragment is trapped within the bone, use one of the prepared cutting tools to free it. After becoming under the axilla is the mechanism that preserves the peritoneum, which is a device made of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a handle, and its width should be according to the size of the bone, whether large or small, and its length should also be according to what is suitable for work. It should have two ends; one end is wider, and the other is narrower.
If the wound tear that you made when removing the shard of bone is wide and you have ensured the hot tumor, then close the wound with stitches. If the tear is slight or you fear the hot tumor, then widen the wound with more cuts and bandages according to the extent of the tear.
If there is a hot tumor, moisten the wound with rose fat and vinegar or wine, and apply it. For severe bone fractures without wounds or cuts, cover the bone with a compress made from flour and egg white. Place a soft pad on it, then place a ball under his armpit if necessary, then take a long cloth, about a span wide, and wrap it around the padding, bandage, and splint, then take a thin board as a splint. It should be three fingers wide and long, then insert it into a cloth, wrap the cloth around the splint and the wound, and place it in the position of the wrap on the fracture, then tighten the wrap around the fracture as you would spin. Position the splint along his neck, under the healthy armpit and under the affected armpit, and repeat this process on both sides. As you can see, the tension secures the fracture firmly, which is evident.
And it is imperative that the splint not be removed from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day. If the bandage relaxes and the splint falls off, tighten the bandage and then have the patient sleep on their back with a small pillow under their armpit at night.
To raise the arm, have the patient rest it against their side, which will elevate the broken shoulder to the level of the shoulder joint. If no complications such as itching or swelling occur within twelve days, do not loosen the bandage.
Then, renew the bandage if necessary. Apply tension and maintain it until the fracture heals and solidifies, strengthening and gaining more power within twenty-eight days. This may occur in some people in less time.
Chapter VI.
In the treatment of a fractured shoulder, if the shoulder is not broken at its broadest point but only at its edges, then when a fracture occurs in one place or in the middle, it can only be determined by touch. According to the shape of the fracture, the treatment will vary.
After reducing the fracture and returning it to its natural position using all available means, apply a layer of powder from a mortar along with egg white and soft linen bandages. Place a supporting pad made from a folded cloth on top, then cover it with a wide splint made from a board.
Make the splint thin, covering the entire shoulder or slightly wider. If there is a protrusion under the splint from a fracture in one of the shoulder's places, correct it using a soft linen bandage until the splint lies flat, then secure it with a long cloth wrap.
Tightly secure the splint and ensure it remains in place, checking the bandage daily. If it loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint. The patient should lie on their healthy side, and the shoulder will heal in approximately 20 days.
After 20 or 25 days, if this period is complete, release the bandage with confidence, for it is a bone that is unlikely to break or splinter further. If a fragment of bone protrudes and presses against the skin, make an incision and extract it. Make what is mentioned regarding the treatment of the broken clavicle in case of a hot tumor, if such occurs.
Chapter VII
In the treatment of broken ribs.
The chest may break in the middle, but this is rare; more commonly, it breaks at its extremities, which shatter and fracture easily. Among its symptoms, if the chest breaks in the middle, it tilts downward, causing severe pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, and distress.
And if blood gushes out, the deformity of the broken bone becomes evident to the touch; it must be set by having the patient lie on his back, placing a cushion between his shoulders, pressing the elbows, and bringing the ribs together with the hands from both sides, while softening its positioning as much as possible.
And once the bone returns to its proper form, apply a splint and bandage over it, placing a light wooden splint, such as willow or hemlock, wrapped in cloth over it. Then, gently secure the bandage to the broken bone to prevent its displacement; wrap the bandage around the back in a circular motion multiple times and tighten it firmly. Check the bandage regularly and tighten it whenever it loosens.
If a wound or pain or swelling arises and requires treatment, address it immediately, remove the dressing, treat what has arisen with regard to its cure, and then replace the dressing if you deem it appropriate. Apply pressure and compress it until it heals.
Chapter VIII
In the case of a broken rib, know that fractures typically occur in the stiff parts adjacent to the back or at the ends from the front, due to their cartilaginous nature. This is easily discerned through physical examination with the fingers.
Correct it by aligning the break with the fingers on the strong portion until the shape is as it should be, then bandage and secure the broken bone with a splint if necessary. If the ribs are fractured inward, it can cause severe pain for the patient.
And the puncture, like a puncture that occurs in those with a hernia, as the bone punctures the peritoneum, and it also causes difficulty breathing, coughing, and spitting up large amounts of blood, which is difficult to treat. The ancients used many tricks for this.
Some said that the patient's diet should include foods that produce gas and wind to inflate the abdomen, allowing the fracture to push outward. We dislike this method, as it may cause or worsen an internal heat swelling. If it does not occur, then it might lead to its development, exacerbating the condition.
Some say that a cup should be applied to the site and then sucked out strongly, similar to the previous method, except that there is a concern that the cup might attract curiosity to the weak area. Some suggest that the site should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
The dressing should be placed between the ribs until it fills, making the bandage even when rolled into a circle, then treat the patient with food and medicine as for a broken bone; if the injury is severe, the patient cannot bear it. The bone was piercing the peritoneum painfully, and we were concerned for the patient, so we should make an incision at the site, expose the broken rib, and place under it the device that preserves the peritoneum, as described previously, and carefully cut and remove the bone.
Then, if the wound is large, sew it shut and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling appears, hurry to soak the dressings in rose oil and apply them to the site, also treating the patient internally to alleviate the swelling.
He should lie on the side that makes it easier for him to sleep until he heals.
Chapter IX
In the treatment of fractures of the back and neck.
If the bones of the neck are fractured, and such an injury is rare, it is often due to impact or pressure, and similarly, the vertebrae of the back should be considered in the same manner. If you encounter such a case, to determine whether the patient will recover or not. Examine their hands; if you find them limp, numb, and lifeless, with no ability to move or grasp, and if pricking or poking them with a needle causes no sensation or pain, then know that recovery is unlikely in most cases, for they are destined to perish.
If they can move their hands and feel when pricked or poked, indicating that the spinal cord is unharmed, and if the patient recovers with treatment, then knowing whether the same applies to fractures of the back, you should understand that recovery is indeed possible.
Examine his legs; if they are weak and show similar symptoms to the hands, and if laying on his back causes gas or feces to escape involuntarily, or turning onto his stomach makes urine leak out uncontrollably, then the recovery process is complex. If he lies on his back and cannot urinate, it is a sign of impending death, so do not bother with treatment. If none of these symptoms occur, the condition is less severe. Treatment involves alleviating the intense swelling.
By applying rose fat alone or, alternatively, fried egg whites to the affected sinus three times a day, one can alleviate the hot swelling. Once the inflammation subsides, apply a strengthening and drying dressing to the area, secure it with a bandage, and instruct the patient to remain still and at rest.
He should not sleep on the affected side until the pain disappears. If a fracture or something that has come loose involves the bone, it is necessary to incise the skin and extract the bone. Then, if the wound is large, close its edges with stitching, and treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx (the tailbone) breaks, insert the little finger of the left hand into the anus.
Adjust the broken bone with the other hand as best you can, then apply a dressing and a splint if necessary, and secure it tightly.
If you feel a broken fragment inside, incise the skin and extract it, then treat the wound as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Ten
In setting a hip fracture.
If the bones of the hip do not break, or if they do break, it is only that they crack at their extremities and split along their length, inclining inward, and cause pain in the affected area for the patient, numbness, and paralysis of the leg from which the fracture occurred.
It should be manipulated with your hand to assess the fracture and its position. If the fracture is only at the extremities, adjust it as best as you can to resemble its natural shape.
If the fracture is along its length or has inclined inward, lay the patient on their stomach to manipulate the fracture. Once adjusted, apply a bandage and then place a splint made of wood or leather, securing it tightly.
Do not be concerned about the fracture moving or the splint coming off, and correct any indentation in the hips using a filler to ensure equal pressure. Order the patient to sleep on their back or their healthy side, and if a hot swelling occurs...
Cease to extend or manipulate the fracture until the hot swelling subsides, and apply what has been mentioned to soothe it. Then return to setting and securing the splint as appropriate. If any fragments or fragmentation from its extremities are visible on the bone, they should not be removed.
It should not be touched from within, but only smoothed from without, and its tension should be left until it heals.
Chapter XI.
In the setting of a humeral fracture.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it breaks, it can be set in one of two ways: by using a curved, smooth stick of moderate thickness, as illustrated.
Both ends of the stick should be tied with bindings, then it is hung from a high place. The patient sits on a chair and places their broken arm on the stick, so that their armpit rests in the middle of the stick's curve. Then a heavy object is hung above it.
Or a servant can extend it downwards, and the physician can adjust the fracture with both hands, ensuring it aligns properly. The alternative method is to have the patient lie on their back, and hang their hand from their neck with a binding.
Then command two servants: one to hold the upper part of the fracture with both hands, and the other to hold the lower part. If you wish for a stronger pull, tighten bindings beneath and above the fracture, and have each servant extend their arm towards their respective direction.
If the fracture is close to the side of the shoulder joint, the bandage should be placed under the armpit and the other end under the fracture towards the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, the bandage should be applied at that point and also around the elbow itself.
Then, the fracture should be gently adjusted without force until it is aligned as required and united well. If there is no sign of fever, it should be immobilized. If a fever occurs, do not immobilize it until the seventh day, applying a compress soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Once the swelling has subsided, then immobilize it. The procedure involves placing a bandage over the fracture, covering it with a new cloth wrap, and then wrapping the arm around the elbow with the hand facing downward towards the shoulder.
Carry the cloth and tighten it around the upper arm and forearm, allowing the arm to act as a splint if there are no obstructions and the fractured bone does not change position. If you fear that the fracture may shift, use traditional splints instead.
This involves placing a splint directly on the fracture, which should be wider and stronger than other splints. There should be a space of one finger width between each splint, and the length of the splints should correspond to the fracture, with an additional three fingers' worth of material on each side.
Then, apply the tension to the bandages as mentioned in the introduction of this book.
And it should be that your tension on the fracture site is greater.
And the more distant the fracture, the less tension should be applied; and when you apply the bandages and tension as we have said in your time of setting the member, then do so.
And if you fear the formation of a hot swelling, then leave off the tension and bandages until the seventh day, as we have said; then examine the dressing every three days to ensure that there is no itching, bulging, or blockage of food reaching the member due to excessive tension, so that all these issues may be rectified as we have mentioned.
If you are certain that none of these issues will occur, then do not remove the dressing for a long time; and the patient should lie on his back, with his hand on his stomach and placed under the arm a padded cushion filled with wool, moderate in thickness.
Examine at all times, day or night, to ensure that the shape of the fractured member does not change or that the bandage does not become loose; and rectify all these issues to the best of your ability, and provide the patient's food according to the order we have given, that it be soft initially, so that when the bone begins to harden.
It is necessary to thicken his diet, for the arm and leg usually harden in forty days, at which time they should be loosened and a bath taken, and treatment with ointments suitable for this purpose, if the fracture is severe and dislocated.
Do not loosen the bandage for fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve
In the Reduction of Arm Fractures.
The arm consists of two bones, called the humeri, one smaller than the other, which is adjacent to the thumb, and the other larger, placed below the smaller one. It is possible for the larger humerus to break alone, or the smaller one, or both together.
When the smaller upper humerus breaks, its reduction is easier and recovery faster. However, when the lower humerus breaks, the fracture is more severe and healing slower. The worst case is when both bones break together; if the bone attached to the smaller humerus breaks, the prognosis is better.
When reducing the fracture, the doctor should make it easy and gentle to align the bones. If the larger humerus is broken, the stretch should be more severe; if both humeri are broken, the stretch should be much stronger.
The hand should be positioned when setting and extending it on a cushion, with the thumb above all the fingers and the little finger below all the others, while the patient is seated in a comfortable position.
Let the cushion be at the same height as his face to avoid any discomfort for the patient.
Then, the assistant extends the arm from below either by hand or with a strap, and another assistant extends it from above as well. The doctor then adjusts the bone until it is in the best possible shape, crushing any shrapnel if there are fragments in a broken bone.
Crush each fragment back into its place as much as possible. If a fragment is protruding and causing skin irritation but cannot be reduced, make an incision and extract it as described previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate chapter has been dedicated to it.
If, while you are initially setting the bone, a hot swelling appears, apply a cloth soaked in a mixture of rose oil and white wax, medium between thickness and thinness, and gently compress it until the swelling subsides.
Remove the cloth and apply a dressing made from flour with egg white. Then, prepare splints, making sure that the one placed directly on the fracture is slightly wider and stronger. Note that in most cases, six splints will be required for the arm.
When the fracture is in one clavicle or in both, then make your grip on the site of the fracture stronger and more intense. And whenever you rotate the tension upwards or downwards, make the tension slightly looser as mentioned earlier at the beginning of this section.
And let the bandages wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not too stiff. The thread used for tightening should be of special linen, medium between softness and hardness, as described earlier. Check the member and ligament after several days.
If anything needs to be corrected, such as a itching in the member, it should be soaked in warm water until the itching subsides. The member should then be left unstrained for the night to rest, and strain should be resumed afterward.
If the tension has loosened and the bone has shifted or something similar, use all your effort to correct it. Also, check if food is unable to reach the member due to excessive tension; in that case, loosen it slightly and leave it for several days until nutrition reaches it, then tighten it again.
If the patient does not experience any of what we mentioned, no treatment should be initiated until after 20 days or so. Then, hang the patient's affected arm from their neck, keeping it at a moderate length and conserving their strength from abrupt movements. They should sleep on their back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will heal in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps even in twenty-eight days; all of this depends on the conditions of the humors and the strength.
Chapter XIII.
In setting a broken hand and fingers.
The metacarpal bone of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers are rarely broken, but they are often bruised. If there is a fracture or bruising to the hand, it is as follows.
The patient should sit cross-legged, with a level chair in front of them.
Then he places his hand on it, extends it, and the osteopath straightens the broken bone until it is properly aligned. If there is no swelling or heat, then a splint should be applied over it as needed.
It should be placed in a soft cloth, and if the fracture is towards the palm, make a semi-sphere of cloth and order the patient to grasp it with their broken hand, then secure it with a long piece of cloth.
The splint should be made of soft leather that conforms to the bend of the hand and holds it in place, especially when the fracture protrudes. In such cases, a splint should be applied above and below the hand to keep it open and extended.
Then the tension should be carried as the hand rotates, and the fingers should be interlocked with bindings. If the fracture affects one of the phalanges, the thumb should be positioned as needed, then secured along with the hand. If desired, a small, separate splint can be used to stabilize the fracture.
If the fracture does not shift and involves any of the other fingers (middle, index, ring, or pinky), they should be aligned and bound together with the adjacent healthy finger, or all secured in order, which is preferable. Alternatively, a small, separate splint can be used to stabilize them.
As mentioned earlier, with regard to the thumb, inspect it during the splinting process and ensure it is freed from excessive heat swelling. Adjust and apply the necessary treatments as described previously.
Chapter XIV
In Splinting the Hip Fracture.
The femur bone often breaks and is easily felt due to its movement forward and backward. Its splinting involves securing a strap above the fracture and another below, with the patient lying on their face.
Then, each servant tie should be extended to its side with moderation if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the origin of the thigh, a soft tie made of wool or similar material should be tightened.
If the fracture is at the origin of the thigh, extending up to the groin, then one tie should be above and another below the fracture; likewise, if it is near the knee, the tie should be close to the knee, extending downwards. Then the doctor should adjust the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape.
The bone will heal well when, in the absence of swelling, it is bandaged and compressed. If there is a hot swell, leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then resume treatment.
As for compression, a broad and hard bandage should be wound around the fracture twice or three times, leaving some excess. Then, the leg should be twisted until the heel is at the origin of the thigh, and a long thread should be inserted between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, with the ends of the thread extending above from both sides.
Then, the remaining bandage should be wrapped around the leg and thigh, forming a single brace on the thigh at the fracture site. The space between the thigh and leg should be filled with soft cloth to ensure equal compression.
Then begin to tighten the strong ligature from the middle to the fracture site, three or four times, and as you move away from the fracture with the bandage, your tension should be less and more gentle and loose.
Then, proceed to the ends of the thread that you had inserted between the thigh and the leg, and tie it to the subsequent bandages above, then pass the ends of the thread downward until it ends at the heel of the man, and also tighten the ends of the bandages on the other side with them.
To prevent the bandage from slipping out of its position, leave the tension on it as long as there is no pain, swelling, or inflammation in the member. If any such thing occurs, hurry to untie it and rectify all that has happened, as we have mentioned many times before.
If there is a splinter in the bone that protrudes, you should smooth it out if possible, otherwise cut it off and extract it, then treat the wound as mentioned before until it heals. This fracture of the thigh may be secured without adding the leg with bandages.
As we mentioned regarding the humerus and arm, except that its treatment in this way does not expose the patient to a limp. However, if only the upper arm is broken without including the leg, it is certain that the patient will always walk with a limp.
Know that the thigh will stiffen for fifty days or a little more or less, depending on the variation of humors and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the fracture of the knee joint.
The knee joint is rarely subject to fracture, but it may be bruised frequently; if a fracture does occur, it is either a tear or a dislocation of its parts, with or without a wound.
All of this must be done by touch, and it should be reduced by gently manipulating the separated parts with the fingers until they come together and heal, according to what is possible with skill, caution, and precision. Then, a bandage should be applied and a rounded splint should be used if necessary.
The tension should be tightened according to the appropriate tension, and then all conditions described for other fractures, such as hot swelling and so on, should be treated by addressing each occurrence with what is suitable until healing occurs.
Chapter XVI.
On the reduction of a leg fracture.
The leg has two bones, one thick, called the shin bone, and the other thin, called the fibula, and they suffer from types of fractures similar to those of the arm bones. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is one and the same. If both bones are broken, the leg turns in all directions. If the thinner bone breaks, the leg turns forward. If the thicker bone breaks from below, this is clear to you.
Therefore, use extension and compression, and apply splints accordingly, except that if the leg fracture is severe with numerous fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and be careful in its treatment.
In the leg, there is an additional step in the process: if you have finished adjusting the splints and all your work, take two strips of pine wood used for flattening rooms placed between board gaps, or palm frond papers, or similar.
Select strips that are slightly thick and not too thin, with a length corresponding to the leg from the knee to below. Then, wrap each one with two turns of cloth along its length, placing one on the inside and another on the outside of the leg.
These should extend from the knee to below the foot, and then tie the strips in three places on each side and in the middle. With this strapping, the leg is prevented from tilting right or left and is shaped well. A wooden splint of corresponding length along the leg can also be used.
And it should be placed to preserve it from movement, and this is most necessary in the case of a fracture with a special wound, then inspect the leg every day and take care of it carefully regarding swelling or puffiness or any other issues. If such a thing happens, treat it as necessary until it heals, and the leg bone will heal in about thirty days or so.
Chapter XVII
On the fracture of leg and finger bones.
As for the heel, it never suffers a fracture, while the leg bones can suffer fractures, and the fingers even less often, as they are more susceptible to bruising in most cases.
If a leg bone suffers a fracture and you see that the bones have shifted, the patient should put their foot on the ground straight like walking, then you place your foot on the elevated bone and press it down, smoothing it out.
Until they return to their proper positions, then take the dressing and bandage, placing a small board with two flat ends under the foot, after wrapping it with cloth and using all necessary materials.
If three or four days have passed, then release the bandage. You will find the bones either levelled after being broken, or reunited after having been separated. The procedure is as you see fit. If some of the fingers are broken, set them and straighten them according to the method described for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then, make a splint for the broken finger that extends along the finger and is slightly wider, then place this board beneath the foot as described to you and secure it tightly. If the broken bones number two, three, or more.
Make a splint for each finger, graded in size and placed in a soft cloth, then secure the board at the bottom of the foot. Let each head of the board rest outside the sole of the foot to ensure proper adjustment.
You must not forget to attend to all that has been mentioned for the various types of fractures, and meet each condition with what is appropriate.
Chapter XVIII
In this chapter, the text discusses fractures specific to women (fractures of the hip and pubic bone) and men (fractures of the groin area).
When the vagina of a woman is fractured, have her sit up and then gently bend her towards her back. The midwife should grasp her from behind and insert cotton into the vagina until it fills and becomes shaped like a ball within her.
Then the woman should be shaken and her body slightly raised, so that this cotton comes out little by little until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the vulva and the bone fracture heals. Then she should put a pad on her back. Whenever she wants to urinate, she should remove the cotton gently.
Then return it to the position you initially placed it, and go back to your first pad. Do this for about seven days or so, and it will heal. If you wish to use a goat's bladder, tighten a tube around its opening, insert the entire bladder through her vulva.
Then blow into the tube strongly, so that the bladder inside the vulva expands. The fracture will heal. Then stuff it with cotton and maintain this position for several days as described until it heals. For a broken pubic bone in men or women, this method can also be used for healing.
You should treat it as we described for the hip bone; the correct approach is evident to anyone familiar with this rare type of fracture, especially those with some experience and understanding of this book. The fracture itself often provides clues in most cases.
Follow the correct procedure for its repair and stabilization. If a man's penis fractures, insert a piece of an ostrich's throat into it, then place a wrap of cloth around it, secure it, and leave it for about three days or so until it heals.
Chapter XIX.
In bone fracture reduction when accompanied by a wound.
For anyone who has a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the femur, ulna, or similar, one should act promptly to reduce the fracture as soon as the conditions for reduction are met, as we have previously discussed.
If the wound is bleeding, one should promptly apply crushed ash to stop the bleeding, if none is available, and then proceed with reducing the fracture on that same day, without delaying.
If a hot swelling does not occur, but a warm one does, leave the reduction until the ninth day, so that the warm swelling subsides, and do not touch it on the third and fourth days, for it will then manifest bad symptoms.
If the fractured bone is exposed through the skin, one should attempt to reduce and set it with their hands gently and a slight pull, but if this is not possible, use this tool, which is made of iron, about seven or eight fingers long. And position it according to the size of the wound.
Therefore, the surgeon should choose three or four, according to the need for treatment in each type of fracture, and they should be rounded with a slight thickness to prevent them from bending when pressure is applied during surgery.
It should have a sharp point with a bend at the tip, and the upper part should be thick, while the lower half is very thin, and this is its appearance: .
It is called in Greek "perim" meaning a small hook, and it should be used to bend the sharp end onto the edge of the bone fragment, then push with one swift motion to realign the broken bones. Once some stability is achieved, a file can be used to smooth out the edges of the fracture.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, one should cut off the end of the bone so that the tool can grasp it. If the bone cannot be aligned as described above, then it should be cut with a similar cutting tool mentioned earlier.
Or use a saw to cut it as best you can, then remove any remaining roughness and thin layers from the bone. If, after realigning the bone, the patient experiences severe pain, indicating that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can successfully realign the bone to its natural position, you will greatly benefit the patient. Once the bone healing is complete, dip a cloth in a contracting, black, special solution, especially during summer, and do not place any bandages or greasy substances on the wound.
To prevent putrefaction and corruption, then use bindings when you have finished setting the bone, and leave the wound exposed by making a hole in the bandages with scissors, proportionate to the size of the wound.
And be extremely careful not to tighten the wound with the fracture, for this has often been done by ignorant physicians, causing either death, gangrene, or a severe cold. Let your tightening be gentle and relaxing, contrary to the tightness of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or it is a large wound and you fear some of the adverse symptoms we described, and there is pain in the area that is disturbing, then do not apply bindings to it, and make for it bandages from strong cloth in the place of the bindings and tighten it with these.
Then, after a day or two, if you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth with which it was covered using a drink (presumably a liquid), then use ligatures and ointments as we usually treat wounds, such as the quadriple ointment and similar ones. You should also loosen the binding.
Examine the wound every morning and evening until it heals, and you should position the limb so that the pus can drain easily downwards. If many days pass and the wound does not close, and the pus does not stop, then know that there are small fragments of bone.
It is necessary to examine the wound with a probe. If any of the fragments are protruding, extract and remove them. If some are not protruding but press on the member and cause pain, crush and extract them in every way you can.
If the wound presents a cold, inflammation, or any other form of corruption and putrefaction, it is necessary to apply to each of these manifestations the corresponding treatment described in its chapter.
And among what you should pay attention to and keep in mind: if a large bone breaks and is pressed against a member, such as the femur, ulna, or other large members, do not attempt to pull it out or remove it. Often, this leads to death. Leave it until it putrefies; perhaps it will fall off on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then, treat the wound if you see a place for treatment, otherwise leave it be.
Chapter Twenty.
On the treatment of complications that occur as a result of some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially near joints, disfiguring the member and possibly preventing it from performing its natural function.
If the swelling is soft, use drugs that contract, such as frankincense, myrrh, gallnut, asafoetida, and acacia, either alone or in combination, mixed with a contracting drink, egg white, or vinegar.
If the swelling is hard, apply pressure in a firm manner and maintain it for several days without releasing it, then release it and reapply pressure as needed until the swelling subsides, or use a tight plate of lead.
Lead has the property to dissolve all that forms in the members. If the swelling has become hardened, and pressure makes it necessary to remove it, make a cut from above and remove the resulting fragment or scrape it with scissors until it disappears, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of fractures, if the bone heals straight and remains soft, unlike its original state.
If a bone fracture heals with the member remaining soft and weak, it may be due to numerous reasons. One of the reasons is either loosening the ligatures excessively and binding them improperly, or over-tightening the ligatures to the extent that nutrition is blocked from reaching the member, or excessive rest, or inappropriate movements at the wrong time, or lack of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
Treatment for this includes nourishing the patient, enriching his body until blood increases within it, using baths, and bringing joy and happiness to him, then applying pitch to the affected area to attract a great deal of nourishment to it or prolong its healing with warm water. until it absorbs the nourishment and returns to its natural state.
Chapter Twenty-Second.
In the treatment of broken bones that are bent and prevented from functioning as they should.
When a member that has been set after its deformation or the curve or complication of the broken bone has healed, and as a result, the shape of the member is distorted. Except if the member continues to perform its natural function, one should not accept the claim of those who assert that the member must be broken from the head, as many ignorant doctors and carpenters in our country have done so.
This action is highly condemned and leads to great danger; its least consequence is injury. However, if the deformity and complication are still fresh, it should be bathed in water heated with relaxing herbs such as spinach leaves, nettle roots, and laurel crown, among others.
It is treated with soothing astringents such as well-made diachylon, or saliva from the root of the plant is mixed with chicken fat and sheep tallow and applied as a poultice, or ripe figs are pounded with pigeon droppings and other drugs known as fibrin dissolvers.
The clot dissolves with gentle, continuous friction applied by the hands, and the movement of the affected part is utilized at all times. If the deformity has become advanced, severe, and rigid, and requires treatment with iron.
It is necessary to make an incision above it, release the attachment of the bone, and cut what remains of the clot or bone with delicate cuts, using gentleness and care in this process. Then, treat the wound as mentioned earlier until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The mandible, when it deviates from its joint and moves out of place, obstructs movement, disfigures the member, and causes severe pain and suffering to the patient. Therefore, as soon as any deviation in one of the jaws occurs, it is necessary to promptly restore it to its proper position.
It should not be delayed at all, for if swelling makes it difficult to return the jaw to its proper position, then the delay will cause spasms and severe pain for the patient. Therefore, one should not delay or move or stretch it during the time of its swelling.
However, if this occurs, one should promptly proceed to drain the patient's illness, then leave until the swelling subsides somewhat, after which the affected member is bathed with hot water and fat, then returned gently, and each member is treated as mentioned in its respective place.
The chapters on the jaw have also been arranged according to the preceding discussion of fractures, from the upper part of the body to its lower parts.
Chapter XXIV
In the treatment of the lower jaw (fissure of the mandible).
It is rare that the jaws become completely dislodged, and their detachment occurs in one of two ways: either they move slightly out of place, relaxing somewhat, or they are completely and totally dislodged, also resulting in relaxation. It is directed towards the chest, causing the patient to drool and unable to hold it or close their jaw, with their tongue twisting during speech. If the dislocation is minor, in most cases, it may return on its own with minimal effort.
If the dislocation is complete, one must urgently proceed to reduce it and not delay under any circumstances. The attendant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert one thumb into the origin of the jaw within the mouth, if it is on one side.
If both thumbs enter from both sides of the jaw, and all other fingers are placed externally to adjust it, he orders the patient to relax his jaw and release it so that it goes in every direction. The doctor adjusts the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If the jaw is difficult to move, especially if both jaws are involved, use a hot water compress with fat until they become easier to move. Do not delay their movement as we have said. If they return and align, and the patient's mouth closes without relaxing, then place strips of cloth, made from wax and fat, on them.
Then, bind them gently with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on their back with their head raised on two pillows to prevent any movement to the right or left and avoid chewing. Keep their food soft.
Until the pain goes away and the jaw heals, he may eat as he wishes but should do so gently, without straining to open his mouth while eating, drinking, or yawning, until the jaw recovers fully.
If the jaw does not return to its place when it becomes unstuck and fails to heal, it often leads to fever, constant headaches, and sometimes abdominal pain and vomiting. If these symptoms occur, know that the condition is severe.
And often, he dies within ten days of being exposed to it.
Chapter Twenty-Fifth.
In the reduction of the collarbone and the acromion.
As for the collarbone, it does not separate from the inside due to its connection to the chest, but it can separate outwardly, which is evident to the touch. It must be reduced by having the patient lie on their back and extend their arms.
