Then, you press the area with the palm of your hand firmly, and it will return. Next, apply a bandage and splints and tighten them. The end of the collarbone that connects to the shoulder does not dislocate except rarely. If it does dislocate, it should be reduced and aligned as we have mentioned and as is suitable for you.
Then, apply a bandage and splints and tighten them. You must order the patient to remain still and at rest until they recover. With this same treatment, the end of the shoulder will also be reduced if it becomes dislocated again.
Chapter Twenty-Sixth.
In Treating Dislocations of the Shoulder Joint.
Know that the shoulder joint dislocates in three ways: towards the armpit downward, towards the chest, and in rare cases, upwards over the shoulder.
It does not dislocate backward to the position of the shoulder or forward to the position of the nerve; most commonly, it dislocates downward towards the armpit, especially in those with little flesh, as it exits and re-enters quickly.
On the other hand, for those with abundant flesh, it is quite the opposite; it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty. Sometimes, a person might suffer a blow or fall, causing the shoulder joint to swell with hot inflammation, leading one to believe that it has dislocated; therefore, it should be examined to confirm its condition.
Then, you should proceed with its treatment. You can distinguish the dislocated joint by comparing the affected shoulder to the healthy one; you will notice a clear difference, and upon touching, the head of the shoulder bone feels like a chicken egg beneath the armpit.
The patient cannot raise their hand to their ear or move it normally; likewise, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, this will be clearly visible to anyone examining it.
And if the jaw is soft or the patient is a child, it can be returned to its place by having a servant raise his hand above, then you place your thumbs under the armpit and lift the joint forcefully upward to its position, and the servant raises his hand and extends it upward.
Then, if it is lowered gently, it will return quickly. If it does not return as mentioned and the jaw has been affected for a long time, the patient should take a hot bath and use relaxants that soften the joints, such as cooking the roots of fennel, dill, and rosemary in water.
Then the patient should lie on his back, and place a woolen ball under his armpit, moderate between hardness and softness. The physician then places their weight on the ball and holds the patient's hand, extending it downwards.
And by pushing the ball with his foot, it will return to its original position. If you prefer, you can do this by bringing a taller man who stands on one side of the patient and places his elbow under the patient's armpit, raising the armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. Another servant pulls the patient's hand towards his lower abdomen. If the patient is light in build, a weight should be tied to them to make them heavier, and the jaw will return to its place immediately. It may also be corrected using another method.
It is to focus on the ground a long wooden beam whose head is round like a pestle, neither thick nor thin, and then place it under the armpit of the patient after placing a soft cloth over the head of the beam while the patient stands along the length of the beam.
Then he extends his hand downwards on the other side and also extends his body from the other side with force, so the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to return it using all that has been mentioned, use this treatment: take a beam whose length is about two arms' length. It should be four fingers in width and two fingers in thickness, with a round head to easily enter the armpit as shown.
Then tie a soft cloth over the round head to prevent any harm to the beam, place it under the armpit, extend the whole hand and arm downwards on the beam, and bind the beam to the upper arm, forearm, and wrist.
Then the arm is placed on a support. Place your hand on it and extend it downwards, while leaving the rest of the body hanging from the other side. The joint will enter of its own accord at that time. If it enters in any way possible, place a moderate-sized ball of wool under the armpit.
Then take the prepared bandage made from flour mixed with resin and egg white, apply it to the entire shoulder as it is rolled over, and tightly secure the ball under the armpit. The bandage is then wrapped on top of it and the hand is hung around the neck.
And he should keep the hand immobile for seven days, and the food of the patient should be reduced so that the member gains strength, thus accelerating its healing. Then, after seven or five days, it is trained through movement. If it holds firm and does not relax, it has healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to moisture or another condition, it is necessary to use cauterization with three sapphires, as mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. If you do all this and loosen the bandage after seven days, but the joint does not hold firm.
If you repeat the binding and tightening but it does not hold firm, falls, and cannot be raised back up, then know that the nerve in the shoulder joint has either been cut, stretched, or relaxed. At this point, understand that the joint will never remain stable in its place.
Regarding the jaw that is positioned towards the chest and breasts, and moves backward with a push and stretch of the hands, it should be manipulated through all forms of pulling and treatment until it heals. If, after healing, it shows signs of weakness in the member and movement is delayed, then it should be re-examined.
The patient should use the bath frequently until the weakness in the member subsides and it returns to its original state.
Chapter XXVII.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the shoulder joint.
The shoulder joint dislocates with difficulty and returns with difficulty, moving in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is evident to the eye and palpable to the touch, revealing its shape.
When you compare the dislocated joint with the healthy one, you will notice a distinct indentation, preventing the arm from bending and the shoulder from moving. You must act swiftly to reduce the dislocation before it develops into a severe inflammation.
If it develops into a hot swelling, reducing it becomes difficult, and in some cases, it may never heal completely, especially if the dislocation is backward. This type of dislocation is the most severe and painful, often leading to death.
If it is possible to reduce the dislocation, have the patient extend both hands and arms fully, while the surgeon places one hand above and the other below the joint, using the thumbs or the palm's base to push the joint back into its socket.
For a forward dislocation, try bending the hand once, striking the acromion (the bone at the top of the shoulder blade) with the palm. If this does not reduce the dislocation, apply extreme force by having two assistants extend the arm fully.
The patient also has two helpers so that he does not slip at the moment of tension, then the arm is turned in all directions after it is wrapped with a folded cloth or a wide bandage, and if the doctor begins to treat the joint with his hands, he smooths them with oil to facilitate the movement of the joint.
Then, the joint is pushed firmly until it returns, and after its return, the dressing that includes compression and drying with egg white should be applied tightly and the arm should be suspended from the patient's neck, leaving it for several days.
Then, check it; if the joint stabilizes in its place, release the bandage and leave it. If you see that the joint does not improve as expected, reapply the dressing and bandage and leave it for a few more days until it strengthens, then release it. If any pain occurs after the joint's return and its release.
If there is difficulty in movement, use moisture in a bath and gentle massage and stimulation until it softens, or place a thin sheep's bladder over the joint, then bind it and leave it for a day and night, then remove it and immerse it in a bath; if it sweats, carefully rub the joint.
Then, repeat the application of the bladder once, twice, and three times while immersing it in a bath until it softens; if you prefer, apply wet, diluted cow manure with butter and tighten it; do this several times, as it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight
In the treatment of wrist dislocation.
The wrist joint is often dislocated and its reduction is more difficult than other joints, except that it should be reduced immediately upon dislocation to prevent the formation of a hard lump or swelling.
Its reduction involves placing the patient's wrist on a board, having a helper extend the patient's hand, and the physician applying pressure on the joint to return it, while ensuring that the dislocation has moved back to its proper position in the palm.
The patient should place the palm of their hand on the board during both extension and flexion, if the dislocation is visible on the palm, they should rest their back hand on the board, allowing the physician's hand to access the same joint surface. If it reduces immediately, apply a compressive bandage to manage swelling.
Allow the reduction to heal without further intervention, as it may not be possible to reduce it again after several days. The joint's position will remain corrected and the patient should avoid straining their hand, ensuring they can still grip objects.
If the nerve has been damaged or there is severe trauma, there is little that can be done except to apply heat therapy, which may or may not help. Once the wrist is reduced, apply the compressive dressing and keep it in place for five days.
Then, if the hand becomes rigid and any impurity appears in it, soften it with warm water and massage it several times until it becomes flexible.
Chapter Twenty-Nine
On the Treatment of Dislocated Joints
If a finger is dislocated and turns in any direction, such that it points to the palm or the back of the hand, stretch the finger and push the joint with your thumb until it returns to its place. Then bind the top of the finger and hang it towards the side it turned to.
Leave it for two days, then release it and stretch it until it becomes straight. If it's at night, tie it as described before. Continue to loosen it during the day, move it, and re-tie it at night, doing this for several days until it strengthens.
Similarly, if a finger turns inward towards the palm of the hand, tie it in the same way as described, and follow these steps until it heals. The same applies if it turns in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty
In the treatment of a spinal disc herniation.
When a spinal or cervical disc completely separates or many discs shift from their positions, treatment is not possible as death approaches the patient rapidly.
A sign of this is that the patient's faeces are released involuntarily, they are unable to control them, and sometimes their legs or arms, or one of them, weaken. If just one disc shifts from its position, it often moves completely.
If a disc shifts to any of four directions, it is either called a hunch or a curve. Its treatment involves observation; if the curve occurred during childhood, there is no cure and it will never rectify. However, if it resulted from a fall or a blow, etc., then treatment may be considered.
He has described them [the treatments] in detail, but most of it is not beneficial. I have summarized what is useful from his explanations and descriptions, and depicted the instruments differently than he did.
I say that the hunch occurring at the front in the chest, or on either side, offers no cure and will not rectify itself. However, the one that happens in the back can be treated by positioning the patient face down.
On a flat surface near a wall, lay a damp cloth beneath to prevent injury to the chest. Then place a standing wooden pole embedded in a hole in the ground at one end of the surface near his head, and another wood at the other end of the table near his feet.
And a servant should hold the pole, not fixed in the hole, and another holds the other in this manner, then wrap around the chest of the patient and under his armpits with soft cloth and extend the cloth's end to the pole by his head and tie it there.
Then secure another cloth over his hips and above his knees and at his ankles, and gather all the ties and bind them to the other pole by his feet, then each servant extends the pole with its bindings towards their respective sides without moving the poles from their positions.
Except that they are not fixed as we said, and the physician places his hands on the pole with force until it returns, or he places a board on top of it and leans on the board with his legs until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, take a board that is about three arms long.
Dig a place in the wall that we mentioned should be near the patient, where the end of the board can enter. Then place the middle of the board on the hump and the physician places his legs on the other end, and tighten it firmly until it compresses the pole.
Return it to its place, and if you wish to make it with a spiral twisted by hand, it is to drive two wooden sticks into the ground at the head of the patient on the far end of the bed, each with a length of an arm, and also at his feet on the same end of the bed, two more wooden sticks.
And after this, between each stick, a space of one span should be left, and in each stick a hole made, through which the spiral passes, and all the sticks are firmly fixed into the ground so that they do not move at all; then a round stick is inserted, which is the spiral on which the ligature is wound in the holes of both sticks.
At the end of it, a hole is made in which a stick one span long, twisted, is inserted; and likewise in the other two sticks. Then the ligature previously fastened on the chest of the patient is wound around the spiral at his head, and the ligatures on his legs are wound around the spirals at his feet.
Then, at each spiral, a servant stands who twists his hand with the instrument that winds the spiral, while the doctor adjusts the hump as previously described. This is an illustration of the spiral, bed, and patient: .
Then, after the spine returns to its place and the position is levelled, the dry bandage made from egg white should be applied, followed by a compress, and then a splint placed on top of the bandage, with a width of three fingers or slightly more, and a length that corresponds to the position of the hump.
And upon some correct seeds, then bind with the ribbon that is due, and let the patient use the delicate food until he recovers. If there remains some protrusion in the place at the end of recovery, then it is necessary to use medications that relax and soften, along with the board that we have described for a long time.
And in that, a lead plate may be used, and there may be a protrusion in the last seeds of the spine, leading one to think it is dislocated, and this could be an extra bone that has grown, so do not subject it to this treatment, as it might cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the elbow joint only suffer dislocation, not the decay or deformation that occurs in other joints. The hip joint can be dislocated in four ways.
This is because the hip joint can dislocate towards the inside, outside, front, or back, but most often it dislocates towards the inside, and rarely towards the front or back. The sign of dislocation towards the inside is that if you compare the healthy leg to the affected leg, the latter appears shorter.
The knee is bent more than it should be, and the patient cannot flex their leg at the thigh. The area following the thigh is clearly swollen, indicating that the top of the femur has moved there.
And the sign that the jaw deviates outward presents symptoms opposite to these symptoms; and when the jaw deviates forward, it straightens the leg completely, except that he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he tries to walk, he is unable to do so forward.
He experiences difficulty urinating, his thigh is stiff, and when walking, he bears weight on the heel. The sign of a jaw deviation to the rear is that he cannot straighten the knee or bend it without first bending the thigh, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
The thigh is relaxed, and the head of the femur is visible in the hip socket. As for the correction of this type of jaw, if it is old and has been associated with its owner and has not attempted to return to its normal position, it will not be treated at all.
You should not attempt to treat it, except for a recent jaw fracture from any of the four types of fractures; in that case, immediately manipulate the joint, flex and extend it, and move it right and left, as it may heal.
Return and do not require other treatment, if it does not return in this way, then you should consider a strong helper who extends his leg from below with his hands or a cloth tied above the knee on his leg, and another assistant extends him from above.
Then let him insert his hands from under his armpits, tighten a soft cloth at the base of the thigh, and another assistant, a third, holds the end of the cloth, extending it either from the front, from the groin to the shoulder blade, or from behind, towards the back.
They should all be extended at once until the patient raises his body from the ground and remains suspended, for this type of extension is common to all four. If the jaw returns as we have said, otherwise each type requires the specific treatment I have mentioned.
As for its specific return, if the jaw is pushed in, the patient should lie on his right side, then a cloth should be wrapped around the base of the thigh between the head of the femur and the point below the groin.
Then the bandage is extended upwards from the groin to the upper body, towards the shoulder blade, and another strong assistant with his arms wraps around the thick part of the thigh of the patient and extends it outward with a powerful pull, causing it to return to its place.
And this type is easier than all other types of treatment that aim to restore this member, if it is impossible for you or it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then you should bind both legs of the patient with a strong and flexible bandage on the ankles and knees.
And each one should be at a distance from the other equal to four fingers, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by two fingers, then the patient is hung on a wooden beam in the house at a distance from the ground of two arms' length.
Then you command a strong boy to embrace the thigh and another boy attaches himself to the patient, while another boy pushes the one embracing the thigh with force; then the joint will return to its place quickly. If, however, the jaw is pushed outward, its return to its proper position will be slower.
The patient should lie on a couch as described for the hunchback, with the affected leg particularly bound, and his chest bound as well. Then place one wooden beam at his feet and another at his head, and add an additional beam in the middle of the couch, secured very tightly.
A damp cloth should be wrapped around it to prevent injury, with the beam between his thighs to prevent him from sinking downward during the tidal pull. Then each assistant extends towards their side, while the doctor adjusts the hip with his hands; if it returns to its place, then it has responded successfully.
And if it is necessary to dislocate the bone, place a board on it and press as we have mentioned for the curvature, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side. If the dislocation is towards the front, stretch the patient's leg once while he remains in this position.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the diseased thigh and presses with his left hand, extending the pressure downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is towards the rear, the patient should not extend their leg downwards.
It should be elevated above the ground, placed on a solid surface, and the patient's hip joint should be dislocated outward, as we have mentioned regarding his position on the board with the ligaments tightened.
As we have mentioned above, the board should be used for compression on the area where the joint has dislocated. Once the hip joint returns to its proper position, as it should, and its return is evident, the patient will extend their leg.
If you see both leg and foot in their natural position, the patient can contract and extend their leg without difficulty, then know that the joint has returned to its proper place. At this point, align the thighs and carry out the bandaging, securing it tightly with a wrap that does not allow the hip to move in any direction.
The patient must rest for three or four days, then the bandage and dressing are removed, and the leg is measured against the other. If they appear equal in length, know that the fracture has healed, so release the patient to walk; if there is still some relaxation observed.
Then return and dress it and bind it as you did initially, leaving it for three more days, then release it and let him walk slowly on it for some days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter II and III.
In the treatment of the knee joint.
The knee joint dislocates in three ways: towards the inside, outside, and downwards (i.e., behind), but never forward. The indication of its dislocation is to order the patient to bring his leg towards his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. To reduce it, sit the patient down with his legs extended if he can bear that, and have a servant sit behind him, gripping his midsection and gently leaning him back slightly, then sit on his thighs.
Press your back against his face, position his leg between your legs, then place both hands on his knee, interlocking your fingers over it. Then, with firm pressure from your hands, and help from another assistant extending his leg, the knee should return to its proper position.
Sign of its healing is when the leg adheres to the thigh in a soft, non-forced manner, then bandage it and attach the leg to the thigh, then bind them all with a bandage for three or four days, and then release them, and use only a little walking for several days until it strengthens.
If it is not possible for it to heal as described, then use the strong tension with bandages that we have described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In the treatment of the ankle joint.
The ankle may heal slightly or completely detach, and its dislocation is either towards the inside or outside. The sign of its dislocation is that you can see the ankle swelling and protruding to the side it has moved towards.
As for the treatment of its detachment, it is easy to reduce it by gently pulling with the hands until it returns. However, if it detaches completely, the patient should sit down and a strong servant should hold them from behind, in the middle of their back, to help them heal.
Then, hold the patient's foot with your right hand at the top and your left hand at the bottom, near the ankle. Pull the foot towards you with your right hand twice, then extend it with your left hand, gently pushing the foot's front towards the thigh without force.
Make these two movements as described, then in the third movement, push the foot forward onto the leg while pulling on the ankle. If it returns once or twice in this way and you observe the foot to be level, otherwise repeat the procedure.
If, after making the movements described, the foot does not return to its place as expected, place the patient face down on the ground and insert a peg firmly into the earth between his legs, wrapping cloth around it to prevent injury to the patient. Then, have an assistant hold the patient's thigh.
Then, another assistant extends the patient's leg with his hands or a strap tied around the patient's neck, extending it against the direction of the first leg and the peg holding the legs apart. The surgeon then adjusts the jaw with his hands.
Another assistant holds the healthy leg down, and if the jaw returns to its place quickly, take up the bandage and splint, binding them tightly, and secure the foot with a strap towards the sole, being careful of the nerve above the heel from behind.
To prevent the strap from being too tight and causing injury, leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it again, and then release it on the fourth day or after 40 days, after which the patient is prevented from walking.
If one attempts to walk before this period, there is a risk that the jaw will dislocate and become damaged, and subsequent treatment will not be effective. If a hot lump appears, one should use the remedies we have described in various sections of treatment and immobilization until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
In the treatment of a man's finger dislocation.
One should adjust the displaced bones using a slight stretch, without force, which is easy to do. If the dislocation is in one of the toes, the patient should place their foot on a level surface.
Or, while the patient stands on a board, you should place your foot on the displaced joint and then press down firmly with your other foot until it aligns and appears level.
Then, place a board under the sole of the patient's foot, covering the entire foot with two ends, and secure it tightly for three days. Release it and protect it from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are confident that it will not reoccur, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
In Types of Mandible with Wound or Fracture or Both.
When such things occur, whether it be a wound or fracture or both, the treatment and reduction of the mandible is often followed by death.
Therefore, one should not undertake such treatment unless they are skilled in the craft, experienced, delicate, compassionate, patient, and cautious, and start with only cooling drugs to alleviate the heat of the wound, leaving the patient in care.
For Allah's sake, unless you pray for safety from harm with a light illness and see some hope of recovery, do not delay in returning it to its place at the first sign of heat inflammation.
If the organ returns as you intended, use measures to alleviate heat inflammation and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments. If the mandible is fractured and there are protruding bone fragments, they should be removed by forceps. Follow this with the same treatment as mentioned for simple diseases in their respective sections.
Exert your utmost effort and purify yourself from venturing into uncertain paths, as I have advised you; this will preserve your honor and protect your dignity, if Allah wills.
